Literary Notes.
Over the Coals .
[This paper demurs,to some of the data, as well as
some of the conclusions, which follow, But it has lec-
tured contributors so often that it rather enjoys being
lectured by a contributor.]
The short story is the reduction of litera-
ture to the paragraph. It is something seen
in a flash and, however brilliant it may be,
a more continuous light is more impres-
sive. The short story has little grip ; and
few, even the best, will bear reading twice.
Try this experiment : Read Kipling—then read
Zola, a play ot (Shakespeare, or anything con- (
tinuous, and then go back to Kipling. You
feel that the latter starves the imagination
instead of feeding it. The short story is written
ho that no one need think or exercise his imagina-
tion. The man in the streets reads so many
papers and so quickly that he has no time to think
or imagine, and the short story is made for him.
When he tries something with depth in it he is
perplexed because the author demands thought,
and he votes the author a bore. One of The Bul-
letin’s most brilliant contributors confessed to
me that he could not read anything beyond a
column. Years ago, when I was at school with
him, he could read one book all day. At this rate
he will shortly be unable to fix his attention on
anything over a paragraph. And this I think is
the state of mind of the journalist, and the raison
d’etre of the short story. It does not justify such a
state of things to say that modern life is so hurried
that a man has no time for lengthened reading.
For the hurry is for Mammon, and to admit this
as a justification is merely to assert that literature
is to be pushed to the wall by greed.
*****
Again, Poe’s reason for the short poem must be
taken variously. It depends on the individual
how long an emotion will last. The fallacy in
Poe is this : to sustain a strong emotion for a long
time may be a physiological impossibility—that is,
one emotion —but a varied series of emotions may
be continued for a long time. I prefer to read a
play of Shakespeare at one sitting. Say it’s
“Othello”: I get a long series of emotions care-
fully varied and led to a climax without any sense
of fatigue. I read “Germinal ”in one stretch of
16 hours, and always read thus without a break
when I can, and find what is thus read makes a
deeper impression than would a whole year’s read-
ing of short stories. I quote myself because I
find numbers of people who read in the same way
—following Mrs. Browning’s hint to go “soul
forward into books profound,” and I cannot find
anything to envy in the man who can’t read any-
thing over a column.
* * * •
Coming down to Bulletin matters : it appears
to me that this condensation is pushed farther in
The Bulletin than anywhere else. There is
more decoration, scenery, in even Maupassant,
Coppee, Quiller Couch and other short-story
writers than in Bulletin work. Consequently
there is a loss in the latter. Thus, in Mont-
gomery’s “ Four Fools ” no one can tell from the
story itself where it takes place. One begins it
in the idea that the Netherlands have something
to do with it; then, half-way through, a bamboo-
bridge is mentioned, so one conjectures the East;
and then an obscure name is mentioned, which I
have not been able to trace in an atlas. It is my
previous knowledge of Montgomery that tells me
his scene is somewhere about Borneo. The same
withßecke —I find myself perpetually requiring the
help of previous knowledge of the South Seas to
construct the scenery. He knows the Islands so
well that he leaves out much that would be a help
to us who do not know them. I say this of
him and Montgomery while freely admiring the
character, incident, and dramatic power of both.
The same applies to Bulletin bush stories—one has
to be something of a bushman to appreciate them.
Some day a man with ideas will arise who’ll absorb
all The Bulletin’s bush work and turn it into
literature that the world—not bushmen only—will
read. He -will not know the bush anything like
as well as The Bulletin’s back-block contribu-
tors, but he will have what they lack—style,
passion, pathos, and big ideas—and he will simply
steal, as Shakespeare did, all their work and
transform it. The Bulletin coteries will cry
“Plagiarist!” but the world that knows nothing
of the back-block bards won’t listen, and will hail
him as Australia’s genius.
* * * »
Another point. The Bulletin makes strong
demands for originality. Sometime ago it said
no one was great through imitation, which I deny.
Every artist and literary man in the first rank
began by imitating some favorite. Shakespeare’s
earliest work is imitative —something else as well
—but imitative. Children learn by imitation ; in-
dividuality developes, and does not arise from a
preconceived desire for originality. If some
appear to have always been original, it simply
means that they have suppressed their earlier
imitative'work —as Wagner did. If a man is true
to himself he will attain all the originality he is
capable of. Seeking it, he merely lands in eccen-
tricity and gets farther and farther from the com-
mon ground of humanity, where all Art has its
power. There is nothing more pitiful than the
desire of half - read, small - brained creatures
of originality. They seek some sensational
effect in some accident; they get a small smatter-
ing of cheap science and work it in, and call that
original. The result is melodrama—without the
good there is in stage-melodrama. The Bulletin
short story works up to “He is dead ” or “He
is dead-drunk” —this being a generalisation of the
two favorite climaxes—one some novel accident,
the other a “ sell.” By “ accident,” I mean some
external cause acting on the characters, who are
at the mercy of it, and this is characteristic of
melodrama. Incidents —the more sensational and
“original ” the better—fall upon the characters ;
while all true literature works the other way—the
characters command the circumstances.
* * * *
One other matter verse contributions. I
have seen it printed that Swinburne is the
favorite poet of The Bulletin. Whether this be
so or not, the trail of Swinburne is over all The
Bulletin verse. I admire Swinburne, but find
him the least helpful of poets—helpful as regards
writing or living. He is never dramatic—he
o«kn’t be —never soul-searching. I find more
passion in ten lines of Browning and bits of Ten-
nyson than in all the “Laus Veneris ” and others.
He wails over a dead love ; Browning gives you a
real woman. And his wail is that of a burnt-out
voluptuary. Browning gives you torrid passion in
a healthy man. I contrast the two because I fancy
The Bulletin cares little for Browning. Most
journalists I know worship Swinburne—l think
because Swinburne requires no introspection,
while Browning does. The journalist has no
time • his faculty of collecting humorous pars,
would be lost if he introspected. My point is :
that it would give far more of an impulse to Aus.
writers to read Browning than to read Swinburne.
It is not for nothing that Kipling’s earlier work is
woven in and out with Browning. Gontrast
Browning’s roughness and Swinburne’s melody if
you like, but melody is an adjunct— though a
necessary one in verse. It is music one goes to for
melody, and no poet ever understood the aim and
end of music so much as Browning. A man whose
pleasure from verse mainly arises from its music
is merely gratifying a rudimentary taste, which,
if fully developed, would seek satisfaction from
another art. I have a notion, too mainly de-
rived from pars, published—that The Bulletin
cares least for music—that is, symphonic music
of all the arts. If so, it fits in with the slurring
of Browning and the psychological because all
music is soul-searching. The greatest musicians
- the Germans —are the greatest metaphysicians ;
and the merely practical nations like the English,
are lowest in music, philosophy, tragic drama,
tragic acting, the roots of which are all found
within, not without. .
* * * *
In conclusion, it appears to me that three
ladies Ethel Turner, Lilian Turner, Louise
Mack —have brought The Bulletin the best
literature that it has published. They give charac-
ters, and dig their stories out of hearts, besides
employing the charm of literary finish. J.H.G.
“ Casa Braccio.”
Marion Crawford belongs to that delightful
class of novelists whom, thank Literature! it is
not necessary to read as Trilby posed “ for the
altogether.” In the press of authors who must
be skimmed if one is to keep within hailing dis-
tance of the spirit of the publishers, gratitude to
the author who can be skipped is almost a religion.
“Casa Braccio” (Macmillan) is Crawford’s
twenty-fifth story, and—so several English critics
say—his best. So that here again is matter for
thanks. The artist who “ revolves in a cycle of
masterpieces, of which each is more perfect than
the last,” allows us to separate his cream on very
easy terms.
Distinctly second-rate, Crawford is still a plea-
sant companion. If he cannot excite, he can
generally interest, and even when he does not
interest he does not bore. Many faint little
flashes of man-o’-the-world philosophy light his
books: occasionally he crystallises tersely and
aptly some trut i of observation. And he con-
structs admirably ; his studies of character (see
his Italian peasants) are keen; and his fluent
stvle brightens now and then into something
approaching eloquence of phrase.
The first part of “ Casa Braccio ” tells the loves
of an Italian nun and a Scotch doctor—tempta-
tion, fall, flight. To good Catholics this will
seem sacrilegious, and good Protestant- will
resent just as much the nemesis which (in Parts
11. and III.) follows the sacrilege. Sister Maria
Addolorata soon dies ; Align- Dalrymple nourishes
a lifelong grief. Their daughter embitters one
man’s life, ruins another’s, and suicides. Her son
is left alive—to dree his hereditary weird in
another novel, after the fashion of Zola? It is a
pity, or a pleasure (according to the point of view)
that the Sin is much more agreeably and con-
vincingly described than is the Punishment.
Charles Kingsley*
“Yeast” and “Water Babies” are two new
volumes in Macmillan’s pocket Kingsley series.
“Yeast ” has more rational interest than most of
the hateful, class of novels with a purpose. One
can skip the preaching, and the remainder isn’t
bad reading. There is a delicious bit of bathos
when Lancelot pops to Argemone. The lady’s
eyes shone out like t wain lakes of still azure, beneath a
broad marble cliff of polished forehead.
And this allusion isn’t bad for a clergyman of
“ the Established Church ” :
“ Quieo sabe,” said the Spanish girl, when they asked
her who was her child’s father.
“Water Babies” is pleasant enough, but too
long The satire is sometimes good, more fre-
quently clumsy. The interspersed verses touch on
cleverness. This bit of manly sentiment is well-
known, but should be known better :
When all the world is young, lad,
And all the trees are green ;
And every goose a swan, lad,
And every lass a queen ;
Then hey for boot and horse, lad,
And round the world away ;
Young blood must have its course, lad,
And every dog his day.
When all the world is old, lad,
And all the trees are brown ;
And all the sport is stale, lad,
And all the wheels run down ;
Creep home, and take your place there,
The spent and maimed among ;
God grant you find one face there,
You loved when all was young.
Lawson’s new book of verses has received a
cordial welcome from press critics, and its per-
manence in Australian literature is assured.
Copies will be sent from this office for ss. sd. in
stamps or postal notes of any province.
The new Poet Laureate commemorates the
defeat of Jameson’s freebooters in verse which
befits himself, his office, and his subject. A
sample :
Right sweet is the marksman’s rattle,
And sweeter the cannon’s roar;
But ’tis bitterly bad to battle,
Beleaguered, and one to four.
I can tell you it wasn’t a trifle
To swarm over Krugersdorp Glen,
As they plied us with round and rifle,
And ploughed us again and again.
Pass the sentiment, though all the right, all the
honor, are on the side of the Boers ; and consider
the style ! Imagine Swinburne’s sardonic grin !
Conceive Kipling’s disgust at the mishandling of
a metre with which he fires the blood ! Nothing
lamer, flatter, more insipid, more disastrously com-
monplace was ever penned by alleged bard. It is
to be hoped Alfred Austin will be spared to write
reams of similar stuff, in order that the notion of
“ honor ” may be for ever detached from the office
of Court-sycophant. Evidently, he is anxious to
show he can earn his sherry, even if he does not
get it. Maginn, in a brilliant set of Latin-English
verses, refers to the uses first found for the
Laureate’s wine on the Laureate s island :
Fair Sherry, Port’s sister, for years they dismissid her
To comfort the kitchen-maids’ bellies.
Literary associations considered, revocation of the
dismissal was an absurd error.

This is the miscellaneous mob which enthusiastic

George Collingridge's “Discovery of Australia
gets from London Saturday Review a two-col.
notice which does justice to the author’s industry
and research, and does not grumble too much at
his literary shortcomings. “Whilst, therefore,
others in the same field may have made their work
more attractive so far as it went, none has pro-
duced a volume likely to prove more useful in
determining the evolution of Australian geo-
graphy.”
Sydney Telegraph's L<m ’on correspondent
says that Macmillan’s Col. Library is to be en-
i riched by Dent’s new Balzac series, and a com-
: plete Kipling and Hardy. The Balzac is as yet
three-fourths unpublished, so surely the cheaper
edition will restrict the market for it consider-
| ably ? As the best of Hardy is already issued in
Macmillan’s Library, news on this point is a
little “subsequent”; but a cheap Kipling is
badly needed. Six shillings is too much for most
book-buyers' pockets.
To Correspondents R. : “ Trilby ” was the
subject of one of the sheep-public’s periodical
obsessions, but there is nothing in the book to
justify the boom. And, frankly, discussion as to
which of its characters was who, and who
which, doesn’t in the least interest us Zeta :
Carlyle gave light to the darkness of a million
souls, and broke the heart of one woman who
loved him. (N.B.—'This isn’t exactly true, but
epigrams, like hares, aren’t meant to be eaten
quite fresh.) “Et s’il revenait.”—Further
versions received from E.S. ; L.B. ; R.K.D. ;

The Marlyr.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Not only on cross and gibbet,
By sword, and fire, and flood,
Have perished the world’s sad martyrs
Whose names are writ in blood.
A woman lay in a hovel,
Mean, dismal, gasping for breath ;
One friend alone was beside her —
The name of him was—Death.
-For the sake of her orphan childien,
For money to buy them food,
ohe had slaved in the dismal hovel
And wasted her womanhood.
No rest; no pause ; but always—
-1 hrough sick’ning, stifling heat
1 hat, in panting days of summer,
Reeked in the squalid street,
When folk sat out on their doorstep
Praying for faintest stir
Of air—she kept slaving, slaving ;
There was no rest for her.
She worked with limbs sore-aching
And eyes that dare not tire,
In shuddering nights of winter,
With candle alone for fire.
The songs of the rolling seasons,
Tender, and gay, and grave
For others; for her had ever
The one grim burden—“ S’ave !
Winter and spring and summer
Came each with its load of ca es ;
And autumn to her brought only
A harvest of grey hairs.
Far out in the blessed country,
Beyond the smoky town,
The winds of Cod were blowing
Evermore up and down.
The trees were waving signals
Of joy from the bush beyond :
The gum its blue-green banner,
The fern its dark-green frond ;
Flow’r called to flow’r in whispers,
By sweet caressing names,
And young gum-shoots sprang up ward
Like woodland altar-flames ;
And, deep in the distant ranges^
The magpie’s fluting song
Roused musical, mocking echoes
In the woods of Dandenong;
And riders were galloping gaily
With loose-held flowing reins,
Through dim and shadowy gullies,
Across broad, treeless plains ;
And winds through the Heads came wafting
Breath of life from the sea,
And over the blue horizon
The ships sailed silently ;
And out of the sea at morning
The sun rose, golden-bright,
And in crimson and gold and purp'u
Sank in the sea at night;
But in dreams alone she saw them,
Her hours of toil between ;
Like galley-slave to his oar she
Was chained to her sewing-machine—
Save only when back and forward,
With tired and throbbing feet,
Bearing her work to the Sweater,
She trudged through the crowded street.
Loiter ? She dared not loiter,
Howe’er her heart might crave,
But passed, like a ghost at cockcrow
Hasting back to its grave.
Her heart was in the grave, too,
That held her children three,
Nor work, nor prayer could save them,
Nor tears of agony.
On the lips of her last and dearest
Pressing a farewell kiss,
She cried aloud in her anguish—-
“ Can God make amends for this
Dull, desperate, ceaseless, slaving
Bereft her of pow’er to pray,
And Man was careless and cruel,
And God was far away.
But who shall measure His mercies 7
His ways are in the deep ;
And, after a life of sorrow,
He gave her His gift of sleep.
Rest comes at last to the weary,
And freedom to the slave ;
Her tired and worn-out body
Sleeps well in its pauper grave.
But His angel bore her soul up
To that Bright Land and Fair,
Where Sorrow enters never,
Nor any cloud of Care.
They came to a lovely valley,
Agleam with asphodel,
And the soul of the woman speaking
Said —“ Here I fain would dwell !”
The Angel answered gently :
“ O soul most pure and dear !
O Soul most tried and truest! —
Thy dwelling is not here !
“ Behold thy place appointed—-
Long kept, long waiting—come !
Where bloom on the hills of heaven
The roses of martyrdom.”
VICTOR J. DALEY.

the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
( Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 29, 1896.
State Fire Insurance.
The proposal of the Maoriland Government
to create a State Fire Insurance Depart-
ment as the natural complement of the
marvellously solid and progressive State Life
Insurance Department already existing is a
remarkable forward step in the direction of
State Socialism. Also, viewed in a cold,
business aspect, with no reference to State
Socialism or any other abstract theory what-
ever, but as a mere matter of cash and con-
venience, there is a great deal to be said in
its favor. All the stereotyped objections,
and the old, threadbare Tory scoffs about
* ‘ State interference with private enter-
prise,” “coddling,” “ grandmotherly legisla-
tion,” and the other catchwords which the
stagnant party uses over and over again
because it is incapable of inventing anything
new, may, in the first place, he taken as
read. They prove nothing except the ex-
ceeding barrenness of the Tory intellect.
They were all employed by previous Tories
when State railways were first proposed, when
railways of any kind were first proposed,
when it was first proposed that the State
should own the telegraph system, and when
the State took over the post-office from
private speculators. Also, they were doubt-
less used when the State proposed to collect
the taxes direct instead of farming them out
to “ private enterprise,” when it began to
run the army and navy “ on its own” instead
of leaving them in private hands under the
primitive feudal system —in short, every
time the State attempted to do anything
which, it hadn’t done before. In very early
days the whole embodiment of the State was
an undressed autocrat, carrying a club with
some human hair and dried, brains on the
end of it to show where he had put down a
recent insurrection, and every time the State
has made or attempted any advance on that
primitive model, sound old Conservatives
have arisen to point out that it was going in
for wild and reckless experiments. There-
fore, this particular objection may be taken
for granted.
* * • *
Maoriland, like Australia, is being over-
run by British and other extraneous fire
insurance offices which grind profits out of
the province with which to pay dividends to
British and other foreign shareholders.
About a dozen years ago there were seven
Maoriland offices doing business in the
country ; they are now reduced to four. One
dropped out through gross and infamous
mismanagement. One, at least, the Union,
was bought up by a foreign competitor ; and
the fate of the third this paper doesn’t at
present remember. Probably the two
smaller of the native survivors, the Standard
and National, will go the same way as the
Union by-and-bye, the baleful eye of the
British purchaser being already fixed on at
least one of them. ' Against these four local
offices there are now ten foreign companies
doing business in Maoriland. For a good
many years past the big English companies,
some of which were large and wealthy before
Australasia was first settled by the white
man, have been steadily buying up or crowd-
ing out the native concerns. Within ten
years fourteen local offices have disappeared
out of Australasia, most of them being swal-
lowed by English competitors. In these
same ten years' the premium income of
the local offices throughout the seven
provinces has declined from £1,800,000
to a little over £1,000,000, and this des-
pite the fact that population has increased
in that period about 25 per cent. All the
new business, and all the old business which
the local offices have lost, has gone to the
ever-increasing foreign competitors, who are
now much more numerous than the local
concerns, and still keep increasing and multi-
plying.
* * * *
The results of this state of things are
various, and all more or less disastrous. In
the first place, the-seven provinces of Aus-
tralasia pay some £200,000 or £250,000 a
year in profits to foreign companies, which
tribute leaves the country to pay dividends
to shareholders in distant parts, and never
by any chance comes back. It gets nothing
whatever in return for this sacrifice. Its in-
surance business would be done quite as well
and as cheaply by local companies, but, in
their case, the dividends would go to local
shareholders, and be expended by them
among local tradespeople, and the country
would be £200,000 or £250,000 a year better
off. The gradual wreckage of the local
offices means a further reduction in the
number of profitable avenues for investment.
The Australian who has money to invest
can’t put it to any great extent into State
securities, for most of them are in Lon-
don. The supply of good bank stocks is
limited, for a vast quantity of them are in
London. Municipal stocks are scarce, for
the best of them are also in Bull-land and
are not locally obtainable. Insurance stocks
are very few, for three-fourths of the local
insurance business is represented by shares
held in London. The same monotonous
remark applies to some of the best mining
stocks, many of the shares and almost all the
debentures of the big pastoral investment
enterprises, and most other things. There-
fore, Australian banks and life-assurance
societies have to bury most of their money
in long mortgages, where it isn’t realisable
when wanted. Further, the fierce compe-
tition of the foreign fire-insurance companies
is disastrous to the insurers. Maoriland
alone now supports fourteen separate sets of
managers, directors, offices, office staffs and
agents when four would be more than suffi-
cient,, and the cost of all these has to be
charged to the insurer. When fourteen
separate agents and all the rest of the
machinery are running after a £lOO risk on
the furniture of a six-roomed wooden cot-
tage, the premium paid on that £lOO has to
cover indirectly the expenses of the thirteen
men who didn’t get the business as well
as those of the one who did. As a
consequence, the expense of doing about
£1,000,000 worth of insurance now is
very little less than that of doing £1,800,000
worth 10 years ago ; the profits are less ; the
dividends are smaller, therefore the share-
holders have less money to spend, and things
generally are unsatisfactory.
* * * *
Two visible ways to stop this particular
leakage are either to tax the foreign com-
panies out of the country and thus diminish
competition, or to throw them out bodily by
Act of Parliament for the same purpose. The.
third alternative is to create a State Fire Insur-
ance Department which may be able to com-
pete with the enemy to better advantage than
local private enterprise can ever hope to do.
And, though tire -business is a decidedly
risky one, the State has so many advan-
tages that the bold experiment proposed
by the Seddon Ministry is well worth
trying. In the first place, the State can
offer better security than the biggest private
co. on earth can attain to, and considering
how many private companies have crumbled
away at different times, in England as well as
elsewhere, leaving their Creditors hopelessly
in the lurch, this in itself amounts to a great
deal. It has got all its machinery ready to hand
without any preliminary expense worth men-
tioning. Postmasters, officials of the State
Life Assurance Department, land-agents, and
other Government employes can be appointed
its representatives. Their office accommo-
dation is already provided ; they can afford
to work at lower rates than private agents in
consideration of the fact that they are already
in receipt of fixed salaries ; they can be
audited without any extra expense by the
State auditors who overhaul their other
accounts and who travel from place to place
cheaply on the long-suffering free-railway-
pass system ; and they are spread over the
length and breadth of the land in every town
and village and hamlet, so that the State
Fire Department would be, from the very
beginning, the most widely-represented affair
in Maoriland. The insurance of State pro-
perty would give the new department a large
and profitable connection to begin with, and
it would probably also command from the
beginning all, or nearly all, the business of
municipal bodies. And in cases of arson and
attempted fraud the department would have
the police and detective forces and the
machinery of the Crown Law offices at its
direct command, so that it would not be likely
to suffer any more in this direction than do
private offices —perhaps not so much. More-
over, it would operate only in its own
country, where it would command the best
business that was going, whereas many of
the private offices extend their operations
into far-distant lands where, in most cases,
they ,get only the dregs. The Australasian
citizen has a holy reverence for British enter-
prise, which in these provinces secures much
of the best business. But Britain and other
foreign countries by no means reciprocate
this feeling, and the Australasian companies
which trade abroad get, as a rule, only risks
of a very inferior character.
* * * *
The State Department would be able to
offer better terms than its private rivals
because, in the first place, its expenses
would be less. The experience of the
Maoriland Life Insurance Department has
already shown that the State can do this
branch of business much more cheaply than
any private concern of its size and age,
and with the same machinery it should be
able to make a similar saving on fire business.
Probably the increased economy effected
by working the two Departments together
would make the saving still greater. The
State would make a further saving through
having no shareholders to claim dividends.
It would invest its reserves mostly in State
securities, and thus do a little more to
make Maoriland independent of the British
money-lender, and every such gain, how-
ever limited, is a movement in the right
direction. It would stop a part, at all events,
of the tribute which goes out of the country
to pay dividends to English shareholders,
and everything which is done in that direc-
tion is so much clear gain. In every possible
way it is a move towards making Maoriland
independent of the foreign capitalist and the
foreign money-grabber. And, though the
Tory publications which whoop vaguely
about the necessity of taking “ large views ”
and “broad, statesmanlike measures” and
other shadowy things which are never clearly
defined, may scoff at it as an attempt to stop
a comparatively small leakage, still the
leakage is steadily growing larger, and even
the smallest kind of leakage is worth stop-
ping, or partially stopping, in a country
whose wealth drains away England-wards
through so many holes, large and small.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Lost Territory.
At latest advices the scheme for handing over the
Northern Territory of S.A. to England, or to a
syndicate, or a chartered co., to be run on
Chinese and nigger-labor principles, is still
alive. It is not at present leading a very
healthy existence, it is true. Joe Chamber-
lain, British Secretary of State for the Colonies
—the renegade with the orchid in his button-
hole —has declined to move in the matter, and
the only hope of the colored-labor party now is
that S.A. will do the fatal act on its own
account. There is no earthly prospect of such
a thing at present, but some day or other a Fat
Man reaction may set in, aud a Ministry of
syndicators and company-promoters may lay
the foundations of a racial war and a nigger-
question in the Territory. Considering these
possibilities, it would be interesting to ascer-
tain, even at this late hour, to whom the out-
cast province really belongs, and who has a
definite right to job it away to any foreign
speculator. Its history is confused and remark-
ably obscure. In the first beginnings of Austra-
lia’s written history N.S. Wales was understood,
in a vague, shadowy fashion, to cover all the
continent from the borders of what is now
Westralia to the east coast, and Tasmania and
Maoriland as well. By degrees, however, this un-
wieldy province dropped to pieces through the
ends being too far away from the central sup-
port. South Australia, Victoria, Tasmania
and Maoriland were one by one established as
separate provinces, and when N.S. Wales
became a completely self-governing province it
consisted only of what is now N.S.W., Queens-
land, and the Northern Territory. About this
period the confused history of the Territory
really begins. The separation of Queensland
cut a large piece out of the centre of the province
and divided it into two widely separated pieces
——N.S. Wales as it now is and the aforesaid
Territory. There was no land communication
between them. Communication by sea meant
a voyage half-way round Australia to
arrive at a place which, in these days,
didn’t seem worth arriving at. And so it
was generally agreed that the separated
half of N.S.W T . was not worth keeping at the
price, and when it fell over the tail-board of
the cart one day no one stopped to pick it up.
The Imperial Government, finding it lying
about loose, lent it to South Australia, and that
province has the loan of it to this day, and so
long as its administration of it is not in any
way a danger or a nuisance to its neighbors it
will probably keep it unquestioned. But if
S.A. ever proposes to sell or lease the Territory
to a foreign syndicate which will have power
to flood the North with cheap alien labor and
alien leprosy, some one may be induced to raise
a question as to the real ownership of that
forlorn dependency. It is alleged, on what
seems to be excellent authority, that there is
no evidence of tiny legal cession of the lerritory
by N.S. W., or any legal resumption of it by
the Imperial Government. It doesn’t seem to
have been severed from the original province
by any recognised legal process as were \ ictoria
and Queensland ; as far as can be ascertained,
it was merely a kind of neglected infant which
got left behind in a careless, accidental fashion,
and was never claimed again. And whether,
according to the written law of the case, the
Territory and its deficiency and its insolvency
and all its other troubles don’t really belong to
N.S.W. to this day seems to be an open
question—at least The Bulletin has failed to
trace any absolutely certain evidence to the
contrary.
Some Victorian Critics.
Ever since things began to get really bad all
over Australia the windows of heaven have been
opened, and for forty days and forty nights
it has rained good advice, to the effect that the
various Governments should “do something.”
Almost everybody has been, and still is, of
opinion that the Governments should “do some-
thing.” As a recent example, Melbourne
Paunch reports that things in Victoria are in a
truly bad way, and this is to be remedied,
“not by fighting over miserable, pettifogging
Post-office Amendment, Betting Suppression,
Korumburra Subdivision, Women’s Suffrage,
and One-Man-One-Vole Bills, but by some
broad, well-defined schemes of practical legisla-
tion.” Melbourne Argus cordially endorses
this view, only the Argus holds that what is
wanted is a Ministry that will take “ large,
statesmanlike views.” The Argus got this
especial hunk of foolishness off its chest when
the Turner Government proposed to save ,£BOOO
a year of needless expense by doing away with a
wholly useless Melb. railway station, on which
occasion that paper besought- the Cabinet to
cease from worrying over small trifles like this,
and instead to throw itself buoyantly and pro-
miscuously up against the atmosphere of things
and take “large views.” The Argus also asks
it to establish a “ national railway policy,” and
some equally coherent Yarra-bank orators say
that the only thing which will do any good is
for the people to “awake” and “arise.”
Paunch doesn’t even suggest one sample of the
“broad, well-defined schemes” that it wants,
and the Argus doesn’t explain which “ large
view ” the Government should take —they are
merely of opinion that it should “do some-
tiling.” Victoria is a self-governed country ;
the people, by a more or less indirect process,
make the Parliament and the Ministry ; the
Parliament and the Ministry are their servants
paid to carry out their instructions ; and these
instructions apparently are to take “large
views ” and have “well-defined schemes,” and
unfurl their banner generally. The Argus
evidently doesn’t know what its own “ large
views ” are, and Paunch hasn’t the faintest
idea what “broad, well-defined schemes” it
is talking about. These things are only
ragged chunks of eloquence which the two
organs wave round their heads promiscuously.
And for all practical purposes the man who
shows how to remedy one evil, if he only thereby
saves his country £1 per annum, or even 2s. in
a decade, or who makes one banana-skin less
on the pavement, or one smell the less in a
back street, or repairs one old-established rut in
a bush-track, is worth more than all the papers
which call wildly on other people to have
“large views” and “broad, well-defined
schemes of practical legislation ” from now till
the year 3000 or even later.
The Waverley Election.
The Waverley (N.S.W.) election showed no-
thing very clearly, save that there is one more
constituency that doesn’t want Henry Parkes
at any price. Out of 1395 votes the fallen
leviathan scored exactly 160. This should be
a broad enough hint for any man, but even it is
lost on the Grand Old Has-been, who retired
with dignity, announcing his belief that the
country will yet call for him with a loud voice
to come to its rescue. The true state of the
case is that the country has almost forgotten
that Parkes ever existed—it has a short
memory and no cultivated taste for ruins, and,
besides, the old man keeps assuring it that he
doesn’t want “ hoffis,” and it naturally takes
him at his word. Beyond the fact that Waver-
ley didn’t attempt to shove Parkes into the
“ hoffis ” which he is so anxious to avoid, there
was nothing very surprising about the election.
The Foreign-trade vote mustered 698 strong,
against 920 six months ago, and the Protection-
ist vote was 528, against 511 last year. There-
fore, the good cause of Japanese boots is still
ahead, though not nearly so far ahead as it was.
It is only right that it should be ahead ;it
would have been a great pity if anything had
happened to spoil the symmetry of the political
situation just yet. hew South Wales has voted
pretty consistently for Freetrade during 40
years or so, and has never yet got it. It has
lived all these years under a partially-Protec-
tive tariff, which it somehow or other imagined
was Ireetrade, and because it prospered mode-
rately under that tariff it has persistently voted
that I reetrade was a good thing. Now, for the
first time, it has a fair chance of getting what
it has been asking for so persistently, and
it would be a thousand pities if anything hap-
pened to weaken the shoddy majority until the
blessed thing actually arrives. It hasn’t really
come yet, but it is getting here by instalments,
and The Bulletin asks the province to keep on
voting the Calico Jimmy ticket a little longer
till it gets the boon it has so earnestly prayed for.
I he only thing that will cure the N.S.W. Free-
tiade superstition is Freetrade, and the people
who go trying to spoil the show now, just when
it is becoming really interesting for the first
time in 40 years, are an ill-timed, inopportune
crowd, with no sense of humor.
The Westralian Chaos.
The Westralian Government and its officials
lave now plumbed the abyss of their own
rbecility and found it bottondess. Legitimate
*J ust y Province is a stagnant
' lv °ir. It is fed continually by streams of ex-
lirp^eoKus | oa P lfcal l a, nd enterprise, and continually
held back by the huge dam of crusted Tory


prejudice and incapacity which the “ Six
Families ” and their allies so jealously maintain
—grudgingly permitting a little, a very little,
stream to issue from one tape-hinged sluice.
During the past three years, Weslralian mineral
discoveries, or alleged discoveries, have revolu-
tionised pre-existing conditions. The Ministry
and its satellites and servants, able, perhaps,
to muddle the affairs of a municipality, have
been suddenly called upon to direct the
business of a miniature nation, and have
responded by muddling more magnificently
than ever. Rip Van Winkle, wakened from his
sleep in the Catskills, placed on the footplate
of an express locomotive, and told to break the
record to Chicago, could not have cut a more
blinking, ludicrous, stupefied figure than that
of the Westralian Govt, departments, placed,
for the first time in their lives, face to face
with the essentials of progress. Their incom-
petency would be pitiable, were it not so
deadly. The postal and telegraphic ser-
vice is “ like to a chaos, or an unlicked bear-
whelp.” The pile of telegrams in arrear would
freight an Orient liner, and every morning the
sulky officials begin at the bottom of the
accumulation, a fortnight behind, while the
day’s collection is stuck on the top of the file to
wait its turn. The newspapers have been
restricted to so many words ; private
individuals are told “the mail will get there
quicker.” And this is the slow Westralian
mail the slowest on earth ! Meanwhile,
the railway department cannot supply trucks,
and industries are stopped and men thrown out
of employment waiting its convenience ; the
delay in getting a mining lease, a registered
title, is interminable, unless British capital has
stuck to certain palms; everywhere is con-
fusion and mismanagement. The officials
meet objections and complaints with surly
insolence : “ If you don’t like Westralian ways
you had better leave Westralia ” one indignant
Easterner was told. And the heads of the
Govt, are either supine or wish to be ; they
talk, and promise, and never take a broom
within five miles of the Augean stable. There
is only one remedy : the Government must go.
It should have gone last election, but the
diggers were unready and unregistered, and
turned a credulous ear to bribe-laden whispers.
Next time there will be no mistake. The tramp
of the invading army is the knell of Six-Family
domination.
“Socialism in our Time.”
Dear Bulletin, — Re your article on “Socialism
in our Time,” I think there is something to be
Baid on the other side. There are great reasons
for doubting whether this policy of “ support
in return for concessions ” ever did or ever will
benefit Labor. Was the cause of Home Rule
consummated, or even materially advanced, by
the support accorded Mr. Gladstone by the
Home Rule party? The N.S.W. Labor-party
abstained from opposing Reid’s followers on
his fervent declarations that, if returned to
power, he would scotch the Upper House
beyond recognition, and send to that Chamber
such a body of democrats as would make every
respectable hair on the head of an M.L.C. rise in
horror. What is the resultof six months’constant
support ? Why, Reid has pursued a consistent
policy of knuckling under to the Upper House ;
and the band of ardent democrats turned out,
with but one exception, to be “ old timers ”
whom the electors would have none of, and for
whom even Reid felt it necessary to apologise.
This being so, what guarantee has the Q.
Labor-party that Mr. Powers, despite his fair
promises, will act otherwise? And if it supported
him and he tricked it, as men of his class gener-
ally do, what excuse would it have for adhering
to an unwise policy in the face of the same
policy’s failure in New South Wales ? There
is another reason why I think the Q. Labor-
party has acted quite rightly in this matter.
When a party of advanced reform unites with
any capitalist party, it loses its individuality,
its virility, and its independence, which are its
life, the breath of its being; and tends to become
merged with the moderates to its own disadvan-
tage. The only hope for labor in the future is to
retaiu its independence ; as the leader of the
Labor-party said : “ We must recognise only
two parties in politics, Capital and Labor ; the
moment we fraternise with one section of the
party of Capital, be its promises never so
specious, we are lost.” Despite the awful
picture you paint of the probable results of a
continuation of the Boodlewraith’s power, I
think it would not do Labor in politics any
harm. Oppression never did our cause any
harm and never will do. It is more likely to
cause a revulsion of feeling that would increase
the strength of the Labor-party without the
necessity of becoming Powersites. I have but
recently returned from a visit to Queensland,
and, as far as I could gauge it, the feeling of the
Labor section of the public w’as against the
coalition of Labor with any of the old parties
because it was felt no benefit would accrue.
If Mr. Powers were demonstrably sincere in
his promises, the Labor-party would indeed be
fools not to seize the opportunity offered, but
little experience has taught us that these old
politicians are never sincere but wise and tricky
in their day and generation, that support in
return for concessions always means in the end
support without concessions. I believe that
Labor has much more educational work to do
ere it can take the lead in politics, and till
then better to be a virile independent minority
than a number of modified Joe Cooks. — May
Day. _
A Monkey Trick.
An interesting struggle for the baby-soul of a
certain Prince Boris lately happened in Bul-
garia. Baby Boris is, to the best of every-
body’s belief, the son of the local Prince
Ferdinand, by his devout R.C. wife, Princess
Marie, and soon after his appearance in the
royal palace the vigilant R.C. Church enrolled
the unsuspecting infant as a member. In short,
the archbishop, who had been lying dark off the
Weather bow awaiting the little iamily event,
sailed right in with a silver basinful of hol v-
water and sprinkled Boris’s bald head. That
made a Roman Catholic of him, it appears. To
the irreligious mind it doesn’t appear to matter
much what they make of an animated lump
of human fat in the first stage of life.
Boris, up to now, believes in nothing
at all save lacteal fluid from Nature’s font,
as Mr. Micawber expressed it, varied bv an
occasional pull at a gilt-edged feeding-bottle.
If the nurse were to drop Boris out of the
window he would proceed, in the Bulletin’s
opinion, straight to Abraham’s bosom, even
though he had been dedicated to Atheism by
Joe Symes and all the apostles. But the
dogmatic mind takes a different view of the
matter. Also, the political eye of Russia’s
Czar has been glaring at Boris’s baptism re-
provingly, on the ground that an heir to the
Bulgarian throne ought to be a Greek church-
man. The Czar, we are told, worried papa
Ferdinand into promising a conversion of
young Boris —the Lord be with him always !
to the Russian national faith, whereupon
Princess Marie denied her husband conjugal
rights, and “ implored the prayers of the Pope
in her affliction.” Again theirreligiaus mind is at
a loss to understand how weeping Marie’s afflic-
tion could possibly justify itself. If the first
baptism carried any weight with the Almighty,
and was at all beneficial to Boris’s immortal
spirit, the second baptism couldn’t wipe out
the good work, Boris not being a consenting
party in either case. However, the devoted
mother evidently suffered much, and either as
the result of her prayers to Heaven or her peti-
tion to the Pope, the R.C. priesthood swooped
down upon Boris last week and hustled him
into some ancient order of monks. It may have
been a special order made for the occasion.
Anyway, Monk Boris is an accomplished
fact, with the down shaved off the top of
his scalp, and sandals on his tootsies, and
a crucifix dangling against his little tummy.
Therefore, the designs of the Greek Church
upon this early Christian are likely to
come to grief. There is no historic precedent
for deconsecrating a monk who has committed
no sin. The enemy might grease Boris an inch
thick with Greek Church ointment, but he
would still remain subject to the Pope, The
Bulletin imagines ; neither can this calm, un-
prejudiced journal bring itself to protest against
the existing arrangement. The day for expect-
ing religion to adapt itself to the leaching of
common sense is not yet awhile, and, in the
meantime, what’s the odds as long as Boris’s
mother is happy ?

The Walsh. Case (at Ballarat).
Jan. 17th. —First brought under public notice by the
newspaper report of “ a pale, delicate-looking young
woman, who had been arrested, for her own protection,
as she was apparently dying in the street.”
She had been discharged from Ballarat Hospital
that day, after being an inmate for three
months. On the Bench suggesting that she
should return to the institution, she absolutely
refused, alleging that she had been cruelly
treated there. However, after persuasion, she
changed her mind, and the Bench sent her to
the hospital in charge of a constable, who was
instructed to request the authorities “not to
treat her harshly.” Whether this request irri-
tated the hospital people, or whether they had
previously determined not to re-admit her, is
not known, but they refused to take her in,
declaring she was a malingerer.
Jan. 18. —So she was sent to gaol for a week. The
Govt. Medical Officer stated emphatically that the woman
was “ seriously ill.”
Jan. 20. —A leading Ballarat doctor declared her to be
suffering from alcoholic gastritis.
(Which, as she had been in the hospital three
months, sounded peculiar.)
“The doctor of the Colac Hospital wires that the woman
was once an inmate, and undoubtedly a malingerer ; she
was got rid of by a ruse.”
The doctors of the Clunes and Creswick hospi-
tals also wrote to the same effect, stating that
the blood she vomited she first produced by
pricking the back of her throat, and swallowing
the blood. One doctor also stated that he had
performed an operation to find out if there was
anything the matter with her stomach, but it
was found to be perfectly healthy ! ' She was a
pure malingerer.
Jan. 28.—The Benevolent asylum took charge of the
woman. The asylum doctor considered her condition
critical.
After daily bulletins to the effect that she was
in no immediate danger !
Jan. 29. —It was suddenly found necessary to hold a
consultation, and four doctors met to diagnose the case.
It was some days before they would give an opinion ;
then they issued a bald statement to the effect that “ the
woman suffers from disease of the gastric organs.”
As a matter of fact, they at first intended to
pronounce her a humbug, and said so to mem-
bers of the Asylum Committee ; but before
they got out their official verdict the case
developed such alarming symptoms that they
were afraid their report and the notice of the
subject’s funeral might appear simultaneously !
Feb. 6. —Another doctor writes to state that he is
thoroughly acquainted with the woman, and looks on her
as “ a poor, helpless, afflicted creature'seriously ill from
disease of the stomach, probably caused by reflex action
from diseased neighboring organs.”
Feb. 7. —The public are informed that the woman is
dying.
Feb. 11. —The Colac doctors again assert that the
woman is a fraud. As they cut her open to find out, they
ought to know!
Feb. 12. —The woman’s children were brought to her for
her dying blessing, and the last rites of the Catholic
Church were administered.
Feb. 18.—The woman is not dead yet.
The Japanese-boot question has gone through
several interesting phases within the last week.
When it was first reported that a consignment
of Jap. boots had reached Sydney, the Even-
ing News and the Daily Telegraph both rose
up and flatly denied the fact. The latter paper
further stated that not only was there no such
shipment, but there couldn’t be one. There
was only one boot-factory in Japan ; it was a
very small affair, and couldn’t keep pace with
orders from the Court and from a few foreign
residents ; there was no surplus stock to ex-
port, and, in any case, Japan couldn’t sell boots
in Australia, even if it had them to sell, at
prices which could compete with the local
maker. Then, in its issue of Feb. 19, it sang a
different song in a rather different key. There
was a shipment of Japanese boots to Sydney—
it was “ only a little one ” to be sure,
but still it existed, therefore the pre-
vious tale about there being no Jap. boots
for export was evidently an invention.
Further, it acknowledged that the imported
Japanese boots were “25 per cent, cheaper
than the colonial-made article, and a good
many people would not be able to tell the
difference.” And, having thus blown its pre-
vious story to pieces, it went on to prove that
there were no cheap Japanese boots after all:
Did you ever realise that the highest price paid for the
actual labor on the pair of boots is not more than 25., or
thereabouts? Where, then, comes in the difference in
price between the home-made articles and the Japanese ?
Obviously not in cheapness of labor. The only alternative
is in the material put into the boot. I need say no more.
Therefore, the situation is something like this :
The only saving is on labor. Labor represents
2s. per pair in Australia, and not more than
fid. in cheap Japan, so the Jap is Is. 6d.
cheaper. Allowing 3d. per pair, which is
sufficiently liberal, for export charges, the Jap
boot is Is. 3d. a pair cheaper. Therefore it
isn’t any cheaper. And nothing at all is said
about the fact that ss. a pair paid in gold in
Sydney is equal to Bs. or 10s. a pair in the
depreciated silver coinage of Japan, which is
another big handicap in favor of the foreigner.
Meanwhile, the Evening News, at time of
writing, was still denying that there was any
shipment of Japanese boots to Sydney at all,
despite the fact that that allegation had totally
gone bung ; and the S.M. Herald was in the
background endeavoring, as usual, not to com-
mit itself.
Mr. Maxted, N.S. W. Director of Charitable
Institutions, gives Sydney Sunday Times some
details of the paupers under his charge, fed and
lodged by the benevolent tax-payer. Four
samples out of a score :
F.B.,aged74.—Twenty-nine years in Government Ser-
vice, N.S. VV., as C.P.S., in two important districts ; been
in institution three and a-half years ; exemplary charac-
ter ; well educated ; gentlemanly demeanor ; wears own
clothes ; case excites such sympathy that he is allowed to
go in and out at will; pleasant habits; brother a police
magistrate, N.S.W. ;< son, bank manager; another son a
magistrate, and well-to-do ; all decline to support him,
except bank manager, who allows Is. a week; have all
been written to on several occasions ; was compelled to go
into asylum to avoid starvation.
P. D., aged 75.—Fifty-five and a half years in colony;
twosons, postmasters in the Public Service, N.S.W. ; one
daughter, married to postmaster in an important town,
N.S.W. ; all relatives asked to contribute ; one son allows
ss. a week; other replied that when he wanted advice
from Dirhetor of Asylums as to how he should treat his
father he would ask it; daughter declined to contribute ;
head of non-contributing son’s department written to,
and circumstances of case explained, but declined to
interfere.
J.H.E.—Brother is a Civil Servant, and receives £4OO
per year. Has paid nothing.
E. J., 67 years of age.—Twenty-four years a colonist ;
before admission to asylum was stopping at Night Refuge;
son now' retired ; lately manager of an important bank in
N.S. Wales ; refuses to assist her, and stopped paying for
board and lodging to force her into the poorhouse.
Will the Civil Service Board kindly bring pres-
sure on the Police Magistrate, the postmaster
and the postmaster’s wife, and the unspecified
civil servant, and compel them to relieve the
State which pays them salaries from the respon-
sibility of providing for their aged parents ?
There may be no law to do it, but there are
more ways of killing a kangaroo than serenading
it with the Salvation Army. It is right for the
people to provide for the destitute, but not when
their sons and daughters are in receipt of the
people’s money every month. And the Civil
Service Board should either put the screw on
or take the “ screw ” off. As for the bank-
managers, squatters, and others who figure in
Maxted’s black-list, the Government should
advertise their names in the Government
Gazette.
Yic. Assembly last week rejected the Turner
Govt.’s Wages Attachment Bill, which proposed
to stop the attachment by creditors of wages
up to <£3, on lines similar to an Act of the sort
already existing in England. The general idea
is to protect the subsistence of small debtors.
The Bill was opposed by nearly every store-
keeper in the House with the argument that it
would ruin the credit of the majority of the
people in the province and “ prevent the honest
working man from running up a little bill when
sick or disabled ” —the obvious fact that the
honesty of the “honest workingman” is the
real security being very conveniently ignored.
Trenwith pointed out that creditors could
still get fraud-summonses against fraudu-
lent debtors, but Harper declared that no
machinery should be put in the hands of
wage-earners to enable them to repudiate their
debts—that privilege, presumably, being a pre-
rogative of the banks. Higgins went to the
root of the matter by showing that the Bill
would prevent unscrupulous storekeepers from
mopping-up the whole of the wages of men,
who, if given time, would pay anyhow, and
that the threatened restraint of credit would be
wholesome, anyhow. Madden declared that
debtors with <£3 per week would defy their
creditors—but he apparently offered no objec-
tion to the exploitation of unfortunate debtors
by rapacious creditors. Specious arguments
fathered by Fat Man were too much for Vic-
toria, and the Bill was defeated by 34 to 33.
One of the first manifestations of the compara-
tive moral cleanliness resultant on the appoint-
ment in N.S. Wales of an independent board to
control the public service was made last week.
The dictation to the N.S. Wales magistrates by
the Department of so-called Justice has for
years been one of the crying scandals of a
scandal-ridden country. The springs of justice
were polluted at their very source, but the
magistrates being, as a consequence of their
utterly dependent position, as clay in the hands
of the political potter, no man among them had
the courage to tell the Department to go to the
devil. At last the change has come, or is
coming. Last week, the Bench of Stipendiaries
held-a meeting to consider a circular referring
to the late diseased-meat cases ; and “a resolu-
tion was carried unanimously that a reply be
sent to the Minister insisting on their
right to decide the cases on the evi-
dence.” Unfortunately for their own credit,
the magistrates have for years submitted
to being brow-beaten by the Department
of Justice without protest—as witness the
iniquitous flogging circular, and the directions
to inflict imprisonment for breaches of the
Betting Act. The natural inference is that
they had not sufficient nerve and integrity of
principle to kick while they were under poli-
tical control; but as soon as they are out of the
Department’s clutches, and responsible only to
the Civil Service Board, they gave the Minister
a well-deserved snub —which The Bulletin
hopes will be the first of a long series of
similar insults to the club “ push ” who have so
long run the Department. Here Tiie Bulletin
takes occasion to remind certain of the Sydney
S.M’s that the Civil Service Board cannot be
toadied to as were the old authorities, and that,
therefore, it will be well for them to refrain
from giving the Board occasion to measure up
their shortcomings—bibulous and financial.
A small, obscure row now going on in Victoria
over the proposed Glenrowan-Hedi railway is
interesting m a way, as casting some light on
the results of a vigorous borrowing policy.
This little line, it is generally admitted, is
much wanted, but as the estimated revenue is
only about <£6oo a year over the estimated
expenses, the standing committee sorrowfully
rejected it. The borrowed money now on hand
has cost about 3§ per cent., and unless a pro-
posed new railway can pay that amount it lias
to be thrown out. As there are no rail-
ways left to be built in Victoria which
seem likely to pay anything like the needful
percentage for a long, long time, the
throwing out process is repeated almost every
time a new line is suggested—in fact, the Rail-
way Commii tee now exists for the purpose of
decisively throwing out almost everything.
Therefore Victoria is broughtup against the dead
wall of three alternatives. One is to stop railway
building, and as almost all the lines built in the
past were crowded together in the centre of the
province, while the east and west were unpro-
vided for, this means that about half the pro-
vince must remain indefinitely a neglected
wilderness. The second alternative is to go on
borrowing money at 3J per cent, and building
railways which only yield 1 per cent, till the
province bursts. The third is to raise more
money by more taxation, or by State monopo-
lies, or by some other means, and build
tlie Gippsland and Wimmera lines out of
revenue, and let the 1 per cent, profit, or what-
ever it amounts to, be a clear gain to the Trea-
sury. Even in the thickly-peopled regions of
Europe and America there are very few lines
nowadays which pay 3J per cent, on their cost,
and now that Australia has made most of its
good lines, and the ones left to make are affairs
that won’t, as a rule, pay over 1J per cent, for
many years to come, and as money can’t be
borrowed at 1| per cent, anywhere, the old
policy is practically played out. But there are
2000 miles of railway still to be built in New
South Wales and Victoria that will yield a
small profit now, and a much larger one later
on, if any Government feels inclined to attempt
the work on a new basis.
“No Australian lawyer,” said a Sydney stipen-
diary the other day, “advocates punishment
for attempts at suicide on the ground that the
individual actually intended to take away his
life. The basis of the statute lies deeper in
human nature. Most attempts at suicide,
notably among young females, are not intended
to be successful; they are merely ebullitions of
vexation or bravado whose object is to stun or
appal some interested person or persons. Will
any sound reasoner say that Law should pro-
vide no punishment for these? It is also
morally certain that a large percentage of suc-
cessful attempts result from accident. Of
course it is impossible,” the stipendiary
warmly proceeded, “to discriminate with
mathematical exactness in moral questions,
but when a person charged with attempt
at suicide comes before a magistrate, that
official* is expected to exercise discretion.
If the accused seem mad, he sends him for
observation; if he have yielded to impulse,
just as one might yield to a sudden assault on
another, or if the result be traceable to a fit of
pique or vexation, a punishment suiting the
various conditions is imposed. The magistrate
may sometimes be a ninny ; but does anyone
who knows human nature think that such at-
tempts should go unpunished ? Again, suppose
the law on this subject repealed : Would not
attempt at suicide be the favorite resort ot the
hysterical, the distressed, and the cunning
advertisers of their private injuries ? ”
New South Wales Chief-Justice Darley and
Justices Innes, Simpson, and, for a few minutes,
Cohen, at the opening of term last week dis-
ported themselves according to custom in
scarlet, ermine, and full-bottomed wigs. The
spectacle provoked an occupant of the gallery
to call up the petition presented by a weary
public to Oliver Cromwell in the old dictator
days, which petition prayed that the Judges
who then went circuit in their scarlet should
no longer be allowed to “ affright the country
with their blood-red robes and their state and
pomp.” The strong minds on the judicial
Bench have been the least addicted to law-court
George Rignoldism. Baron Pollock tells how
Lord Campbell, when at the Bar, likened the
array of judges in scarlet on opening of term to
the scene in “ Othello,” where a parcel of
carpenters and scene-shifters are dressed up to
come on as the senators of Venice. The smaller-
headed the man on the Bench, the more
zealously throughout British history has he
cultivated the externals of his office. British
judges, whose manners Australians take as
models, began the judicial career in ordinary
attire, as ecclesiastics also began. But year by
year, or epoch by epoch, they added a garment
or a color, until a judge at the opening
of term looks a cross between an insanely-
overdressed policeman’s wife and the harlequin
of a pantomime.
Germany’s determination to get Holland
is the explanation of many things.
Judge Docker did one good service the
other day, at Bathurst (N.S.W.), when he sat
heavily on the police for not informing a prisoner
of the absence of a material witness subpoenaed
for the defence. The police round up- the Crown
witnesses most carefully, and, if one of them
insists on being absent, he gets a bad time.
The prisoner, being in gaol, can’t round up his
witnesses ; if he has no money, he can’t pay some-
one dlse to do it; and the police won’t do it—they
generally won’t even tell him whether his wit-
nesses have turned up or not. Also, when the
prisoner’s witnesses don’t turn up, the Court, as a
rule, doesn’t fall on them with anything like the
vigor it manifests when falling on the person who
didn’t turn up \o swear on the other side of the
case.

Westmacott’s new drama, the “Enemy’s
Camp,” at Sydney Royal, is a garish, painted howl
in many, many acts; also it is an honest, de-
serving howl which does all that mortal can do in
its particular line to command success. The
scenery all through is super-excellent; the co. is a
good one ; and a large sensation is turned on every
five minutes, and sometimes oftener. And in
addition to these recommendations the “Enemy’s
Camp ” is not more hopelessly improbable than
the average, and it is a good deal less improbable
than many things the present writer has seen in
cold print on a tombstone. There is an insur-
rection in it, a desperate fight around a burning
railway-station, a blazing city that flames like
Sodoin and Gomorrah when they wereover-insured,
and a rope-bridge, which Plimmer cuts with a
sword while Scot Inglis is standing on it, where-
fore the latter falls into an abyss with both hands
full of broken bridge, and a girder on his head.
Further, there is an entirely new sensation,
wherein Scot Inglis, being a prisoner with both
hands chained behind him, keeps up his end of a
desperate encounter by means of a knife which
he holds between his teeth, and bobs his enemy
with his head till the foe is laid out. The foe
revives and rushes him again, but tnis time the
desperate captive boots him in the stomach, and
gives him indigestion for life. This is a rather
new mode of combat, but possibly it would be
even more realistic if the prisoner bit his enemy in
the second round. A military execution, and a
duel with swords between Misses Harrie Ireland
and Fitzmaurice Gill, and a soared clergyman who
falls into a drain, are some more features of this
lavish drama.
* * * *
When Miss Harrie Ireland isn’t duelling
she spends most of her time languishing in a white
dress on a bed of straw in a dungeon, and escaping
through a hole in the wall, and being recaptured
and dragged back again by her brutal gaolers.
Miss Ireland doesn’t go back willingly, therefore
her captors seize her by her snow-white arms and
trail her over mountain peaks and across rocky
chasms on her return journey. She languishes on
the straw very artistically and has her food thrown
at her head by the principal ruffian in charge.
Scot Inglis, as the hero, is about half the drama,
and the remainder is mostly Plimmer, Sweeney,
and Miss Ireland. Plimmer is the person who
locks up Miss Ireland in the dungeon, and mur-
ders Miss Gill with a gory knife, and sools on the
ruffian who is booted on the stomach in the cellar,
and who conducts the execution and want 3 to
smack the dead body afterwards, but is prevented.
However, he tells the dead body what a fool and
a dunder-headed ass it was, and explains to it
that he is the person who stole its lost daughter,
and that the daughter isn’t of much account, any-
how, and he intends to throw her out, and the
dead body makes a note of these things and gets
square with him afterwards. All these people,
including the dead body, do their work remark-
ably well, and a lot of other people and an able
mechanist back them up in a capable fashion.
Taking it all round it is a satisfactory drama, and
the only thing The Bulletin regrets is that
Plimmer doesn’t really smack the body, whose
language under such circumstances should be
worth hearing.
H* H* H* H*
“Gondoliers” started a brief dazzle at
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday, the gloomy “ Yeo-
men of the Guard ” having retired to moan in its
appropriate cave till further orders, and pour
ashes on its head and weep. The new edition of
the Venetian opera is one of the brightest yet
seen in Sydney, and though the piece is now past
its first youth it ought, as done by the present co.,
to be good for much longer than the four nights
allotted to it. Brownlow and Courtice Pounds
make the best two-headed king shown at the
Lyoeum up to date. The new man, when he lets
himself go, is as good a dancer as the adored
Ryley ever was, and is a better actor than that
vanished deity, and when he runs on little errands
for the Minister of State his agility is simply
appalling. Brownlow also comes out unexpectedly
as a dancer of much ability, and when the two
kings break loose together they are the wildest
pair that ever wore the crown of Barataria.
Sydney Dean has improved somewhat of
late (though he does not make convincing
love to Florence Young) and seems to have at last
become somewhat acquainted with his own legs ;
and those ever-reliable people—Lauri, Vernon,
Mrs. Bracy, and Misses Florence Young and
Nina Osborne--shine as of yore. Miss Graupner
acts better, and looks better, than she did, but on
further acquaintance with her singing-voice it
appears to The Bulletin that that organ is still in
a badly-enfeebled state. By the time these few
remarks get into print the “ Gondoliers ” will have
retired to make room for “Nitouche,” wher in
the convent organist will get drunk and have his
hair cut for two nights only. On Saturday “Ma
Mie Rosette ” and the Living Pictures start a
week’s business, and the bad King is getting his
persuasive voice in order to lead May Pollard
astray when she dreams that she arrives with the
milk.
The management of Her Majesty’s intends
trying its luck on Saturday with “Monte
Christo.” This paper doubts if there is at present
any demand for the gorgeous hero who was im-
prisoned for 19 years in a dungeon, and then put
into a bag along with a large rock and cast into
the sea, from which he roue again to wreak an
awful vengeance on his enemies. Still it may
prove that he is the very person the public is
craving for. Anyhow, Dumpier is to be cast into
the moaning deep on “a scale of unparalleled ex-
cellence,” and will afterwards emerge and climb
on to a dripping rock with a completeness of detail
never before witnessed, or some tiling to the same
effect.

A new comedy, entitled “Wanted, a
Wife,” is to be staged on Saturday at Sydney
Criterion. It is expected to throw some more
light on the reasons why a man should undertake
to find board, lodging and clothes for another
man’s daughter, in return for getting his latch-
key confiscated and being ordered to get up early
and chop the wood for the kitchen fire.
* 0 H* S*
The Sydney Music Hall closed on Saturday
night for a brief rest, and when it re-opens the
management proposes to go in for drama. The
Hall is a small establishment; and the amount of
drama which could be put in a place of that size
is strictly limited, and for horse-race or tank or
military spectacle purposes it looks practically
hopeless. Meanwhile, the Cogills have started
business at the Opera House, in order to keep up
the legend that Sydney can’t get through without
two variety shows, and are giving a large, healthy
entertainment on the usual lines. At the Tivoli,
Coleman, Alice Leamar, the Ivanoff Brothers and
La Belle Maie, serpentine dancer and slack-wire
performer, are all raging simultaneously, and the
Rickards boom is, if possible, more violent than
ever. Also, a small, casual entertainment is run-
ning at the Guild Hall, once the forlorn Gaiety ;
and the Jerusalem Cyclorama is going strong with
the Star of Bethlehem turned on every evening at
eight.
Mrs. Harry Marshall, whose por-
trait appears in this issue, is one of the
many attractive daughters of Mr. Jas.
Thynne, the well-known Australian
actor an:l manager. Mr. Thynne is
now a member of the N.S.W. civil-ser-
vice, and has long since quitted the
thorny path at the back of the foot-
lights, but his name is still well-remem-
bered in theatrical circles. Mrs. Mar-
shall was born 22 years ago at Murrum-
burrah (N.S.W.), out beyond Goulburn,
in that peaceful region where nomen-
clature grows to abnormal dimensions,
and such places as Wallendbeen and
Cootamundra and Cumbamarrah and
Mullengandra and Gurrangoorang
(which is close to Korkbitoo) are things
of quite common occurrence. Her
first appearance on the stage was made
after her marriage with that clever
comedian, Harry Marshall, and her
success is entirely owing to his tuition.
Mrs. Marshall’s stage career so far has
been comparatively brief, and has been
principally in Queensland, her most
notable record being a three-weeks
season as Polly Eceles at Rockhampton.
Anyone who knows that forlorn town
on the muddy river, where the inhabi-
tants are too weary to live and too ex-
hausted to die, will understand that it
requires more than o; dinary ability to
run anything there for one consecutive
week, let alone three. Mrs. Marshall
is now joint lessee with Mr. Alfred
Woods at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, and
the shilling part of the house in par-
ticular has fallen in love with her as
one man for her performance of Dolly
in “To the West.” Owing to the
deficiencies of the Australian language
the programme is reduced to describing the firm as
“Messrs. Woods and Marshall.” This has created
an impression that Mrs. Marshall is a man —which
she isn’t.

The Rickards Touring Co. started its
career at Brisbane on Feb. 22. The co., which is
50 strong, drafted from Sydney Tivoli and Melb.
Opera House, will work down through N.S.W.
and Victoria after exploiting Bananaland. Rickards
hopes to have it on tour, reconstructed from time
to time, as long as his managerial banner waves.

“Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith” is much
sought after at Melbourne Princess’s, especially
by young women who stand trembling on the
brink of matrimony’s seething, choked-up pit.
These go joyfully into the theatre, attended by
Adolphus, and about three hours later they come
forth in a thoughtful mood which lasts fully ten
minutes. “ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” attempts to teach
too many things. It advises the young idea
against marriage, rails against the hollowness of
platoni; fri ndship between the sexes, and snorts
at the sin of unlegalised love. “ Would you have
every man with hi 3 head shaved, and every
woman in a cloister —carrying out to the full the
ascetic principle?” asks Thackeray’s Pendennis
of his friend Warrington. “ Would you have
conventicle hymns twanging from every lan
in every city in the world ? Would you have
all the birds of the forest sing one note
and fly with one feather?’ Pinero interjects
“ Why, certainly ! ” At the finish all his principal
characters, with the honorable exception of Duke
Boucicault, sing one note. Mrs. Ebbsmith regrets
her brief burst of passion for Lucas Cleeve, who is
sorry that he brought it about. Mrs. Oleeye
curses herself for having asked the penitent to
backslide, and the Rev. Amos and Ins sistei,
having been good all the time, beam upon the
pious misery of their disciples. Rev. Amos is the
best pleasure that earth can afford to Mrs. Itbo-
smith, as far as one is able to comprehend Pinero s
meaning. He mustn’t marry her, or be her intel-
lectual chum, or kiss her slyly in the vestry.
Nevertheless, he’s the hero of her Hour when the
curtain falls.
* * * *
Duke Boucicault is the one healthy-minded
person in this crank play. Amos’s sister reckons
him up as “ a rather bad man,” and such a testi-
monial from such a lady does him great credit.
When Boucicault meets the pretty widow he im-
mediately hopes to improve the shining hour by
discussing Shakespeare and the musical glasses
with her at her lodgings at 4 p.m. next day. She
is an independent charmer, living on the pro-
perty of a late beast of a husband. A flirtation
with a clever, gentlemanly, gouty old peer would
be just the thing for her. But, because she fea,rs
that his Nibs wouldn’t approach her in a mis-
sionary spirit, the narrow-minded woman tries to
make him feel ashamed of himself. Happily,
she doesn’t succeed. A duke who knows
his own mind thoroughly never feels called
upon to blush for it. Dion Boucicaults
performance gathers artistic moss as it
rolls along, and Mrs. Brough improves somewhat.
But her delivery of such lines as “ One day father
turned a deaf ear to mother, and put his face to
the wall and died,” is quite insincere ; her high
notes are forced ; and an old tendency to cry
“ O-o-o-oh ! ” seems to be growing upon her. Cecil
Mrs. HARRY MARSHALL, of Her Majesty's Theatre,
Sydney.
Ward, on the other hand, prefers to suck in his
breath and say “A-a-a-ah!” This way of ex-
pressing feelings begins to annoy The Bulletin.
Some years back Mr. Ward was content to sigh
without an “ A-a-a-ah ; ” and if he must blow off
steam, or burst, this paper would prefer him to
return to his original practice.

in a Bar-room, and every tune that lurid sermon
on the strong drink question is offered to the

to have a simple moral lesson rubbed into him
again and again, so the chances are that he doesn’t

the drama is full of stirring incidents. If one

At Poverty Point.
Of contemporary lyric lady-stars, only four are
Britishers, says a London weekly. Then the writer
gives the names of the “British” quartette—Madame*
Melba, Albani, Frances Saville, and Alva. Two Aus-
tralians, one Canadian, and—what country did Alva spring
from ?
Ada Crossley got her first lessons in voice-production
from a surveyor’s wife in S. Oippsland. When a mere
child she would ride along the hush roads for miles to her
teacher’s solitary cottage among the scrub and sand-hills
adjacent to Port Albert. In Dec., ’B4, she made her first
appearance in public at the Port Albert Mechanics’ Insti-
tute, when she sang “ Wait till the clouds roll by.” During
the next year or so her services were much sought after
at bush concerts. Her favorite songs at this time were
“ When the leaves begin to turn,” “ Daddy,” and “ We’ll
sell the old grey mare.”
R. S. Smythe writes“ I never thought of engaging
Rudyard Kipling as a lecturer. Four years ago, in
London, A. Patchett Martin suggested him to me, but
without knowing whether he could lecture. When I met
Kipling afterwards, at the Yorick Club in Melbourne, I
didn’t think he could. Every year, both from England
and America, I receive dozens of applications from people
who, as lecturers, wouldn’t draw the rent of a hall. Of
all the celebrities I have run during the Jast 20 years, only
the Rev. Charles Clark stood in the front rank as a plat-
form orator ; he was the first, and he was the best. The
last man suggested tome is Jameson, who would probably
turn out to be a Bore.”
Maud Hobson supplied her own cerulean tint to that
specially naughty line in “In Town.” “Shrimp, if the
dark gentleman calls for me at the stage door—you know,
the dark gentleman, not the fair one—tell him my hus-
band has returned unexpectedly, and I’ll have to go home
to-night." In the prompt-book, the excuse for putting
off the dark gent was quite innocent. A sick mother, or
something of that sort. The husband was purely a Hob-
sonesque idea.
Gee, a one-time popular Melb. baritone, has been sing-
ing at the London halls. At the Palace he was pro-
grammed under the name of Gresham, so as not to injure
bis concert prospects. He is only following in Sims
Reeves’s footsteps. Sims received £2OO for three appear-
ances at the Empire.
Arthur Greenaway, spoken of by S. African papers as
“The Australian Romeo,” was amongst the first who
volunteered for service with the Uitlan lers in Johannes-
burg, where he intimated in a ringing voice, clearly
audible to the reporters, that he was prepared to shed his
last drop of blood in protection of the women and
children ; which, as the Boers are notoriously humane,
does not imply that the fair young A.G. is in any danger
of bleeding to death.
Eversfield, whose suicide is cabled, was the original of
Boucicault’s part in “ The Magistrate.”
Santley’s violent antipathy to the tremolo in singing is
partly a matter of business. All English singing-teachers
