Boil up “The Scarlet Letter,” of Hawthorne, and
“ Judah ” and you will (says The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent) get the essence of Henry Arthur Jones’s much-
talked-of new play, “ Michael and His Lost Angel.”
Given a very high church parson (or “priest” as those
gentry prefer to call themselves) who is morality itself,
and who, on one occasion, publicly reproved a young lady
who had been led from the Path of Virtue by a Villain,
yet who himself becomes the victim of a married woman,
and you have Forbes Robertson at the Lyceum. By and
by, conscience smites him, and he determines to do
penance publicly in full face of the congregation who have
so long worshipped him as the very acme of goodness and
virtue. Finally you'find him, after soothing the death-
bed of his fellow sinner, trying to work out salvation as a
monk in a R.C. monastery. Jones is nothing if not bold,
and few dramatists would have had the daring to deal
with such a subject a second time as he has done in this
play and “ Judah.” Marion Tree is uncommonly good as
the grass widow to whose wiles the ascetic Robertson
falls a victim, whilst Forbes is admirably suited to the
part of the man who cannot resist temptation. No
actor on the stage to-day could look or act the part
better, and he wears the priestly garb like a professional
sky-pilot. It is a moot question whether the public will
flock in their thousands to see this semi-religious play as
they do to “ The Sign of the Cross,” but it is bound, for a
time at least, to be a succPx de curio site, especially as the
papers have begun interviewing the clergy as to their
ideas of having such a subject treated on the stage.
Amongst others Dr. Parker, of the City Temple, gives his
approval, saying he is not one of those who hold that
religious services should never be presented on the stage,
quoting the church scene in “Much Ado about Nothing”
at the Lyceum ; but he adds the reservation that they
must be done well. Archdeacon Sinclair, who is a leader
of women, has also given his ideas on this subject, but he
is distinctly of opinion that Jones has in this instance
“exceeded the bounds of good taste and discretion”;

It seems as if religious plays are going to have a boom,
vvuson Barrett having made a hit with “ The Sign
of the Cross both in London and the provinces (writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent). Crowds are
turned away nightly, and clergymen, actuated, no doubt,
by free stalls, give big ads. of the play from their pulpits.
Barrett is cute. There is now a great opportunity for
Joseph in Canaan,” the play Rignold lately produced ill
bydney. The title will not alone comm n 1 itself to
the flighty, but is bound to draw the Church as well.
Again, Rignold is not your ordinary giddy mummer—
he has always had the reputation of being a perfect
Joseph in private life, and it will be a treat to see his
virtue displayed coram populo.

Mrs. Brough’s fondness for flopping on the (assumed)
red-hot stove in “ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” and escaping scorched
arm is not an oversight on her part. The book contains
specific instructions for her to do what she does, and as
“Mrs. Pat.” in Hingland and Julia Neilson in ’Murka do

Has this anything to do with Dot Boueicault’s projected
departure for Hingland in June? Ascribe in the Gentle-
woman writes : —“ With the roses of June a wondrous new
song-bird is to fly into our midst. . . . Several friends
of mine . . . assure me that since Patti’s no such
voice has been known. And the name, you ask, of the
young Diva ? It is Boucicault. Well, Miss Boucicault,
then, is a niece of the great Dion himself, and, apart from

high order. And her age, again, you ask? Just twenty.

first abroad, and in June at Covent Garden, under the

And introduced a mate so true,
A comrade true you proved to be,
A comrade true you’d always be. (!)

We pray that in your next you may know no care ;

May you have found them lit, as Clear as day. (!)

May we all some day greet you at home. (!!)
We mourn your loss, ’twas thus to be ;
The act drops down. Au revoir, Dickie.

Angel,” was produced simultaneously in London and New
York. As Jones could not attend rehears ds at the latter
place, he spoke the whole play into the phonograph and
sent it over in the shape of eight cylinders.

| |“ Agnes : There is only one hour in a woman’s life.
St. Olpherts : One? Agnes : One supreme hour. Her
poor life is like the arch of a crescent; so many years lead
up to that hour, so many weary years decline from it.
No matter what she may strive for, there is a moment
when Circumstance taps her upon the shoulder and says,
* Woman, this hour is the best that Earth has to spare
you.’ It may come to her in calm or in tempest, lighted
by a steady radiance or by the glitter of evil stars ; but
however it comes, be it good or evil, it is her hour —let
her dwell upon every second of it 1 ” — The Notorious Mrs.
Ebbsmith.

Death was busy among London theatrical identities at
Xmas time. The first to go w_as Miss Pauncefort, who
acted in her prime at the Surrey Theatre, under the ban-
ner of Creswick. Twenty-three years ago she joined the
Lyceum Company, and her name figured in “ old woman ”
parts upon most of the famous Irving bills. Pauncefoit
was the original Burgomaster’s >vife in “ The Bells,” also
Queen-mother to the great man’s Hamlet, and many
another useful character. Arthur Swanborough, of the
old Strand Theatre, went under in Xmas week. Arthur
served as business-manager for his mother all through the
best times of Strand burlesque, from the era of Marie
Wilton (Mrs. Bancroft) down Jo the reign of Edward
Terry. His fund of spicy stories, re ladies who have-dong
since drifted into severe respectability, was inexhaustible.
Mrs. Stirling died on December 28th, aged 79. The grand
old dame, who, by-the-way, had a much greater stage
reputation than Mrs. Keele.v —married a seventy-seven-
year-old knight, or baronet, in 1894. The title probably
hastened her decease. Last item in this sad history—Bi 1
Holland, the People's Caterer, pegged out with the old
year. Lots of Anglo-Australians will remember Bill
Holland. He ran many theatres, circuses and pleasure-
gardens, and possessed a rare genius for going insolvent.
Bill was the father of barmaid-shows and baby-shows.
Moreover, he introduced bull-fights into London, at the
Agricultural Hall. The bulls were ridiculously small and
lean, and the tenth-rate toreadors and matadors couldn't
get sixpen’orth of fight out of the poor beasts. Still, it
was the only' show of the kind that Hingland ever got.

The Citizens’Life Assurance Co., for the first
time since The Bi lletin' made its acquaintance, looks
a little better than it did. The income of the indus-
trial branch for ’95 was £133,404 against £127,000 in ’94,
and of this it saved and added to its funds £10,503 (£7790
from interest and rents and just £2713 out of a gorgeous
premium income of £125,000), making the whole accumu-
lation £172,949. Last year it saved £10,059 out of an in-
come of £127,' 00, so in this respect there is no improve-
ment worth mentioning. The point where the advantage
comes in is that, while the co. collected £133,404 and
spent out of it £122,901, a little less went in expenses and
a good deal more in payments to policy-holders than
usual. The expenses, including dividend to shareholders,
were only £71,766, so that out of every shilling paid by
the humble policy-holder the co. spent barely' sevenpence
in ex’s., which is sufficiently dreadful, whereas it formerly
spent more than ejghtpenee. Also, it paid £51,000 to
policy-holders against £32,000 last year, so that there is a
decided improvement in the present and room for a
lot more in the future. The assets of the indus-
trial branch y'ield over £4 13s. per cent, all round, and out
of £7790 interest, only £723 - a good deal less than last
year—is returned as being in arrear. Whether the co.’s
freehold property, which was written up by £24,000 in
’9l is worth, at present prices, anything' like the £69,891
set against it, is an open question, and this is an asset to
be viewed in an enquiring spirit.
One acknowledgment made in the report
re the industrial branch is staggering. It is to the
effect that no liability' has been provided for in the
case of a number of policies “ which have only been in
existence a few months.” As the co.’s liability begins the
instant it issues a policy, this cheerful way of making no
provision in certain cases is highly interesting. At the
1891 investigation it was announced that “ policies for
the whole term of: life of less than three years’ duration
and endowment assurances under one year’s duration
were assumed to have no liability' attaching,” and in re-
gard to this practice it is only' necessary' to say that any'
kind of an office could make its accounts square by
assuming that a vast number of its policies have “no
liability attaching.” The Daily Telegraph estimates
that, on the present occasion, policies insuring £1,163,160
have been “ assumed ” to carry no liabi ity—anyhow the
whole of the co’s liability is set down at just a little over
one year’s premium income, which looks sufficiently
astonishing'. How would the figures have come out if the
Citizens’ hadn’t “ assumed ” so much ?
The Citizens’ ordinary life business shows a
premium income of £40,819, against £28,692 in ’94, which
is a truly handsome increase. Its total income is 144,089,
and out of this it saved and added to its funds over
£26,000. The expenses (16613) are only 16 per cent, of
the premiums or 15 per cent, of the total income. Thoug'h
the two concerns are run together, the ordinary bran, h
makes £4 19s. percent, on its investments, while the in-
dustrial gets only £4 13s. The ordinary branch isn’t
charged with any part of the £2OOO for shareholders’
dividend, nor with any of the depreciation of office furni-
ture, nor with any actuarial expenses, travelling expenses,
exchange or Government taxes. Also, the industrial
branch pays £5367 of the advertising bill, and the
ordinary branch only t 75, while the other charges are
mostly stood by the industrial department in much the
same fashion. Therefore, the ordinary life assurance
branch is one of the most cheaply managed offices in
Australia, while, to all appearance, it would be
nothing of the kind if a great part of its legitimate
charges weren’t borne by the industrial. One in-
teresting feature is that the ordinary department has
£5320 invested in the co.’s freehold property while
the industrial department has £64,571, and 'as the
ordinary makes so much better a return on its invest-
ments than the other, one possible inference is that the
office property isn’t a paying asset, and that the concern
which holds the smaller end. of that unprofitable asset
comes off best in consequence. Anyhow, the ordinary
insurers have just received a very handsome bonus, and
probably they don't care whether their department booms
partly at the ex'pense of the 3d.-a-week branch or not.

zens’ Co. is that Manager Jimmy Garvan shouldn't fill

the co.

The Flood estate trouble is on again. Some
years ago the National Building, Land and Investment
Co. (Sydney) bought a large piece of land at Leichhardt
for £10,600. It paid £3833 and owed the rest. Then it
cut up the property and re-sold at a profit to a lot of
harmless, unsuspecting workpeople, who never asked if
the co. had a title. The co. co.lected the instalments
from these people, and instead of paying off the mortgage
with them it used them in some other way. Now some of
the buyers have paid up in full and can’t get a title. Others
have paid up in part. Almost all have spent their life’s
savings, or most of them, in building cottages on their
allotments. And now the original owners of the land
propose to sell the property under their unsatisfied
mortgage, and if it is sold the instalments which these
poor workpeople have paid, and probably their houses as
well, will all be lost, and most of them will be utterly
ruined.
The National B. L. and I. Co. knew when it
sold the land that it would be called on to supply titles
when the buyers had paid up, and as it never, even in the
good times when money was tolerably plentiful, seems to
have paid a copper off its own mortgage out of the money
it collected from its buyers, it seems fair to assume that
the co. didn’t care what became of the luckless buyers.
So far as The Bulletin knows, there is nothing illegal
about its proceedings, but there certainly ought to be.
The people who sell a valueless agreement to deliver a
quarter-acre of land, and take money' for it, should come
into the same category as the man who sells a valueless
cheque or anything else of the same kind. The difference
between the two circumstances, so far as the people who
suffer by the proceeding are concerned, is practically nil,
but if anything the company dodge is the more heartless
of the two.

A Melbourne paper openly declares that one of
the auditors of a hopelessly-bung Melbourne bank was a
large shareholder in that institution, but sold out gradu-
ally between 1890 and 1894. If this is true, then the
position of an auditor who certifies to balance-sheets which
show, or tend to show, that the concern is solvent, while
he is, himself, gradually getting out of the crumbling in-
stitution, is just a little delicate.

Hudson Brothers, Limited, a big N.S.W. con-
cern with £173,000 of paid-up capital, and 10s.-shares
that are nominally quoted at about Id., is going
the familiar road. In 1894 the co. made a loss
of £14,378, and in 1893 it was £11,151 to the had.
Another heavy loss has been made in 1895. It is there-
fore proposed to turn the preference shares into 6 per
cent, debentures ; to write off one-half the capital ; and
to make “large reductions” in salaries and wages. The
lamentable thing is that, as Hudson Brothers has paid no
divs. for a long time, the reduction of the capital will
make no saving in the outgo, and, if things are at present
anything like on the ’94 basis, even a reduction of wages
to one-half won’t show a profit. In ’94, the gross profits
and rents were only' £1196, while working expenses alone
were over £BOOO, and, under conditions like these,
economy looks about hopeless.

The Savings Bank of N.S.W. has been com-
pelled to reduce the interest on deposits from five to four
per cent. The Savings Bank divides all its profit among
depositors save a certain proportion carried to reserve
fund and allowances for depreciation, and the steady fall
in interest has made the 4 per cent, unavoidable. The
reserve, by T the way, is now £235,000; the interest paid
during ’95 is over £140,000, and the amount at credit of
depositors is £3,951,000.

M'Cracken’s City Brewery (Melb.) has made a
loss during 1895 of £22,923. Last year the loss was 19983,
and in ’93 this same luckless heer enterprise dropped
£4636 of good money into the vat and never saw it. again.
The City Brewery is loaded down with £500,000 worth of
debentures and £lOl,OlO of mortgages, and the load is
evidently a lot more than it can stagger under. Its prin-
cipal asset is £900,000 worth of buildings, public-
houses and goodwill taken at original cost, and the
thought of what the depreciation in this great mass of
public-houses may amount to is enough to turn the
average shareholder grey'. Also, “ goodwill ” in a case
like this is a truly interesting asset.
« * * *
The Queensland Life Insurance Act of 1870 is
supposed to protect policies of deceased persons against
“judgment, decree, order or process of any court, or in
any other manner whatsoever.” It was framed and passed
into law with the express object of making the moneys
due a certainty to the heirs. The Queensland Supreme
Court has recently decided a novel point: One Goodrick,
a chemist, died, and his estate proved to be bankrupt.
Deceased left a will with the old-fashioned proviso that
“ after payment of his just debts, etc.,” his estate should
be divided as directed. His creditors claimed the benefit
of a £2OOO policy, and the judges have held that, de-
spite the Act, there was no bar to the assured disposing,
while alive, of his interest in the policy, and that the
wording of the will put the policy outside the Act; so
the amount claimed was ordered to go the estate. If
Goodrick had died intestate his family would have bene-
fited, as no doubt he had always intended, to the full
extent of the policy. The judges virtually said : Always
let creditors look after themselves, and make no mention
of just or other debts in your will.
* * * *
Bankruptcy item from Sydney D.T. : “The
witness, said the Registrar, had sworn over and over again
that he had made entries in a certain book prior to 1894,
whereas a calendar for 1894 printed into the cover showed
that the entries must have been made subsequent to the
dates sworn to.” With or without due respect, the
calendar shows nothing of the kind. It merely shows
that the entries were made after the calendar was printed.
As it is the trade custom to print calendars —
especially in book-covers—months in advance, in order to
sell them before the year comes round, it is possible that
the book in question had been written in any time within
the last six months of 1893. A legal non sequitur, there-
fore, on which a witness got seven days’ commitment to
gaol for “prevarication.”

A sweet passage-at-arms between Minister Best
and Elder Harper occurred in Vic. Assembly the other
night. Harper was showing some more antipathy' to
Attorney-General Isaacs when Best interjected : “ The
Companies’ Act again 1 ” Then Harper solemnly warned
Theo. Fi' k’s partner that if he made any more insulting
remarks re the Companies’ Act he would “get as much as
he gave.” The haste with which Best apologised for
hurting his hon. friend’s feelings was not more painfully
funny than Harper’s alacrity in accepting the with-
drawal. All the rest of the evening the holy Harper
seemed to be praying inwardly : “O Lord! teach Thy
servants who dwell in glass houses to refrain from throw 7 -
ing stones.”

The boot-manufacturing trade in Melbourne is
so rotten that during the last three weeks there have been
no less than 16 meetings of creditors in that line. The
wine and spirit trade is cut so fine that, practically, mer-
chants have to sell at a gross profit of 9 per cent, to 10
per cent., and, in nearly every case, have to pay cash out
of pocket (spot) in duty on lines on which they take bills
at three months, with the risk of the customer “ standing
up ” for that time.

The Victorian Income-tax Commissioner got a
nasty jar from the Full Court last week. He claimed to
charge the tax upon a legacy of £3675 received by a Mrs.
Everett, but the Court—it goes without saying - decided
that a lump sum of legacy is no more income for a
particular y ear than bank-shares are interest on the in-
vestment. A millionaire who kept all his wealth in gold
in a cellar wouldn’t be subject to income-tax though he
lived at the rate of £85,000 a y'ear.

crushing, 2313 tons at ss. 3d. per ton, £629 10s. Bd., leav-
ing a balance of £2174 Bs. Id. The company tools one-
half of that balance, £lOB6 13s. 9d., and charged the men
who were prospecting and developing that mine at the
expense of their butchers, grocers, bakers, and landlords,
£l4 for keeping the air courses clear, £53 7s. 6d. for plat
work, and £1 for charcoal making, a total of £1152 Is. 3d.
After paying sundry expenses there was only £BB5 10s.
left to be divided between twenty shareholders for twelve
months’ work, and that was considered one of the very
best tribute mines in the district. Unless the Minister
did something in the direction of improving the tributing
system of prospecting, there would be very few miners
living in Ballarat a few y'ears hence.”
* * • •
Civil servants are in a quandary over the
N.S.W. Superannuation Act, as recently amended. Any
effort on their part to find out from the authorities just
how they stand in relation to the Act, leaves them in a
worse fog than before. The other day a C.S. wrote ask-
ing the straight-out question whether or not, in the event
of his resigning the service, he would receive back
his contributions to the fund ; and, by way of reply, was
informed that his letter, “seeking information on the
Superannuation Act,” was being held over till further
notice, “ pending further consideration and legislation.”
Which looks as if the fund can’t stand the strain of too
many withdrawals all at once, and that the Govt, intends
to amend the Act some more. If this is so, there is but
one way for the Govt, to redeem its promises to the civil
servants, tacitly' given them when the fund was inaugu-
rated, and at the same time conserve the interests of th*
people : Let the Superannuation Fund be converted into a
State Life-assurance Department.
* * * »
The savage battle between the rival Manly
ferries continues. Within a few weeks the Co-operative
Co will have running the finest ferry-boat yet seen on
Sydney Harbor. The Co-operative people are understood
to claim that even under the 3d.-fare system they are not
losing capital, but only interest. It is hard, however, to
believe this.
* * * *
Service wept. It was when Coppin resigned his
Melb. Commercial Bank directorship. Coppin weeps too,
but does it at home. Harwood and Dick Stewart weep,
because their friend Coppin put them on to such good
things, and the ghost of Greville turns in his grave,
though he saved a bit out of the fire. Meanwhile Ser-
vice’s eulogy of Ooppin is warm, for “ introducing a large
amount of business from a quarter which could not other-
wise be reached.”
* * * *
The figures of the Mutual Life of New York
are growing almost too big to print. Its statement for
1895 has just been cabled to Major Rennie, and it shows
that the income for the year totalled nearly 10
millions. The assets had hulged out to £45,400,000,
a chunk of £3,400,000 having been added to them
m twelve months. Of new business actually paid
for, not merely accepted, there was a modest sum
of £30,000,000, which shows a net gain of £12,500,000
to insurances in force, bringing these up to a total of
£184,000,000. The bulk of the policies are held in the
States, hut the business of the company in Australia is
extending.

The next big Australian softgoods smash will
be that of a firm respecting which there has never even
been a breath of suspicion, and it will occur very shortly,
too.

* * * *
In Victoria, payable gold has been got at 3000 ft.,
whereas, in N.S.W., the greatest depth at which payablo
gold had been mined was 1040 ft. With the object of en-
couraging deep mining, N.S.W. Mines Minister Smith has
now approved of a reward of £lOOO being offered for the
discovery of payable gold at a depth of not less than 2000 ft.
The shaft may be perpendicular, or may follow the under-
lay, but the depth must be 2000 ft. perpendicular. For
the purpose of testing the reef, 250 tons of stone must be
taken from it, at or below 2000 ft., and crushed at a bat-
tery to be approved by the Minister; the stone to be
broken down, raised, and crushed under the supervision
of an officer of the Department of Mines. To obtain the
reward, the stone must yield not less than lOdwt. of gold
per ton. This is the minimum which will be deemed pay-
able. The reward will go to the first person who reports
gold at 2000 ft. and complies with the conditions.
* * * *
The Bulletin has often wondered if a £lOOO
reward would enable the N.S.W. Mines Department to do
something sensible. The unfortunate Minister Smith no
doubt imagines that the reward is sufficient to cause a
rush for it by many nines, and that some shafts will start
from the surface with the hope of winning this great
prize. Why, in many cases it is not enough to sink the
last 100 ft. Ten dwts. might be more than payable under
some circumstances, while lOoz. would not be under others.
The N.S.W. mines will go down when the owners think
there is anything to go down for, and to offer a miser-
able £lOOO for 2000 ft. in a country where the average depth
is not 200 ft. is a most grotesque absurdity.
*»* . *
B.H. South, for the half-year ended Dec. 31,
added £6233 ss. Id. to the credit of profit and loss
account, bringing the total up to £10,453 Is. lOd. During
the term the co. received £7448 12s. 6d. in surpluses over
advances on shipments made in the preceding half-year ;
and it estimates that it ought to get £11,971 11s. lid. in
surpluses above advances made in this last half. It has
paid away £7492 13s. for fuel and timber and “mine
charges,” £5OO to the South Disaster Fund, and £2636
15s. 2d. represents “ exploratory and developmental ex-
penses.” The timber-bill has no doubt been abnormal,
because (as The Bulletin said lately) the absence of a
second shaft prevents systematic muik ck ng-up, and the
collapse in the dopes proved that more support was neces-
sary. Also, as th s second shaft-way has been under con-
struction, the developmental expenses have been abnormal.
Reduce them 50 per cent., reduce the ccst of working
through the opening up of a second, shaft, and assuming
no serious fall in the quality of the ore rrthe price of lead,
and the mine, furnished with its present concentrating
plant, would seem to be about capable of paying £15,000
a half-year—three sixpenny dividends. No doubt there
will be a slight falling away in the quality of the ore pro-
duced, but gradually better results from the m fling
should be got. Yet nothing that now seems possible can
lead to a return of more than 3s. a year with the present
plant.
The proposal in connection with the B.H. South
is to add to the plant, the funds to be raised by selling one of
the three remaining blocks. (Block 5, by the way, was sold
by this co. !) But already the South has 200,000 snares of £1
each, and that seems even more than these blocks can
stagger under. One way would be to about double
the present capacity of the mill by mopping up the exist-
ing credit balance, but that would mean the certainty < f
no dividends for another half-year or so. Still another
way would be to be content with the present plant and
output—or, at least, to put up with it. Admittedly that
prospect would be only a dull drab, but it would be com-
paratively safe. Even as it is, to pay 3s. a year (3d. a
month) would mean the extraction of about 70,000 tons of
ore; and, though there has been much airy talk of the
“millions of tons of sulphides” in the Broken Hill mines,
a very big hole indeed is made in such a lode as the
South’s by the extraction of 70,000 tons. Visitors to the
Junction begin to appreciate that.
* * « ♦
Great Cobar Copper directors’ report would lead
anybody out of “ the know ” to think that the progress of
the mine was due to the board’s special exertions, while,
as a matter of fact, the aboriginal discoverer of the big
lode has done as much “ steady development ” as ii
has. The co. owes everything to the tributors and
Longworth’s method of economically treating low-grade
copper sulphide ore ; and, if the board had the mine on
its hands to-morrow, the shareholders would probably
divide a good deal less than the tribute-money. This
amounted to £3542 for the half; and, starting with a
credit of £5350, the co. paid two 6d. divs., totalling £4OOO,
and carries forward £4852. The average copper contents
of the 23,000 tons treated was only 4A per cent., but the
gold return of nearly 4oz. per ton of refined copper made
all the difference. With a 5 per cent, royalty on thi
copper and 10 per cent, on the gold, the co, is appar
ently doing almost as well as the tributors ; butthetribu
tors deserve all the credit.
* * * *
Beginning to look as if the Wentworth (N.S.W.)
mines are in for a poor year compared with last. Output
for four weeks ending 6th Feb., 750 tons for 2Jloz. gold—
also concentrates containing 3160 z. and 960 z. from six
tons of ore shipped to London. The glory of half a ton
of gold per month has gone. It would be interesting to
know exactly how that transient glory was obtained.
* * * *
Mount Brown goldfield, in the far N.W. of
N.S. Wales, includes Mt. Brown, Warratta, New Bendigo,
and Tibooburra: from an alluvial field, discovered 16
years ago, it has very slowly passed to the reefing stage.
There are several fair “shows,” but the solitary battery
cannot save more than two-thirds of the gold, owing
chiefly to the presence of galena in the stone. An attempt
has been made to get cyanide introduced to the district:
but it is hard work to get anything introduced to a scat-
tered place that lies 250 miles from any fair sized town,
along a track waterless most of the time.
* * * *
A rich find of silver and bismuth has been made
at Jingera, near Pambula (N.S. W.), a place created on
the strength of a discovery of this kind some four years
ago. It proved a patch, and the field was abandoned.

CAMEO
Melb. policemen have been ordered to stop
flower-girls of tender years from trading in hotel-
bars. Lately the wily practice has grown into a
sort of charitable institution. Hebe, when she
possesses a heart, is apt to encourage the ragged
little merchants to skirmish around her bar, tempt-
ing johnnies to buy a thrip’ny bouquet for the
lady. Threepence isn’t much to pay for a gracious
smile. Miss Hebe selects a cluster of three mori-
bund rose-buds and pins it in her breast, and
johnnie departs—happy. Then the infant flower-
vendor comes in to collect the “ buttonhole ” from
her patroness, who presently chooses it again from
the same basket at the earnest request of the
next johnnie. He departs happy, and again the
floral tribute returns to the place whence it
first came, and so the game proceeds. On
some days, when admirers are plentiful, the
fair philanthropist is responsible for at least half-
a-dozen threepenny purchases of one damaged
bunch of vegetables. In fact, some days she has
been the brief possessor, at one time or other, of
every “button-hole” in the child’s stock. Her
reward is, perhaps, a husky “ Gordblessyer !
miss,” spoken with a more or less clever assump-
tion of artlessness. Then the child goes home,
thinking how sweet it must be to stand behind a
bar for about 10 hours per diem “ foolin’ the chaps
like that. ” This is a crafty world. It is probable
that Hebe derives rather more satisfaction from
foolin’ the chaps than from fairly earning a bold-
eyed youngster’s benediction.
* * * *
The news that- diphtheria is prevalent
among the fleet at Hobart, and that of two officers
attacked one has died, will not tend to advertise
that beautiful but evil-smelling city as a health-
resort. Diphtheria comes from dirt. Without
dirt there can be no diphtheria. Dirt-diseases
don’t generate aboard well-scrubbed men-o’-war.
Let Hobart scrub itself.

Typhoid in some of the Sydney suburbs.
And it is the old, old story—scarcely varied in a
detail. Hollows filled up with garbage. This
they call “making ” the land. (It is being done
in Moore Park now.) Then the allotment-monger
.nines along, followed by the jerry-contractor, and
the time - payment, be-your-own-landlord auc-
tioneer. Next—streets are proclaimed. Result:
typhoid. The hardy community gets over it, and
population thickens around. People awake and
clamor for sewerage. They get it at long last.
And directly the drains commence to be dug—
typhoid ! among a crowded and all-ready popula-
tion. Yet fool copybooks tell us that j ‘ experience
teaches.”

• * * *
The Adelaide Gimlet Woman wants Govt,
to alter the local divorce law so that a wife can
get rid of her husband on proving against him
adultery alone—without cruelty. There is a sub-
stantial difference between male and female adul-
tery. A wife can generally make a roughly-
accurate guess as to the identity of her child’s
father, but husbands are not always in so fortunate
a position. A certain (or let us say uncertain)
Frenchman had surprised and was about to slay
the destroyer of his wedded happiness, when
Madame frenziedly interposed. “ Wretch ! ” she
exclaimed, “ wouid you slay the father of your
children ? ” As to the Adelaide Gimlet Ladies’
agitation “for amending the laws relating to
seduction by fraud or false pretence, so that a
woman may claim damages in her own right,
without the plea of loss of service to her guardian
or employer,” it looks like a New Woman’s
“ring” to boom the gentle art of being seduced
into a profitable industry.
* * * *
The Adelaide W.C.T.U. wants the age of
protection for girls to be extended from 16 to 18
years of age. Premier Kingston says the Govern-
ment is strongly inclined to do this. This being
so, it should also be prepared to pass an Act pro-
viding protection for men —say up to 90 years old.

“ Yes,” shrieked the lady-lecturer, in
shrill and breathless peroration, “now is dawning
the Age of Woman, the grandest age the world
has ever seen, and it has seen many ages, each
raising the human race to a higher plane than ever
before, each a crisis in the world’s history. Let
us name some of them—the Stone Age, the Copper
Age, the Iron Age, and now we enter upon that
whieh is, of all, the greatest, the noblest, the most
perfect, and the most critical, and that, friends,
is .” And, as she paused for breath sufficient
to pronounce once more in soul-stirring tones the
glorious name of that magnificent age, an unutter-
ably horrid Old He-Man among the audience
jumped up and yelled—■“ Yes—the Age of Con-
sent ! ”
Victoria, after a long lapse,
has again begun to make Govt, ap-
pointments from outside the service.
The police force and telegraph-
messenger staff have run down so
low in numbers as to make this nec-
essary. As far as possible, during
the last two years, police recruits
were drawn from the surplus hands
in other branches—chiefly the rail-
ways ; but few suitable men were
found. Only small boys are now
being appointed as telegraph-mes-
sengers ; as the retiring-age of lads
taken on has been fixed at 17, most
of the new appointees are not yet
out of knickerbockers.
* * ♦
Some Smellbourne sassiety
women are again bitten by the craze
to be photoed in the nude, a craze
which comes in with the warn
weather, as a rule. The trouble is
to find a first-class photographer
willing to do the deed. To “ take ”
a spinster mid nodings on is notori-
ously bad business, because she is
apt to recover her modesty, such as
it is, and evermore avoid the studio
where she made an object of herself.
This explains why a politician’s
shapely daughter has been pleading
in vain at more than one establish-
ment for permission to expose her
nakedness to the camera—not to
mention the blushing operator in
charge. It is a pathetic situation,
that of a woman begging a com-
parative stranger to gaze upon her
beauty unadorned, and being told to
take her improper suggestion to the
next shop.
A few days ago a Melb. dentist was
approached by a very pretty, well-educated girl
who asked him to separate two front teeth of her
upper jaw, so as to leave a space of about one-
twentieth of an ipch between them. He tossed
back beauty’s head, got the “ separator ” in posi-
tion and screwed it up. “ But there’s nothing the
matter with your teeth,” he explained, jamming a
mirror against the roof of her mouth. “ Matter !
Gf course not,” retorted the girl, “ but it’s very
lucky. I know a girl who had it done about three
months ago and she’s going to marry a man with
■£3ooo a-year next week.” The man of forceps re-
monstrated for some minutes, but as the girl was
determined and threatened to give someone else
the job, he did the work, took a guinea, and
watched his patient travel down the steps looking
both ways at once for the man with £'3ooo a-vear

* * * *
One moral Brisbane might have drawn
from its late accident, but didn’t—that it really is
the slowest town. For three years people have
risked their lives on a rotten structure in a state
of chronic collapse, and have another year of this
joy ahead. And when the new structure is
finished it is certain to collapse in exactly the
same way as the old one. But the property-
owners at both ends of the bridge won’t hear of it
being shifted to an intelligent site a little lower
down. Great is Proputty !

The Pearl ferry disaster at Brisbane was a
big windfall for the local pulpits—the sucking
theologians among them beat the subject thin as
goldleaf. Strange how they gather like vultures
to the feast.

his umbrella and bag in hand. Didn’t want to

Half the inhabitants of Brisbane, it ap-
pears, were either just about to step on the ill-
fated ferry-boat which sunk in the flooded river,
or knew people who were about to step on, if a
variety of apparently trivial accidents had not
been interposed by a Providence who knew the
world and their wives couldn’t spare them just
then. The human liar is always at his best after
a great catastrophe, and the human itch to prove
some connection, however slight, with the event of
the hour, is apparently one of the last instincts

Perhaps the best joke of the Lormer
Board enquiry was elicited by old Bird,
from a free-and-easy female witness. Bird was
anxious to show the puritv of his friendship for
ex-barmaid Fleming. “ Didn’t 1 try to lead her
to the path of virtue ? ” lie asked. “ Yes,” said
the grinn'ng witnesi, “ Yes, you did—in Richmond
paddock.” N jvv, Riciimond paddock is not exactly
the nlace one would select for a small spiritual
meeting.
* * * *
The Social Purity Branch of Yic. Presby-
terian Assembly the other day sent to the Chief
Commissioner of Police (Vic.) several foolscap
sheets complaining of the immoral tone of “The
Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith.” The pious censor is
wroth because none of the critics called attention
to the “wickedness” of the play. As a conse-
quence of this complaint, Constable Muldoon, the
first policeman to hand, say, was detailed to
closely watch the piece, and immediately arrest
for inspection all improper passages. Upon this
functionary’s report the official reply to the snuffle-
buster’s complaint will be based.
* * * *
The love of woman for the glory and glitter
of brass-bound militarism, and her abject worship
of a fighting uniform, is perhaps necessary for
race preservation and the survival of the best.
The advanced woman goes one better. Daybreak,
the Wellington (M.L.) women’s paper, advocates
the formation of a class to instruct girls over 16
in the use of firearms. Which is not so much the
outcome of a feminine desire to face the Maxims
as another reach-out for the same weary old
Trousers.
* * * *
The proposal that Sydney police shall wear
india-rubber or similarly soundless soles is again
to the front. But the evil which calls for increased
protection is to be met by a numerical increase of
the police force, not by endowing an insufficient
force with extraordinary powers. The arming of
Sydney police with revolvers gave individuals, to
be used at their own private discretion, an execu-
tive power which only a Supreme Court could pre
,^?, Uf!i ]y claim. The silent soles are another corn
m T . same direction. At the wildest
,l ~. dack the Ripper panic in London the
authorities there rejected proposals for silent
soles. Why must Sydney ape Draco ?

Melbourne’s most recent skull-mystery has
had a prosaic ending. The skull, found in the
stock at a local bone-mill, was traeed to the dust-
bin of -a dentist, whose mother had thrown it there
because she had no liking for anatomical bric-a-
brac in the drawing-room. The skull was that of
an aboriginal woman, and was found at Mortlake
years ago; and the thrilling fracture (knitted
without the aid of a surgical operation) was pro-
bably nothing more than the result of a tap from
her loving husband’s waddy. The new woman
should secure this relic as an evidence of the
manner in which the tyrant man treated the
revolutionary gin of other days.
A small boy, who ‘ ‘ did not appear more
than 10 years old,” was stood on a gin-case in the
dock at Melb. Criminal Court, the other day, and
solemnly tried for having indecently assaulted a
little girl. The monster pleaded guilty, and has been
sent to the “ Deformatory ” school. The fact that
he is not to be hanged is accepted with delight by
The Bulletin as an evidence of the humanitarian
influence of its gospel.

S. : “ There are three marriages in ‘The
Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith,’ all ghastly failures.
Of the married six mentioned, four are in purga-
tory and two are in hell. The two are to be con-
gratulated. ‘Unreal,’ ‘exaggeration,’ say the
daily critics. I don’t know, but there were six
married men in an establishment with which I
was associated, a couple of years back, and five of
the six were living miserably with their wives.
The sixth was living happily—with another man’s
. wife. lam still a bachelor. ”

Apropos, it is rumored that one Melb.
paper, issued every day, meditates engaging an
unsalaried staff of boys anxious to learn reporting.
As was explained to an ex-member of the staff, it
was absurd to pay reporters when you can get
them for nothing.

At the next N.S.W. final law-examinations
for the admission of solicitors the following ques-
tions will be on the paper :
1. Explain what is meant by the expression “ In re
, a gent, one,” &c.
2. Define the law of conspiracy so as to show that it is
asl'egally wrong to husband a secret as to father a lie.
3. State shortly the principal sections of the statute
which give the right of audience to a Chief Justice.
4. Explain the value of a Judge’s eyes in criminal law,
and give cases to show that trial by the spirits is better
than trial by jury.
5. How far do the laws of Japan relate to an Attorney-
General’s salary ? ,
6. What is vacation? And can you show how much
the country pays in salaries during its continuance ?
7. What is a legal conscience ? And show the extent of
its elasticity in regard to trust-funds.

“ Nat” Kinsman’s marriages are continu-
ally turning up in Melb. divorce-court. “Why
did you go to Kinsman?” Chief Justice Madden
asked a recent female petitioner. “ Because my
sisters were married by him.” “ Are they
divorced ? ” “No.” “Ah! lam glad to hear
there are some exceptions.” The lawyers should
be the last to carp at Kinsman—it all makes work.
Before the recent passing, by
N.S.W. Education Dept., of a regu-
lation requiring school-inspectors to
give notice to teachers of intending
visits of examination, the inspectors
dearly loved to turn up unexpectedly
in the hope of catching the teachers
napping. At a Sydney school where
this happened awhile ago, a little
girl was post6d off by one teacher,
who* had seen* the inspector coming,
to inform the others of his presence.
After the manner of some children
when bearing a message, the little
girl tripped along gaily singing aloud
so as not to forget : “ Go—and—
ten_Mr. Blank - that —the—inspec-
|dr—is—here. Tell him—to—get—
the—registry-books posted up
and —see that—the—teachers —are
—all—on—duty. ” This she repeated
over and over again. “ Here, what’s
that ! ” exclaimed the inspector, as
the guileless innocent skipped past
liiiii proclaiming her message of
peace. “ Come here ; now just you
go back, like a good little girl, to
Miss , and tell her that 1 U
deliver that message for her ! ” And
lie did, too.

One custom of the Melbourne
County Court Judges is a source of
constant delight to all concerned
Every day about 12.30 a Couit
offi -er takes to the Bench the menu
of the adjacent feedery, and hands
it up to the Judge, no matter what;
is on hand. All business is suspended
while his Honor makes his selection ;
barristers or witnesses in full cry
have either to continue their ad-
dresses to the back of the card, or
shutdown and cufse inwardly until
the “ Yes, Mr. you said, which
notifies them of the-permission to
proceed.

Demeanour again ! That master of deport-
ment, Judge Simpson, in the case of an Arab
charged with stabbing a Zanzibari, left the matter
entirely to the jury, because, although he had
carefully watched the witnesses on both sides, it
was “ impossible to deduce anything from their
demeanour.” Sound sense ! recommended to the
consideration of that oth >r N.S.W. Judge who
“ knew the witness was lying becaugg he shuffled
his feet.”

Letter* received by a Sydney governesses’
agency :
Dear Sir,—The lady I want as governess to my little
girl (13) must be able to teach English, French, music,
drawing, and needlework, and assist in dll household
duties, except washing and ironing. We keep a woman
servant. On Monday the governess washes up w ile the
woman is busy in the laundry, and she sees to the vege-
tables while they are boiling; on Tuesday helps the
woman to fold the sheets and tablecloths ; on Wednesday
the governess airs, sorts, and puts away the clothes ; she
does all the mending (we are only four in family), darns
the socks and stockings, patches the trousers, and
does all repairs. She sees to the table if anything is mis-
sing on it, and clears away; gets afternoon-tea for visitors
If we have anyone staying in the house she keeps the
spare room tidy. Of course, the servant empties the slops
in the morning. The governess make the bed in the spare
room. On Saturdays the governess sweeps and dusts the
drawingroom and her own room, and, of course, makes
her own bed every day. (Of course !) In addition, she
also helps me clean the silver. She cuts the bread for the
table, goes to the store for jam or anything the woman
requires in the kitchen, and does all little duties such as I
hav-e mentioned. I forgot to say she sets the breakfast-
things at night, as we breakfast at seven every morning.
Please let me know salary required. 1 engage for six
months, but if* we find we eannot live comfortably together
a iortnight’s notice to terminate engagement. I will pay
railway-fare one way.—Yours, &c. —■ —. P*.S. —I forgot to
mention that she must belong to the Church of England,
and not be over 30 years of age. I should like to see her
handwriting before engaging the lady.

An Adelaide philatelist, when recently
buying stamps at the central office, noticed that a
sheet of-240 half-penny stamps was’only semi
perforated. He promptly bought the Whole sheet,
and has been since retailing them’ at 7s. 6d. a
pair, with a profit of over £4O. What cranks !

Dear Bulletin, I wish to reply to
“ Heretic ” and other “ Sex-problem ” writers. Is
there any sex-problem ? If there isn’t, what’s the
good of saying there is? If there is, it can’t be
helped, and what’s the good of talking about it?
Besides, there isn’t any sex-problem.—Yours,
Titus Salt.
NEW LIES FOR OLD.
The big-tree lie is heard no more,
The snake lie’s day is done,
The lies of giant turnips or
Great ‘ ‘ goose eggs ” are out-run ;
The rifle lie’s exploded, and
The fish lie’s very dead.
To-day we hear on every hand
The cycling lie instead.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will bn taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.
Rene: Bulletin has no occasion to tell lies
about its circulation, the extent of which is obvious to
anyone who keeps his eyes open while travelling in any
part of Australasia. If The Bulletin- said its circulation
was a million, there are at least two other weeklies which
would at once claim a circulation of two millions. The
Bulletin isn’t a competitive liar Vera: We care
nothing for your dubious ancestors ; but for Heaven’s
sake don’t mix your antecedents The Goth : So far as
poetry goes, you are a Vandal and a Hun into the bargain.
B. : Not quite up to standard, though the close is
rather nice :
Dear lad, through you I’m happy, as are they
Who, shut for hours ’mid city dust and heat,
Toward evening seek a home beside the sea,
And feel, blown cool across the opening bay,
Changing to joyous pace their lagging feet,
A fresh north-easter—Such you are to me.

One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*
Ada Crossley, of whom an excellent por-
trait by Elliott and Fry is printed on page 11, has got
thm since her arrival in London—not altogether a
disadvantage to one to whom stoutness means
coarseness. She has made many friends, and may
be fairly considered both a professional and private
success, writes a London correspondent to The
Bulletin. By a lucky speculation in Great
Boulders she cleared .£lBoo—no more and no less.
Berhaps this accounts for her being one of the
most stylishly-dressed artists on the concert plat-
form. Ada always had a firm, solid-looking figure,
f, n( * 't makes an Australian swell with pride to see
the calm assurance with which she “ comes on”to
an audience of two thousand. During her recent
provincial tour with Santley and other leading
vocalists the newspaper criticisms were extrava-
gantrn their notices of her abilities and future
possibilities. The Sheffield papers spoke of her
ff.the English contralto.” I, however,
think that “throwing the boomerang,” as Clara
Butt is a long way the Australienne’s superior.
Hearing both at a Saturday “morning” (com-
mences 3 p.m.) concert last week considerably
strengthens my opinion. Our singer, though, was
not m her best form.

Sydney, February 25, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda —
It is becoming patent that some of the
v.omanaged tea-rooms of Sydney are working out
t heir own destruction through the ultra Yere de
Vcreness of the society-girls-c«m-waitresses who
condescend to “ yank ” buns for a livelihood. It
is altogether too funny. A waitress is a waitress
even if everybody knows who her pa was, and if
she is a girl of spirit the advantages she has in
natural or acquired refinement over the common
or garden sort should make her services so much
more suave and noiseless —she should hand yoi
the menu as if it was a bunch of flowers and se
the table with the graceful wrist-action she was
wont to observe at the piano. Also, her practisec
society smile should not desert her and merge into
the tired frown of the average waitress because
she now carries a tea-tray in place of a fan. But,
after all, her scowl doesn’t blight and destroy the
business so much as her unnatural desire to snub
and score off the customers. I object to no woman
blighting and withering a youth with a larky eye
or a tendency to flirtation in bold relief —if she
conscientiously objects to either the one or the
other ; but it is quite another thing to jump on to
a harmless citizen passing up his plate for more
scone and thinking hard about his wife all the
time. The other day a youth was obliged to
remonstrate with a more than ordinarily saucy
society waitress. His expostulation was mildly
put: “Surely you can be civil even if you are a
lady ? ”
After all, the aristocracy who deal out the buns
in front, and enjoy the privilege of taking your
meek orders in at least one fashionable tea-room,
should be comforted when they reflect on the
ballet rules which obtain in the establishment they
adorn—l mean the rules by which an all-wise
directress puts her nicest girls on to the tables
while the semi-rococo, spectacled, and fully-blown
are kept dark on the weather-bow, washing-up
and counting the cutlery. The lady-waitresses
should find real crumbs of comfort in that. The
mere fact of being allowed to hand the pickles in
the establishments I am talking about confers a
certificate of youth and comparative beauty,
which many a gilded fright could never attain.
The reduced lady serving tarts to ugly Miss Kil-
mansegg may console herself with the reflection
that, if circumstances razed Miss K. to the ground,
pecuniarily speaking, she could never hope to hand
tarts too, on the face of her.
Apropos tea-rooms, there is one I wot of that
is frontally approached by a sort of Jacob’s ladder,
up and down which the angels go in search of light
refreshment, but at the same time the really wise
woman with one petticoat on (as has been the
sensible custom during a record summer) prefers
making her entrance by the back way, and I never
knew why till one bright day recently when I got
the women on these steps between the sun and me.
Then I saw Society as the seed in a peeled grape,
or Hilda Spong when she played Titania with the
light behind her.
How much more prone to comfort men are in
their dressing as compared with women ! You
quickly find this out when you order a tailor to
build you anything; —it is especially brought home
to you when you discover the easy and convenient
place in which Snip has put your pocket. Dress-
makers are particularly trying over pockets.
Women pretend to men they know where to find
them (I mean the pockets, not the men), but that’s
only a piece of fine old crusted hypocrisy, for it
gives a woman as much trouble to find her pouch
as it does any married man smothering after it in
his wife’s wardrobe. It is a weird experience for
a woman to have a tailor make for her. The
difference between the ninth part of a man and
the modiste lies in two significant questions. She
asks, “Is it tight enough?” He wants to know,
“ Is it comfortable ? ”
Mr. Harry Hyndes—the Sydney society musician
who went to Europe in the green ear and was
eager to perform, and did, before a gathering of
eminent old world maestros before he had even
commenced his studies abroad; but who came
back the same way five years later with the com-
plete musical education he now possesses, and
could not be prevailed upon to strike a note where
once he had performed fortissimo—Mr. Hyndes
purposes issuing a waltz. It is to be called the
“ Mindaribba ” (the native name of the composer’s
birthplace, Maitland), and is to be dedicated to
Gov. Hampden, who may take some pleasure in
this dedication, for the waltz has a soft, sweet
melody set in a sort of musical filagree through
which the beat is always clearly defined. It is
presently to be scored for full band, and will, if I
am any sort of a judge, be one of the pet dance-
tunes of the coming season.
The innocent-looking little Mark Hamboure,
who is safely back on the Continent, thank good-
ness, played a well-deserved trick on a certain
hysterical woman who ohased him from pillar to
post while he was in England last year, and
made his life a burden by threatening to throw
herself over the cliffs for love of him. This lady
seized on Mark and forced him to write his auto-
graph in her birthday-book (a volume no wise
woman of her years ever insists on possessing, by
the way). Mark wrote. But his name wasn’t
enough for the lady, who insisted on a sentiment.
Then Mark lashed out with both heels: “You old
cat! ”he addended in Russian, and the cream of
the joke is that the lady (so writes a London
friend of mine) proudly snows the uncomplimen-
tary criticism to an admiring West End circle in
dense ignorance of its meaning !
The new governor of Bananaland, Lord Lam-
ington, for whom Brisbane is preparing the in-
variable “ prodigious welcome ” of two or three
wavering lines of militia, self-advertising official-
dom in jobbed vehicles, and the great unwashed,
strikes me as being “a somewhat different ” Gov.
For instance, I have not definitely heard that His
Ex. has been alluding to those silken bonds, and,
what is more unusual, it is cabled that a large party
of people saw the northern vice-royalties off and
cheered their departure. Now, as a general thing,
the cheering—ironical or otherwise —is generally
confined to the Australian side of the bargain.
People often remark upon the plain, unvarnished
departures some Governors made from their native
heath—a mere grabbing of a valise and a bolt for
the train. There is frequently a great contrast
between the simplicity of their exits and the circus
parade of their entrances. Gov. Lamington is
the first potentate I mind me of, who, apparently,
means to have a circus at both ends, and I think
northern Society should see in the phenomenon an
augury of general joy. After all, a mob of people
shaking their hats at him, and a long wake of
dead-marines, is the best certificate of merit a
Gov. can have from a Sassiety standpoint.
Mrs. Campbell Praed, in her new Australian
novel, “Mrs. Tregaskiss,” introduces one Cyril
Chance, a miserly millionaire (he is not accounted
miserly by the few who really know him), who is
a real personage very thinly disguised—so thinly
that it seems almost a personal outrage on Aus-
tralia’s wealthiest misogynist when Mrs. P. brings
Cyril Chance up to the mark with half-a-dozen
bottles of beer for the heroine, which he presents
to her, sitting in her drawing-room meanwhile like
any tame cat of a parson. One loses confidence in
the authoress at that juncture. The real Cyrus
would sooner go to the burning stake than into a
woman’s presence. It is one of the funniest stories
Lady Carington has to tell—how she tried to
scheme an introduction to the hardened woman-
hater of so many shekels, but never succeeded.
I am told that during the recent heavy rains on
the Darling it was amusing to see, in one tovyn,
the good old fashion revived of going to the post-
office without boots or socks, and with pants
hauled up to the knee well out of the mud. Every-
body, I hear, was “Trilbvfied” from the banker
downwards, and really tnere’s sound economy in
the notion—l only feel anxious to know how the
ladies managed. In former days on the Darling
it was the caper when entertaining to have a room
fitted up so that the gentlemen on arrival might
wash their feet and put on their boots before join-
ing the ladies ; what the ladies did remains a
mystery. And yet Max O’Rell says there is no
fun in the Australian bush !
The Royal Comic Op era Co. gave a very Mon-
dayish performance of “ Gondoliers ” at Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday, for the bone-melting,
marrow-reaching February heat of Sydney had
got on new-ehum Courtice Pounds’s nerves and
given him a frog in the gullet, and otherwise
incapacitated him from playing and singing
Marco as Mr. Pounds mummed and warbled the
picturesque Gondolier on his debut at Melbourne
Princess’s last November. With that novel and
artistic performance still in my mind’s eye I really
cannot take Mr. Pounds’s Saturday interpretation
seriously. I can t help thinking that if some-
body had slipped a piece of ice down the woefully-
perspiring tenor’s back we would have had a
totally different performance. His worst effort
was “ Take a pair of sparkling eyes,” in which the
action did not always suit the word ; “ Take a
tiny little cot,” warbled Mr. P., holding his hand
out meanwhile about as high as a self-respecting
dog’s kennel. But perhaps, after all, he was
thinking of accommodation for tiny little Flora
Graupner.
Wallace Brownlow (who, I hear, has taken to a
bicycle) made his reappearance this season in his
old part as the other Gondolier, with his large voice
in apple-pie order and the naughty little twinkle
in his eye as good as new. Mr. B. threw more
comic biz. into the part than has ever been done
before, but at least one laugh he got was unre-
hearsed. The moral is—never sit down in dark
clothes up-stage without carefully dusting either
your perch beforehand or your garments after-
wards. Florence Young reappeared as Casilda,
and the melodiousness of her fine voice and the
crispness of her acting were among the pleasantest
things in the show. It must be rather difficult
to appear as fresh as a daisy in a stale role—some-
thing very like the hard work any woman has try-
ing to feel brisk in an old frock. Flo Graupner
was the Gianetta who had to go on tip-toe to reach
up and let Howard Vernon kiss her hand (new
biz), and whose baby faces of distress were capital;
and Nina Osborne (strongly reminiscent of sister
Ida) as Tessa didn’t do so badly ; but she might
have glossed her shoes. However, it’s too late
now, for “ Nitouche ” goes up to-night (Thursday).

Major-General and Mrs. Hutton are leaving
N.S. Wales, and the community, from all 1 can
learn, has deep reason for regretting their depar-
ture. There has been in Sydney for many a year
no such liberal and tactful hostess as Mrs . Hutton,
and to the General is due the credit of having
abolished whisky-and-soda “ and petticoats as
controlling forces in local militarism. So far as I
can see, General Hutton has no enemies, excep
people who dislike professional enthusiasm, ath-
letic habits, and hard work; and he leaves behind
him the reputation of having loved work for its
own sake and never having imposed upon anyone
a burden more heavy than the one he was bearing
himself.
A friend writing from London tells me that
throughout the winter all the smart women, in-
cluding the princesses, have donned high-necked
black gowns for the theatre. Bare shoulders, or
even enveloping opera-wraps, are hardly ever seen
in the boxes now on chilly nights. Soft ruffs of
daintily-colored chiffon relieve the sombreness of
the attire. The fashion is said to have been set by
Maude of Wales, who has always suffered more or
less from bronchial weakness.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Dion E. Boucicault.
Dion E. Boucicault was born 23rd May, ’59, in
New York. His father, the world-famous actor-
author, was descended partly from an Irish and
partly from a French family. His father’s father
was a Dublin merchant; his father’s mother,
prior to marriage, a Miss Darley; a lineal
descendant of the same Darley family is the pre-
sent N.S.W. Chief Justice. In childhood “ Dot ’
had a French nurse, and learnt French and Eng-
lish in a home where both languages were spoken.
As a youth he spent much time in Paris. So,
although nothing more natural than his French-
man in “ The Amazons ” was ever seen upon the
stage, yet nothing could have come more naturally
to “ Dot.”
* * * *
Boucicault’s first tutor was the Rev. Charles
Carlos Clarke, called “the Sporting Parson,” a
writer of forgotten novels. Later he was schooled
at St. George’s Hall, London, and when 15, at the
midsummer breaking-up, he played in one of
Moliere’s comedies. Next time he was Hamlet.
His father meant him for the army, and he passed
the necessary exams, and served liis term of train-
ing with the Royal London Militia—now styled
the Fourth Battalion of Grenadier Guards.
But a ruling instinct, fostered by the associations
of childhood and youth, caused him, against his
father’s wish, to forsake the army for the stage.
And now he says : “ Perhaps I would have done
better to have stuck to the army. ”
In ’BO, at Booth’s Theatre, New York, under his
father’s management, Boucicault made his first
professional appearance as the Dauphin in “ Louis
XI.” Then he went off the boards for many
months, but reappeared with Laurence Barrett in
a repertoire of Shakesperian plays. He played
Osrip in “ Hamlet,” Lorenzo m “ The Merchant
of Venice, ” and often the four characters of Osric,
Guildenstern, Francisco and the Priest in one
night. His next change was to the Court Theatre,
London, then under the management of John
Clayton. Here he spent 12 months playing in
“The Parvenu,” “Comrades,” and similar pro-
ductions; and wrote and staged his first plays,
“ My Little Girl,” a one-act piece, and a four-act
romantic drama, “Devotion.” Here, too, he
brushed shoulders with such talent as Kendall,
Arthur Cecil, Forbes Robertson, Anson, Ellen
Terry, Cavendish and Lottie Venne.
* * * *
Next under engagement to Hare and Kendall,
at London St. appeared in a season of
comedy., Then back to his father in America, at
Wallack s Theatre, where he took the title-role in
Ihe Omadhaun a play which the elder Bouci-
cault wrote specially for the younger. After this
he went as manager with his father’s co. on a 12
months tour through America, playing in “The
Shaughraun” “Arrah-na-Pogue, v ’and “The Col-
leen Bawn. In ’B5, with his father and sister, he
came to Australia. And, since he went into part-
nership with Robert Brough, he has produced here
over 90 plays-all the best work for the past nine
years of the leading English dramatists, as well as
such standard plays as “ The School for Scandal ”
Money, and ‘ Much Ado About Nothing ”
Tins record is unequaUed in the short history of
the Australian stage. J
* * * *

* * * *
‘ ‘ What is the drama ? I take it to be a record
a reflex, of the thoughts and feelings of the period
—a ‘ holding of the mirror up to nature,’ to quote
the immortal phrase of the greatest of all drama-
tists. And is it not patent to the most casual
observer that to-day we are discussing and debat-
ing subjects, and thinking and writing about
phases of human nature, which previously were
never so discussed and treated ? What is the use
of writing down the problem play—of saying there
is no demand for it, when the Brough-Boucicault
Co. ’s receipts for ‘ Tanqueray ’ and ‘ Ebbsmith ’
prove them among the most successful of our pro-
ductions ? The problem play is very much alive,
and will remain alive while intelligent, earnest
audiences look upon it with pure emotions and
grasp the teaching it affords.
* * * *
“ I have a deep admiration for Pinero, with
whom I regularly correspond. I think that ‘Thf
Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith ’ is his masterpiece
from a literary standpoint, just as ‘ The Second
Mrs. Tanqueray ’ is from a theatrical standpoint.
And in writing these two plays he has done what
very few men have ever done—that is, achieved
an enormous success directly after achieving
another enormous success.
* * * *
“ The study of a new part, a new character, sets
me conjuring up in my mind the peculiarities and
idiosyncrasies of the class which the dramatist’s
type denotes. lam naturally a man of detail and
observation. In my walks and travels, at the
hotel or in society, I see a trick of the hand, a
manner of walking, of speaking, of looking, which
I reproduce afterwards in its appropriate type.
Coming home from the theatre, after the night’s
performance, I have heard from the shade of a
building near a club the cynical laugh of the
Duke of St. Olpherts. At a fashionable gathering
I have caught his limp. A year after or before I
have seen his eves regarding a fashionable beauty.
And from a photograph I have learnt his facial
expression and much of his make-up. I have
never found a type complete in one man ; one has
to select—here a little, there a little—just as a
sculptor or painter uses one model for the hand,
another for the arm, a third for the shoulder.’
Every character I have played in Australia has been
a combination of people I have met and studied.
And I value detail and observation because, in my
opinion, they and they only enable an actor to
hope for the greatest virtue of playing—the
player’s ideal—versatility. The more thorough
the detail, the more careful the observation, then
the more does an actor become an actor among
actors. For by what other methods can he manage
to sink his own identity, and so thoroughly im-
personate a character widely different from his
own, as to produce an illusion upon the minds of
his audience ? My goal is reached when those
who know me best say to themselves, on my first
entrance, ‘ How unlike Boucicault that is! ’ and
then immediately forget that it is Boucicault at all.
* * * *
“Itis as easy—and as difficult—to play a good
part as it is to tell a good story. By the
way, I think George Meredith the greatest of
living novelists. The parts I prefer are an old
man on the stage and a young one off it; but now
37 years are whispering that my young days are
going, if not gone. .
* * « •
“ The 19th of June sees the end of my partner-
ship with Mr. Brough. So far I have no definitely
fixed plans. Shall I return ? Well, we are all
wanderers, you know, and it is more than likely
that at some future date I shall visit Australia
again. I don’t wish to think I shall never return,
for I have made many friends here whose memory
will remain in my heart, locked up under my own
life’s key. Though lam cosmopolitan— at home
everywhere—l should not like to say to Australia
—Farewell! ”

The greatest feat accomplished by Melb.
reporter Stark, lately deceased, was in asking
Capt. Tyler, A.D.C. to the Governor of the day,
whether he had married His Excellency’s daughter.
B. L. Farjeon once wrote that “the hair
and eyes of the daughters of Auckland were the
fullest possible realisation of the dreams of poets.”
And then he went and married an American girl.

out of public advertisement that, probably, 95 out
of every 100 in Australia would be unable to name,
without hard thinking, the present M.L. figure-
head.

Kendall was apprenticed to and served his time
with the Sydney “ lolly ’’-making firm of Biddell
Bros. The poet’s mother was a pensioner of the
firm up to the time of her death.

Mr. William Davies,
who is “ milking ” snakes in
the accompanying photo-
gram under the watchful
eye of Demonstrator Price,
of Melb. University Physio-
logical Department, cares
less for the bite of a tiger-
snake than the average
Australian does for a mos-
quito bite. He will stick
his head and both arms into
a 4ft. by 2ft. cage containing
forty tiger snakes and,
knocking out of the way
those he does not require,
pick out any he wants with-
out a moment’s hesitation.
“ Milking ” is the extraction
of venom by making the
snake bite on the edge of an
ordinary watch-glass. The
venom is then dried and
solutions of known strength
made as required for experi-
mental purposes.

G. R. Dibbs, when supercargo of a vessel
trading in the Dutch Settlements, once saved the
life of the famous Bully Hayes, having found the
pirate ill of cholera and abandoned by his servants.
Dibbs stuck to him, and they became excellent
friends. This was his earliest preparation for
political life.

Matthew Burnett, the temperance-evan-
gelist, a man in earnest, whose dark eyes and loud,
hoarse voice are remembered by many in Aus-
tralia, has just died in England. His last days,
many will regret to hear, were embittered by
poverty. When here his fervid enthusiasm
was evidently burning him out. He probably suc-
cumbed to consumption. Close upon 30 years ago
Burnett was a terror to the bung industry
throughout Australia, and (temporarily) extricated
“JIM" STANBURY, Champion Sculler.
Photo, by Ker.
more men from the grip of the Great Australian
Thirst than any oilier evangelist and advocate of
the crystal spring. It was Burnett who undertook
a temperance crusade amongst a tribe of blacks
camped in one of old Jock Winter’s paddocks
near Ballarat. After the third time of asking, on
the conclusion of a most exhausting peroration,
the evangelist, who stood mopping his fevered
brow, was approached by the royal Jacky-Jacky,
who innocently offered a billy of beer, remarking :
“Mine tink it plurry hot. Big yabber-yabber
you, my word. Drink ! ”
Mrs. Besant’s lecturing tour in India is as
successful as have been the previous ones in that
country of many sects and religions. Five
crowded lectures in Calcutta included one for the
Anti-Vivisection Society, of which Mrs. Besant
was at one time such an ardent and strong sup-
porter. The visit to India terminates A.pril 4th,
and we are not likely to see the high priestess of
Theosophy in Australia before the end of this year,
if then. Interest in Theosophy seems to have
almost died out in Melbourne and Sydney since
the J udge affair was arranged ; and Herbert Bur-
rows’ attempt at a fresh disorganisation appears
to have fizzled. 1

There was one important subject, and one
only, on which chairman J. P. Garvan neglected
to speak this year in his great annual oration as
president of the Citizens’ Life Assurance Co.
Incredible as it may seem, he omitted all reference
to the dispute concerning the existence or other-
wise of canals in the planet Mars.
Victorian : “ Premier Turner’s resignation
is again mooted, on the imperative score of health,
and it is certain he will not hold office long. lie
never panted for it like Shiels. Turner sensibly
perceives it is not open to him to accomplish much
more, having done things that are almost great ;
and partner Sam Lyons keeps nagging, while
both of them are off-color in health.
Few men who knew him well do not res-
pect plucky old C. B. Fisher, the erstwhile S.A.
millionaire. Although he was down, he, not long
before he filed, actually took the many gold and
silver medals awarded him at cattle and sheep
shows, &c., and had them melted, and, with
the proceeds, paid every soul 20s. in the £ to
whom he owed money for clothing or food.
Letter by last English mail: —
Editor Bulletin. Dear Sir,—My attention has just
been called to the statement in your paper that the
Sketch has published a story hy Mr. Louis Becke, entitled
“ Kennedy the Boat-Steerer,” without acknowledgment.
I beg to inform you that this story was duly purchased
through Mr. Becke’s London publishers for publication in
the Sketch, and 1 shall be glad, therefore, to receive an
apology in your columns for the imputation involved.
The Sketch has so large a sale in Australia that, had 1
been aware that the story was appearing, or had appeared,
in the Bulletin, I should not have accepted it. —
I am, dear sir, yours truly, Clement Shorter, editor of
the Sketch, London. Jan, 15, 1896.
It is not The Bulletin, but publisher Fisher
Unwin who, assuming that the facts are exactly
as stated by Mr. Shorter, owes the Sketch an
apology. “ Kennedy the Boat-steerer ” (like other
Bulletin stories by Becke which have appeared
unacknowledged in the Sketch) was printed in
this paper at least two years ago. And for some
reason or other, Mr. Fisher Unwin has omitted to
acknowledge in the second volume of Becke’s
stories (it has not yet reached the Australian
trade) that most of the tales therein contained
originally appeared, like 12 out of 14 in “ By
Reef and Palm,” in The Bulletin.
“Jim” Stanbury, the
N.S.W. champion sculler, is
a grandly-proportioned man,
6ft. high, 13st. weight, 43in.
round the chest. Born on
the Hawkesbury, he grew up
partly there, partly on the
Shoalhaven. He is still un-
mistakeably a farmer ; and
looks younger than his 28
years. “Jim’’has seen the
great sport of sculling rise
from obscurity to the meri-
dian where a crazed people
worshipped it; then sink
suddenly into obscurity
again. He was barely in his
teens when, 15 years ago,
honest Bill Beach rowed his
first race in Sydney waters
a skiff-race for £lO aside.
Beach refused to sell the race
for <£2o, and on getting into
his boat he saw that one scull
was sawn enough to break
when the tussle should come,
so he changed that scull and
won. Beach won <£2ooo from
Hanlan in stakes; Kemp
<£looo. Beach, the second
time he beat Hanlan, won
£5OO in stakes, £l9OO in
testimonials, and many hun-
dreds in wagers laid him in
100’s to 0. He won £l3OO
in one sweepstake on the
Thames, and altogether
netted quite a fortune. He
paid £7OOO down for his
Dapto farm. Stanbury rowed
his first races at the age of
17, one day on the Shoal-
haven ; he won five races
that day. He was 19 when
he raced Searle, then 21.
They spurted 13 times the
first mile and a half —the bow
of “Jim’s” boat being op-
posite Searle’s ’rigger. Stan-
bury was a bit greener than
Searle, and Searle won;
nathless “he was too fast for
all of us,” says the present
champion. They came back
up the river together after
the race was over, up past the
spot where the broken column
commemorates the untimely death of Searle ; Jim
was fairly right, but Searle’s right forearm was
much swollen and its muscles were twitching in
evidence of the severity of the struggle. Stan-
bury “goes all over” when he’s done ; Haitian
used to go in the knees ; “ when Ned was done his
teeth would be grinning with desperation, and his
knee.-, would wobble every time he came up on the
slide. Stanbury says “it’s all nonsense about
Peter Kemp being nervous; like me, he never
rowed his race beforehand.” Kemp finished Stan-
bury as a sculler. The champion has a word about
pluck—“ after a man’s strength is gone he hasn’t
any.” Kemp is farming now on the Colo, and
M‘Lean, who beat Jim the first time they met, is on
theßichmond. Stanbury says, “M‘Lean never beat
me ; it was indigestion.” Anyhow, he rowed
M‘Lean blind the second race, and beat Sullivan
how he liked in his last race, over three years ago.
He is a oricketer, footballer, swimmer, and boxer
—he broke two knuckles in America on a big man’s
jaw. And he has been on and off a bicycle twice.
While training, his indulgence is sherry— sherry
and biscuit prior to the run before breakfast;
liberal sherry after-the seven miles forenoon row ;
“ water makes a man weak.” Eating—“ anything
good ” suits Stanbury. Recently, he has been
training at Ryde : sculling in the morning and m
the afternoon walking miles and miles in that dis-
trict of picturesque orchards, green slopes, and
wooded hills He has been married three years,
and has one child alive. In going to England to
race Harding, he leaves his wife at home, as Beac h
left his in the golden days of the wager-bout.

DESTRUCTION OF RABBITS BY
HEAT AND DROUGHT-
Tbe Minister for Lands has received a
report from the rabbit inspector at Wiican-
nla for the month ending January 21, in
which that officer states that the excessive 1
heat and the drought have kilted the rab-'
bits off in millions, so that only a few spots
are even moderately infested.

that, in every stage Of its progress, it
encourages pernicious influences. In its
inception, interested tnfdes band them-
selves together for their own ends, and
bring what pressure they can on the
Legislature.
