Another N.S.W. cement - factory the
second—doses shortly. This practically ends one
local industry—killed off in the first two months
or so of Foreign-raid.
Hard lines that it should have been before
one of Sand-Shifting Premier Reid’s (N.S.W.)
Public Works Committees that Under-Secretary
Critchett Walker deposed—“ There ought to be
a poor rate ! ”
Ex-Labor leader, present Cabinet Minis-
ter, Joe Cook (N.S.W.) : “I believe we will see
the Pacific cable laid, which will tend towards the
binding together of the Empire. ” The Empire !
Sir-r-r Joe Cook ! ! !
Not a day passes in Brisbane now without
a wild shriek from the Government organs, led by
the Courier, over the iniquities of the Labor-
party. This is the tune to which all the band will
dance till the elections are over.
In North Adelaide (S.A.) there are now
201 more female than male voters, and at Noar-
lunga the women are in a majority of 127. In
Kingston’s electorate of Adelaide West the sexes
are about equal. There are 77,464 men and 59,066
women voters on the S.A. roll.
Queensland Premier Nelson “ thinks that
Victoria is the only province that has any real
desire for Federation.” If this were true, there
would be a cold prospect for Federation. The
Vic. Leg. Council will see to that.
Even in its dying hour, Boodlewraith can’t
go straight. Not content with holding all the
trumps, it will spring the elections two months
earlier, and disfranchise the 3500 January voters.
Don’t the Govt, crowd just trust the people
especially if they haven’t votes !
The Yic.
Governm en t
sees its way to
save £BOOO a
year by doing
away with the
useless station
at Princes
Bridge, Mel-
bourne, and
concentrating
the traffic a t
Flinders-street.
The Argus
condemns this
cheese-paring
policy, and
wants the Go-
vernment to
take large,
statesmanlike
views, but the
Government
reckons the
country hasn’t
yet paid for
the large views,
it took in the
past, and goes
on looking for
small econo-
mies, cutting
down gradients
making savings
on the oil and
grease bill, and
closing up the
small leakages.
N. S. W. Rail-
wa y Depart-
ment is also
working hard
to remedy the
large views
taken in past
years, and dur-
in g Commis-
sioner Eddy’s
term of office
the expenses
have been re-
duced £lOO,OOO
a year by cut-
ting down gra-
dients alone.
If a railway
goes over 100
miles of flat
country and
then mounts
one steep bank,
the load an en-
gine can take
for the whole
journey is just
the amount it
can drag over
that one bank, and no more. But the old railway-
building fathers of the country never thought of
this.
The Employers’ Liability, Homes Protec-
tion, and Non-compulsory Vaccination Bills, and
Maloney’s proposed bill to restrict security to the
mortgaged property, are evidently, from their
position on the notice paper, to be among the
“ slaughtered innocents ” of this present Vic.
session. The neglect of the first-mentioned will
probably be fraught with dangerous consequences
on account of the almost immediate expiry of the
old act.
■ The N.S.W. Government has decided that,
as Parkes is attempting to re-enter politics, the
proposed >£lo,ooo grant will be stopped. The
grant was to be made on the tacit condition that
the old man kept quiet, and if the old man won’t
keep quiet there is nothing more to be said.
The original N.S.W. Labor - party was
offered two seats on the Public Works Committee,
but passed a self - denying ordinance against
accepting any emolument (other than salary) on
the motion of a gentleman who, by a coincidence,
is now a member of the present Works Com-
mittee.
Some apostles of the “ profitable utilisa-
tion of our vast tropical territory by colored
labor ” already openly talk of a possible acquire-
ment by Queensland of the Northern Territory—
Kanakaland to stand out of the Federation in
order to have a free hand with which to wallop its
own nigger.
Horace Tozer, Queensland Colonial-Secre-
tary : “ Only the other day I had a proposal from
a gentleman who wanted to go to America and
bring out negroes as cheap labor.” If Tozer didn’t
accept the offer it was no doubt only because he
thought he could get cheaper labor somewhere
near at hand.
Before Chief Sec. Gordon (S.A.) resigned,
the National Ass. paper described him as “ one of
Kingston’s automata,” but now it is amusing to
see them picturing Gordon as “ the one man in
the Ministry who possessed any backbone,” “the
most brilliant .... the only independent
Minister,” &c, &c.
There is a side entrance to the Yic.
Speaker’s room, hidden by a curtain, and behind
this curtain may be seen, any day. Mclntyre,
Shiels, and a prominent member of the Argus re-
porting-staff. It is here they manufacture bogus
crises and alleged impending attacks upon the
Turner Government.
Maoriland, the country that is being ruined
by Protection and Socialistic legislation, had a
revenue for the last quarter of ’95 of <£1,216,511
an increase of £311,000 upon the corresponding
quarter of ’94. Also, in the same province, where
men are being driven out wholesale by democratic
government, meddling, and tyranny (vide any
Tory paper any time in the last five years), the
arrivals were last year 895 more than the depar-
tures, and in ’94 they were 2253 more than the
departures. Even the attraction of Westralia
doesn’t seem able to keep people from going
laoriland-wards.
THE EFFICACY OF PRAYER.
PARSON : “ What, leave the church, Mr. Cooley—the church of which you have been
a pillar and an ornament for so many years? ”
MR. COOLEY : “ Yes; I'm done with the lot of you. It's likely I'm going to pay
for the support of a parson who prays for cold weather, while I'm running
an ice factory? ”
One N.S.W. magnate has shunted nearly
every able-bodied supporter in his electorate into
a certain department, and the Civil Service Com-
missioners will now have to get rid of several
dozen of them with compensation. Three or four
of his appointees were people who had been sacked
from other departments for incompetence or worse
offences.
Sargood, M.L.C. (Vic.) : “ The country
looks to the Council to polish bills that were un-
avoidably hurried through in the Assembly. io
“ polish them off ” he meant.
A SOUL-WAIL.
’ARRIET : "He's too bloomin' proud to look at me."
Free passes on the Tas. railways are
abolished at last, the order having gone forth to
stop them utterly. Still, this is only straining at
the gnat if the pernicious franking system by
M’sP. is allowed to continue—including, as it has
sometimes done, the forwarding of linen either to
or from “ the wash.”
Melb. Argus calmly admits that the U.S.
Democratic party —the I reetraders par.excellence,
so it please you—“ has come to utter ruin, through
corruption and mal-administration,” and “ that it
is, owing to its widespread alliance with villains
and humbugs, a complete wreck.” The Argus
has never said anything half so rough of the high-
tariffists.
Never before had Australia a Ministry so
gluttonous of virtue as the Reid crowd. But the
other day Minister Garrard assured the country
that it was a “ God-fearing Ministry ” ; and now
he yells that it is a “Ministry of modest men.”
Yet a large proportion of it has been in office with
Parkes ! Men who have sat with Parkes may
retain their God-fearingness, but their modesty —
never!
. S.A. Tory press industriously circulates
the lie that the workers are dissatisfied with the
Labor M’sP., who have become lazy and impro-
vident, and spend their time drinking at sixpenny
bars and smoking expensive cigars. Such state-
ments are, of course, made for the benefit of the
farmers, who are unaware of the fact that .75 per
cent, of the Labor members are (sad to relate !)
non-smokers and total abstainers.
What sort of men can Victoria expect to
find in Parliament, considering what is expected
of them ? Beazley, after an all-night sitting, went
yawning down to Collingwood recreation grounds,
and assisted his constituents to prepare a bicycle-
track for the afternoon’s sports. The member
who doesn’t adopt this sort of pettifogging policy
isn’t popular in the electorate. Beazley, M.L.A.,
“ worked like a navvy,” it appears.
N.S.W. Government- —which has just
appointed a Board to foster the export trade of
the province, the timber export among the rest—
sends up imported American and Norwegian
timber to build wharves on the Northern rivers,
right in the midst of the best hardwood districts
in the province. What is the use of appointing a
board to push on the export of timber, and urging
the Agent-General to find a market for it in
London, if the Government won’t use it where it
is grown ?
Sydney Teiecraph (on the eve of Waver-
ley election) upon Parkes :
Take his magnum opus, the N.S.W. Education Act.
When the cause of free, secular, and compulsory educa-
tion was on the up-grade and needed a lift, Sir Henry
Parkes was riding comfortably in the car of denomina-
tionalism, and giving the present Act his political dust.
As soon, however, as other men had laboriously dragged
it up to the summit of the hill, he dexterously jumped in
and secured the box-seat. This is what he calls giving
N.S. Wales its present Education Act.
Which only shows what Party Government is. In
the days when Parkes was in office and supported
by the D.T. that paper never thought of telling
the truth about the ‘ ‘ great statesman. ”
During that all-night session, Vic. Assem-
bly was decidedly “fresh.” Several members
“ had drink taken,” and more than one official had
“ a whisky or two more than usual.”
The Argus persist-
ently fights the minimum
wage idea, because it
regards the reform as a
ridiculous and hopeless
attempt “to eliminate
poverty.” Nosaneperson
expects the minimum-
wage project to “ elimin-
ate poverty,” but the
Argus’s position is that
of the individual who re-
fuses a crust to a hungry
man because the world
is full of starving
poor.
On the list of the
Vic. Ass. stands a mo-
tion in the name of
Graham, M.P., requir-
ing a plebiscite of
Victorians on the Bible-
in - State - schools ques-
tion. Churchmen of all
sorts declare this a
splendid motion ; people
who disbelieve in Refer-
endum and initiative
heartily say this motion
is good business. The
fact is, State Scripture
readings are unobtain-
able on ordinary political
lines no candidate
would dare to make it a
prominent point in his
election programme. The
Bible Referendum is,
therefore, the forlorn
hope of the churches.
It can do them no harm,
and the organised church
majority might make a
big enough show at the
ballot-box if the rest of
the populace were suffi-
ciently inert. No wide-
awake democrat would
be taken in by such
tactics— he would stipu-
late for the whole prin-
ciple of the Referendum,
not a part to suit party
purposes.
Thepowerof theN.S. W. daily morning press
writ large in the result of the Waverley election.
Its mightiest weapon is the possession of the Last
Word before the _poll. There are often enough
waverers on election morning to turn a battle;
these are in the gift of the Freetrade editor.
Herein lies the advantage the morning press
has over the evening journal, which comes out
when the heavy polling is mostly over. Protection
has little hope of success without a strong morning
daily, and those interested might as well take the
hint early as wait for several more general elec-.
tions to rub the lesson in.
Vic. Assembly’s division on the Railways
Conference was significant. The Labor comer
voted with the Noes, supported by oddments from
all parts of the House excepting the Treasury
bench. The Government won by 42 votes to 251
with the aid of the direct and indirect Opposition.
The Labor corner itches for a fight with the Coun-
cil, a deliberate set-to that will mean a dissolution
and an appeal to the country against Pat Man.
The Council killed the Land-tax and One-man-one-
vote ; it is “ dealing with ” the Factories Bill ; it
did its best to have the railways run as a capitalis-
tic bureau.
Parkes is justified in scorning Sydney
Herald and D.T. when they assure him that he is
worked-out and unfit for further political service.
Years ago they assaulted him with language as
crushingly emphatic, and ordered him to betake
himself to obscurity without more ado. “Now
that he is in his coffin,” wrote the Telegraph in
those days, “nail the lid down, and let the coun-
try have done with him.” And the Herald cried
“Ditto.” Yet, even a short memory can recall
the fawning servitude of both papers when a
political boil-up threw Parkes on top. In one 24
hours they remodeled their leading columns, and
fulsomely lauded him as “the greatest political
power ” in Australia, and one of the first half-dozen
statesmen of the English-speaking world.
St. James’s Gazette (London), discussing
war possibilities, admits what The Bulletin has
often stated :
Great Britain would be starved out within three or four
months if our foreign food supply were stopped.
Here is an argument damning Freetrade from a
Freetrade paper. And it is given full currency in
Sydney by the London correspondent of the Free-
trade D.T. ! The confession, even in time of
peace, amounts to this : To keep itself safe, and to
be sure of its foreign food supply, Britain must
spend many millions extra on its navy every year.
lor those millions it must tax its people. There-
fore, what it wrong-headedly supposes it gains by
Freetrade it loses over and over in navy-taxes.
And in time of war ? Absolute starvation. Sr.
James’s Gazette suggests a British Imperial
Zollverein ! Which is Protection, and nothing
but Protection.
In Vic. Assembly, the other day, Premier
Turner had the unique privilege of urging the
rejection of his own Railway Bill. His Bill pro-
vided for the Railway Trust recommended by the
Casey Board, but the Assembly rejected it, and
accepted instead a proposal for a single Commis-
sioner. The Council got the amended Bill and
promptly turned it back into the Railway Trust Bill
as drafted by Turner, and returned it to the Assem-
bly. Then Turner moved that the amendments
be disagreed with. Murray Smith asked if the
Government had any substantial existence at all ?
VV as it only a committee to carry out the wishes
of the majority of the House ? And the Govern-
ment answered Yes —if the House wasn’t to rule
what was it there for ? The idea struck Murray
Smith all of a heap, and showed his dazed
condition by exclaiming that he (sitting in the
Opposition corner) had supported all the Govern-
ment measures. Then the House laughed.

The Young Australia Hotel, Tliley-street,
Sydney, kept by Mrs. Riley, which Mrs. Dean is
now proved not to have visited, has been re-named
“ The Sunbeam.” What has Gov. Brassey done
that he should be thus visited with the Dean curse ?
The N.S.W. Solidarity Labor-party, being
last session offered by Premier Reid one seat on the
Public Works Committee, in its turn offered the
position to three members of the party on condition
that one-half the fees should be given up to pay
other members of the party for organising. Leader
M‘Gowan and member Cann refused, on the
ground that if the salary was earned the earner
had a right to get it—a good labor argument. A
third gentleman took it on the caucus terms.
A Melbourne masseur, under cross-ex-
amination in a police court case, admitted
issuing this business card—“ Spiritualism ; circles
for the development of mediums on scientific
principles, as taught by the Mahatmas in the
caves of India in all its branches, after a personal
study of eight years with those masters of psychic
and occult forces, are now being formed by So-and-
So. Manipulation, 10s. per lesson.” One lady
pupil seems to have had £G worth of the mystery.
Joubert is getting on. He has roped in
the Q. Govt, to support his “International Ex
hibition ” at Brisbane in ’O7, and the much-enduring
taxpayer is debited with the cost. Also, the Cocriei
has been nobbled by the crafty device of taking
offices in its “white-elephant” building; and as
the foreign exhibitor who reads that “ the proposal
has been taken up by the people of Queensland
and the adjacent colonies with such general
approval that the necessary capital has been most
readily subscribed,” cannot see the appalling wink
with which sentences like these must be penned,
he will probably fall in.
In future, no obviously consumptive per-
son will be allowed to enter Maoriland ; so far as
that progressive country is concerned phthisics
will be categorised with such undesirables as
lepers and smallpox patients, and any ship in
which they come will have to take them back.
By strictly excluding consumptives it will no
doubt be possible to immensely reduce the ravages
of that awful disease. When one considers that
a consumptive who walks spitting up one side of a
street and down the other can directly and in-
directly poison half a city, it becomes plain that
any community which allows tuberculous patients
to come and go without let or hindrance is less
than civilised.
There are now so many points at issue
between the two Vic. Houses of Parliament that a
rupture may occur at any moment. The Council
has turned the Assembly’s Railway Bill upside-
down, and it has torn the vitals out of the
Factories Bill. The Council is also riled at the
bad reception its Companies Bill received in the
Assembly, and at the open scorn which met the
proposal to elect the Federal delegates by post.
Members of the Assembly growl audibly at the
increasing boldness of the Fat Man Council,
which doomed body reads its Argus, and rejoices
in its own exceeding fatness. Premier Turner is
not the man to encourage a fight between the
Houses, but if the Assembly furnishes the indigna-
tion he will manage the tactics.

New Australia doesn’t believe in freedom
of speech. Writers to the English press from the
-Paraguayan Paradise are threatened with expul-
, they persist in expressing their opinions in
the public press. “ Two months ago (says one of
the scribes), it was decided by 65 to 41 to alter the
constitution to single-tax lines. Last week, 83 to
23 voted against this change. Out of 2500 cattle
only 2Go cows are left—total value of live stock
being about <£looo. The average wage in Para-
guay is eighteen-pence a day.” In conclusion,
this hunk of wisdom: “Communism would be
all right but for the imbecility of certain indi-
viduals.” Yes, all systems shatter against the
meanness, incapacity, and imbecility of (( certain
individuals.”
The Melb. police reckon they have been
badly taken in by a strange woman. Some time
ago a man was run in on the frequent charge of
wife and child desertion, and, as he failed to make
any satisfactory arrangements, he was detained in
gaol. He had been there some time when a weep-
ing- wife came along, and said she had relented
and loved him still, and then she broke down
utterly and slopped her grief all over the J.P. and
other officials, so that they had to empty nearly a
pint of tears out of their boots when she had gone.
She further withdrew all charges against the old
man, and introduced two gentlemen of extensive
property who went bail for him to a considerable
extent, and on the strength of this he was
promptly released. Now it is alleged that the
weeping female wasn’t the wife at all, but a mere
irresponsible stranger, and that the two gentlemen
of property have no assets worth speaking of.
Therefore, the law is boiling mad to think that it
was taken in by so simple a device.

A PHYSICIAN’S WARNING.
Prof. R. A. Gunn, Dean of the New York
Medical College, does not believe in indiscriminate
and general use of any form of mercury—either
calomel or blue pill. Not only on account of the
dangers which often attend their use, but he finds
that all ailments resulting from a bilious condition
are safely and easily cured by using Warner’s Safe
Cure. This remedy is so well known in every
country, and all parts of the world, that every
medicine dealer has it for sale, and full instructions
for use, and also diet to be followed, are found in
a pamphlet which accompanies each bottle. Mr.
W. Stewart, Main Street, Petersburg, S.A.,
writes : “ I think it the duty of every man who
has suffered as I have and been thoroughly cured
by using Warner’s Safe Cure to send a testimonial
to the proprietors that the wonderful cures that
it effects may be made public. My complaint was
enlargement of the liver, which was of years’
standing. I consulted several doctors, but they
only gave me temporary relief. Being in Adelaide
on a trip, I went into one of the largest warehouses
there and during conversation told one of the
members of the firm how bad I was. He sent for
one of Warner’s pamphlets and told me to read it;
and having done so, I immediately got a bottle of
Warner’s Safe Cure, which gave me relief, and
when I got through twelve bottles I was com-
pletely cured. I have recommended it to several
of my friends, and in every case it has had marvel-
lous results, therefore I cannot speak too highly of
Warner’s Safe Remedies, and will recommend
them to every sufferer.”*

Moran, parliamentary representative of the
Coolgardie goldfields, has been talking sense in Adelaide
He claims to represent as many adult males as all the rest
of his colleagues put together. Why rave over the unequal
representation of the Uitlanders on the Rand after that'
He has faith m the permanence of the fields, but admits
that there have been many disappointments. Unfor-
khe capital nominally invested in the
goldfields never gets there. Properties bought for meagre
a!!!! 1 L have J* e ,? u /l oa te f l in England into companies of
£50,000 and £lOO,OOO, most of which has gone into the
pockets of promoters and brokers, only a very small pro-
portion dribbling to the mines ; yet the little sum for
actual mining is expected to earn interest on the big
amount. Exactly; it is by this means all booms are made
and by the same they are burst. Even the W.A. mines
are not rich enough to carry London Fatman’s babies.
* * * *
Boulder main reef, says a paper at Hannan’s:
The mine is being well opened up, and, as is often the
case on the field, the property passed into the possession
of an English 00. before its value was discovered.” How
consoling for the British investor ! And, really, that is
his main chance to find something 1 the owners knew not
of. It was at least partly so with the Great Boulder, the
chief gold-producer of the West; but so as to let several
well-advertised people have all the credit, a fact known
to thousands on the field is never published.
The West Australian Exploring and Finance
Corporation publishes a balance-sheet, which shows
clearly how British capital is opening up Westralia.
The corporation started with a paid-up capital of
£155,000, to buy auriferous land cheap and sell it to
other British companies at 40 times what it cost. It
expended during the year, in office and other ex-
penses in London, £lB,OOO, and the amount spent
in “opening-up” the province seems to have been
under this amount. The profits for the year —to
be distributed in London—are £320,000, equal to a little
over 200 per cent, per annum. As the co. only bought
or leased bits of desert for very little, which was
probably about their correct value, and passed them on to
its own miserable countrymen, Westralia hasn’t lost
much by its operations up to date. Still, considering that
all the cash it has in Australia is only £21,000, and the cash
in London is £252,000, and that it owes £79,000 in London
and only £5O in Australia, and that against the £5O which
the co. owes in this country there is £1049 owing to it, it
would seem that the money it has actually left in Aus-
tralia is pretty nearly nil, and that somehow this con-
tinent is rather in its debt than otherwise. The
noticeable point about the British capital which comes to
Australia is that it doesn’t come. A person with an eye-
glass arrives, and states that he —haw!—-represents
£150,000 cash in London ; but the cash religiously stays in
London, and the gold, if there is any, goes there. That’s
the “ influx ” —or most of it.
* • * *
Premier Forrest’s water-scheme is to build a
reservoir at Mt. Burgess, 10 miles from Coolgardie, and to
pump water into it from the Darling ranges, near Perth.
Public officers are “ mapping it out.” Of course, it would
never do to bring the water from Esperance—that would
be interfering with vested interests. Everything, even
water, must come from Perth. Besides, this scheme can
be carried out in five or six years, and its cost, with a due
regard to economy, should not exceed 10 millions.
* * * *
The day before leaving W.A. for London, Alick
Forrest, who controls the business end of the Premiership,
gave his ultimatum regarding the proposed Esperance Bay
railway :“ It will never be built. The only object of it
would be to divert the trade of the fields to the other
colonies, and that would be impolitic, and unjust at the
same time. No ; what I think will happen will be that
the Govt, will have to carry at specially low rates to the
fields, and that a line will be constructed from Coolgardie
to the Norseman. As for a direct railway from Esperance,
it is not to be thought of.” Norseman to Perth is about
6;>o miles ; to Esperance is about 150. From Esperance to
Perth is about 1000 miles ; so they coolly propose to han-
dicap trade with 1450 miles extra carriage for the benefit of
Perth ! Nine-tenths of the W.A. trade is with the other
1 rovinces, and must be for many years. Tub Bulletin
wagers that Alick will not have to live long to see the
Esperance Bay line built.

Owing to Vic’s new and correct method of
computing its gold output, the figures for '95 dis-
close a startling increase of 66,4060 z. Of course, the
province has not achieved this jump (though M.L. did it
with something to spare), but owes it mainly to a new
method of keeping accounts. The ’94 estimate under-
stated the yield. By applying a reasonable system to the
figures available, the mining department now concludes
that the real increase is 23,1310 z., or about 3’2 per cent.

* * * *
The old question of a buying broker’s liability
for calls on untransferred shares cropped up in Melb.
Supreme Court last week, and was decided against the
purchaser. H. A. Wilcox sold 100 Cyclorama shares at
Is. 6d., per broker Goodall, to brokers Clarke and Co.
Calls were made ; Wilcox’s name remained on the regis-
ter ; Clarke and Co. repudiated liability ; so Wilcox paid
the calls and sued them. Judge Chomley, in the County
Court, found for defendants; but the Supreme Court up-
set this decision, and Clarke and Co. have to pay. The
sale was made in the ordinary way at a Stock Exchange
call, and the Court held that under these circumstances
there was nothing to show whether the Clarkes were agents
or principals, and as the Stock Exchange rules prevented
the selling broker from asking, they must take the responsi-
bility of principals—which is sound sense, as well as pre-
sumably good law. If a broker wants to refuse responsi-
bility, he can do it by expressly declaring that he buys as
agent only.

F. A. Newman, the Melb. jeweller, writes in
contradiction of a recent Wild Cat statement that Queens-
land opals, cut, are worth from £3 to £l2 per carat.
Opals in general use by the trade are retailed to the
public at from 10s. to £1 per carat. The finest quality of
opals, such as are associated with diamonds in brooches,
run a good deal higher in price, but nowhere near £l2
per carat.

Charters Towers compares well with other Aus-
tralian goldfields—old and new. It produces 70,0000 z.
more gold than Bendigo (which comes second); its divs.
being £112,000 more than those of the latter, while
its calls are £37,000 less. Excess of divs over calls in
Charters Towers, for ’95, was £155,000, while in Bendigo
the surplus was only £7945. Ballarat comes third on the
list —its divs. and calls being usually about equal—
in fact, the surpluses proper for the last 20 3'ears are not
over £20,000. Rockhampton (really Mt. Morgan) comes
fourth, with 131,7340 z. the golden mount paying
£300,000 in divs. Fifth comes Coolgardie with 125,0000 z.

* » * *
The Westport (M.L.)Coal Co. is despatching its
first cargoes of coal to Valparaiso and San Francisco, and
is loading a vessel for Tasmania as an experiment in
another new direction. Evidently the deepening of
Westport harbor to admit large vessels has filled the co.
with new ambitions, and it intends trying to cut into the
Newcastle foreign trade in as many directions as possible.
Maoriland still imports about 100,000 tons of coal yearly,
but the local supply is steadily overtaking the consump-
tion, and this market is gradually being lost to N.S. VV.
The attempt to open up an export trade from M.L. to
Tasmania, however, doesn’t look promising. That island
is steadily developing its own coal-deposits, and will soon
be independent of outside supplies—in fact, with a little
more energy it would have been independent of them
long ago.
* » * *
Pearl-shelling in the Straits is on the decline—
the floating stations introduced from W.A. have ruined
it. They scour the beds —a thing impossible under the
old, simple, one-man-one-boat system. The weakness of
the small man was the salvation of the shell-beds, for the
time lost in coming to port gave young shell a respite and
wholesale denudation was hardly possible. The floating
stations keep boats perpetually on the grounds in
all weathers. With the small men the shell was opened
every evening while the oyster and the spat were still
alive—consequently the latter was not lost. With the
schooners the shell remains unopened for weeks, so that
fish and spat are then both dead and useless.
» * * •
One L. C. Burges, of Oakabella, W.A., and a
N.W. pioneer of ’66, claims to be the first discoverer of
gold in that much-boomed province. In March, ’74, when
within two and a-half miles of Roeburne, he spotted a piece
of dark quartz with four or five gold veins running through
it. He took up a claim, but it “duffered,” and it remained
for another man, a few years later, to locate a soz. reef in
the vicinity. Burges, however, never got the £5OOO
reward offered by Govt, for such discovery. That went
into the pockets of the prospector who started the
shameful Kimberley rush.


A Mangatainoka (M.L.) settler lately
appealed to the Land Board for more time in
which to pay his rent. His reasons were as fol-
lows : “ First cow I gave £6 for got bogged in the
creek and died ; second, a heifer I reared, died
calving ; third, a cow I raised, was shot by the
Government inspector when she was making 101 b.
of butter weekly. Dogs killed my sheep; one of my
pigs died and the other was smashed about in a
neighbor’s paddock in such a way that I had to
kill it. Then I lost £2 4s. in a fencing contract
through a fall of earth, and my potato crop was a
failure.” Go on the land, young man !

About the two most absolutely restful
places in Australia are Borroloola and the Roper
River, in the Northern Territory. Borroloola has
a mail from Port Darwin four times a year, and
so is comparatively active and up-do-date. Twice
a year the mail toils along from there to the Roper
River to tell the lonesome inhabitant and his dog
that the outer world is still alive—still rushing
along in big agitated cities, and getting run over
by tranicars, and hurrying to build great ten-
storey buildings, and falling off them in its haste
to get them up, and racing madly after trains,
and hallooing through telephones because it
hasn’t time to gallop round in a cab, and cursing
because its telegram doesn’t do 100 miles in
and breaking its neck to get out the special
’dishun of the Evening Ghoul one half-minute
earlier than the extra-special of the Evening Dead
Cat, and generally galloping, rushing, tearing,
perspiring, racing, falling over itself, cursing be-
cause it can’t get up again quicker, breaking
records, and trying perpetually to cover a mile in
57|secs. Then the mail goes away again, and the
inhabitant, and his dog, and his native bear, are at
peace for another six months, and really don’t
know whether the Chinese have annexed Aus-
tralia or not.

Even in this terribly-droughty season in
the N.S.W. Far West, and despite the scorching
days, water-melons flourish in the dreariest part
of the sandy desert. They are dotted, at intervals,
along the fearsome track between Broken Hill and
Mt. Brown (Q. border), and are said to date from
the days of Burke, Wills, and Capt. Sturt. W hen
the earliest prospectors went to the Barrier silver-
field, they found there a three-acre sand-patch
covered with melons. Moral : Sow melon-seed
everywhere.

block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 060 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-

Department, containing half-a-million books. Ihe
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation' for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the lengtfy and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price,
POST FREE to any part of Australia or N.Z.
As ELLIS’S Boots are made on specially-constructed lasts,
giving the foot an ample tread, you can wear them
straight away with ease and comfort.

THE BRASS OF BRASSEY.
Thomas Lord Brassey,
Your soul is most massy
To jib at your golden name’s tone,
And turn it to Bracey,
That’s Old Norman racey,
And thereby great glory to bone.
But oh, sacred Thomas,
You spoil your rich promise
When only the sound you ■assume,
Why waste the fine spelling ?
What hinders you smelling
The exquisite written perfume ?

Contrary to preconceived opinion, artesian
water seems likely to play an important part in
the scouring of Australian wool. Some fleeces
washed at Moongulla (N.S.W.) bore are described
as “leaving nothing to be desired.”
Recent investigations point to the tendency
to have more than one child at a birth running in
families. One English doctor records recently
that, out of 17 cases of twins occurring in his
practice, he has been able to trace 13 cases where
twin-bearing has occurred once or oftener in im-
mediate ancestresses or blood-relatives (mother,
grandmother, aunts or sisters). In a case of quin-
tuplets (five at a birth) just recorded, the patient’s
mother had twice borne twins. Of course, in con-
sidering cases of two children at a time, it is not
always that the pair of babies are true twins— i.e.,
the product of one fecundation. These are called
coeval (equal-aged) twins. But the pair may
really be the result of two fecundations (super -
foetation), as in the recorded case where a white
woman bore at one birth a pure white child and a
mulatto.

The new law-court recently opened in St.
James’s-road, Sydney, is a vast improvement on
the grubby old structures in vvhien justice and
various other things have been hitherto dispensed.
It is finished in carved cedar, and both the court
and the adjacent apartments have been beautifully
furnished by Messrs Hordern Brothers, of Pitt-
street, Sydney.*
About the only circumstance that prevents
the present Government from sinking into insig-
nificance and oblivion is the large advertisements
one occasionally bumps up against, announcing
that owing to the repeal of the duties Mr. Ragtag
will hold a Preetrade Sale, and give his goods
away almost free of charge (for their full value in
coin of the realm). Pirst comes the Preetrade
sale, then the clearing sale, after which the bill of
sale brings matters to a climax. A notable excep-
tion to the above, however, is the establishment
known as the Misfit Parlor, where Mr. Nathan
Herman has introduced the latest novelty in sales
—the “ Bobby Dazzler.” Thousands of people are
being attracted daily to this Mammoth Ware-
house, which has been turned into a retail em-
porium, and they usually leave it rejoicing at the
genuineness of the magnificent bargains they re-
ceive, only to return next day for more. Mr.
Herman has a pile of letters (the postmarks on
which are convincing proof of their genuineness),
advising the arrival of bales of orders to country
customers, and expressive of the satisfactory
manner in which the orders were attended to.
It appears Mr. Herman personally superintends
this branch of the business, and he is exceptionally
particular that all orders shall be faithfully ful-
filled, and nothing shoddy sent to his country
clients. A reference to his advertisement in
another part of this paper will amply repay
the trouble, as the great bargains there enumerated
are certainly unheard of. His £2 bale is a marvel
of the nineteenth century ; his =£l dress bale, and
his wonderful household parcel for <£l are eye-
openers ; and the whole lot, combined with other
bargains too numerous to mention, fill a list which
is verily a bobby-dazzler. The goods are all
marked in plain figures, and Mr. Herman invites
anyone to compare his prices. Intending cus-
tomers should therefore call early.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.R.C. autumn meeting, commencing on
the 29th, will get some benefit from the policy of the
rival V.A.T.C., which this year extended its February
programme over a couple of Saturdays. The Caulfield
meeting was a financial failure, partly owing to the
weather, but chiefly because the general public are tired
of the cronk racing at this paradise of “stiff” homes.
Nevertheless, the three principal Caulfield events at-
tracted attention to the Newmarket Handicap and Aus-
tralian Cup, so Melbourne is inclined to “ talk-horse ” just
now. Besides, star performers draw the crowd. Neither
Wallace nor Auraria ran at Caulfield, but these two good,
straight-going animals are almost certain ;at time of writ-
ing) to meet at Flemington. Now, the honest rivalry of
Auraria and Wallace can leaven a whole lump of ordinary
turf wickedness.
The present form of Mr. W. R. Wilson’s horses
was unknown to himself, he said, a week ago. Wallace,
like Challenger, has been reported amiss, but reports con-
cerning' the quiet St. Alban’s stable are worth very little.
If Wallace runs at Flemington these next few days, a fair
degree of “ fitness ” in that “ illustrious son of Carbine’’
may be taken for granted. Auraria has been freely re-
ported lame, yet the mare was going well on the track
last week. Auraria was never lame at all, the writer ven-
tures to say, never more than a bit sore, and soreness, as
evidence of steady preparation, is rather a satisfactory
complaint than otherwise. Presuming that the young
heroine will come forth fit and well for her first engage-
ment, The Bulletin herewith plumps for its Melbourne
Hup “ tip ” to win every weight-for-age race she attempts,
including the Champion Stakes. This is a large predic-
tion, but then Auraria is a great mare, and this journal
refuses to believe that any other animal can quite equal
her in a fast-run race over a fair distance. Should
Auraria not be in good form, owner James will yiot run
her out for all her engagements. When a sportsman has
an idol he doesn’t set it up to be ruthlessly shattered.
A confident tip for the Newmarket is excusable
only on the fact that the six-furlong handicap is run over
a straight, roomy course, where jockeys’ foul play gets
little chance to assert itself, and indifferent horsemanship
is of less consequence than in races round the oval. How-
ever, The Bulletin has no confident tip for this
notoriously uncertain raoe. It can’t see that Courallie
(the animal favorite at time of writing) has a strong
chance ; nor Bung-Bung, nor Cabin Boy, nor even Foliage,
although some Adelaide people swear by the alleged
slipperiness of this Malua—Verdure gelding. Foliage’s
form is not at all first-class thus far, and on paper the S.A.
fancy (at Bst. 31b.) is not in it with The Parisienne.
No doubt Hova, if started for the short race,
would beat at least 75 per cent, of the field. But he,
with Challenger and Laundress, declines to make the
journey. Take Tinstream, The Parisienne, Idolator,
Maluma, Acton, Mercia, and Homespun as a likely lot.
The sex, as a rule, makes a good display in the Autumn,
hence Gehenna, Maluma, Tinstream and Marcia are worth
consideration, whilst Idolator and the stylish Homespun
appear most important among the males. A reckless
guess in favor of Maluma, Gehenna, and Homespun may
turn out as well as most guesses. Redfearn’s Maluma is
an own sister to Malvolio, who appeared capable of im-
provement when she ran six inches behind Coil the other
Saturday. Also, she was injudiciously ridden on that
occasion, which, however, will count as a point against
her chance if the same young Redfearn rides her again.
* » * *
Hova, Lord Richmond, Waterfall, Idolator, or
the best from St. Alban’s should manage to capture the
Australian Cup among them. Of Idolator the Ballarat
sages say “ Back him and ’ave faith ’’—for the Newmarket
Handicap. The old ’un is a Newminster horse, and if he
can get as near to winning the short race as he got last
year he will surely give the 2f mile handicap a severe
shaking. Hova is not the best of stayers, perhaps, but
he’s fast enough to win a slow-run race over any distance,
under any reasonable weight. The writer prefers the best
of the two well-performed three-year-olds, Wallace and
Waterfall, with Idolator. Give us Wallace in his Spring
condition, and he will just about struggle home. A brave
stayer is Wallace, in spite of his cowardly color.
The Y. A.T.C. stewards did a reckless deed last
Saturday. After Lord Richmond had won the Autumn
Cup (1| miles) in handsome style, they remembered the
recent indifferent running of the horse at two petty
suburban meetings, and decided to report owner Eli
Jellett and his fickle steed to the V.R.C. It goes without
saying that Eli Jellett is one of the “ little ” men who
hasn’t been granted a special license to snap his fingers at
turf-critics. Lord Richmond is much less of an in-and-out
performer than 50 other horses that lose races by 50yds.
when they ought to win easily, and yet are never subjects
for official enquiry. The “scandalous” feature of this
case was the outside price at which L.R. started.
Had he been a warm favorite, carrying big men’s
little bits, his previous form might have escaped
remark. Since it didn't escape remark, The Bul-
letin will be glad to hear of the V.A.T.C. stewards drop-
ping upon big owners every time their horses are guilty
of a sudden improvement. Also Tiie Bulletin hopes to
hear of Eli Jellett dropping heavily upon the officials if
they neglect to tar all owners—including some of their
own little family party, if necessary with the same
brush. The Lord Richmond case is likely to prove a very
embarrassing precedent.
* * * «
Writes “ Scrip ” : —Two previous winners—
Hova and Laundress, have declined the Newmarket; also
Challenger, Warpaint, Gaillardia, and others reckoned to
have had a chance., The big crowd remaining include
my original five —Idolator, Foliage, Whakawatea, Dis-
parity, with Monard as a possible cockboat. N.S.W. is
miserably represented, but Whakawatea and Courallie
are sprinters of the right stamp. In a dash of this sort
it’s just as hard to locate the winner as it is to pick a
selling race in which none of the neddies are trying, but
Idolator or Foliage should prove the correct article, the
other trio being expected to run prominently at the right
end.
* * * *
The hack display made by Music in the Autumn
Cup staggered punters. His track gallops have been
second only to Hova’s, but he’s said to be a different horse
when playing for keeps.
* * * *
Y.A.T.C. Autumn Cup was a mere three-horse
race. The unprominent figure cut by the rest of the field
suggests a lot of tilings, but ere next week some will, no
doubt, improve sufficiently to finish well in Australian
Cup. Preston and Pilot Boy’s dead-heat for second pro-
vided the only semblance of a contest right through.
Lord Richmond had the race won before they started.
Even with his 101 b. penalty he can’t be deemed right out
of Australian Cup.
* * * *
A common current theme concerns the relative
merits of those two slashing youngsters, Coil and New-
haven. The former has lost his shy, maidenish ways, and
goes out for his gruel like an old stager. Some assert
that his badly-blocked opponent should have won Oakleigh
Plate. But Coil was blocked, tumbled twice and then
scored. The pair are above the average, and there seems
to be nothing much between ’em at present.
In the Ascot Vale and Sires Produce Stakes at
Flemington, Coil will 'meet Newhaven at an advantage,
the latter being fully penalised in both. The books
already see in the two nice betting-material for next Vic.
Derby and Cup.
* * * *
Owner Oxenham never ceased backing Pilot
Boy for the hurdles he won under a big weight on
V.A.T.C. opening day. P.B. is an undoubted flat and
obstacle gem. His brother, Cabin Boy, also running, will
likely not turn out so well. Full relatives of great horses
seldom do. They don’t seem to be sliced out of the same
side of bacon.
* * ♦ *
The Hova crowd preserves its horse admirably.
It pays acceptance-fees," but changes its mind. Hova was
well backed with the books for the Autumn Cup on
Saturday, but wasn’t saddled. He’s not lucky on the
Caulfield course, anyhow.
There s no difference between sanctioned and
ordinary turf gambling. A “ beak ” settles your hash in
either case Ikey M. or the S.M.
• » * *
Henry Redwood, father of the M.L. turf, now
74, goes 'Ome to see another Derby, after living in Nelson
54 years.
* * * *
At Tauranga (M.L.) lately, seven neddies that
could never have seen a hurdle started in the Maiden
sticks, and fell 32 times in all.
* * * *
Licensed jockeys are not allowed to own horses.
Why should licensed bookmakers be allowed to do so?
Surely’ Ikey Mo has quite enough “ in his bag ” ordinarily,
without adding his own to the list.
« * * *
A more or less muggish punter who has been back-
ing chestnut horses with white feet for the last six months
on account of a dream, struck his ideal at a pony meeting.
It won and was disqualified.
* * * *
Spider-legged, peacocky only-out-of-use-now-
and-again alleged racers ornament every racing-track and
paddock. One, perhaps, can’t stay ; another can’t race in
the mud ; a third would break down on a hard course ; and
“ when the handicap and the day and the track arrive to
suit the neddy it’s time to go and get a new horse.”
The M.L. ’chaser, Norton, is already being
roughly handled in minor steeplechases at ’Ome in the
matter of weight. When he meets the best of company
over stiff courses in the Nationals—well, if people over-
advertise their neddies they’ll have to pay for it.
A one-time M.L. writer, finding that he hadn’t
tipped a winner in his sheet for six months, started tick-
ing off horses that had “ no possible” in handicaps, and
selecting the worst as his particular choice. The system
turned out as good as any other, and people used to
wonder where he got his good information from about the
long-shot outsiders.
* « » *
A “poor” Melb. trainer: “I could afford to
race straight if all the richer men at the game did the
same.”
* » » *
A “mug” accosted a bookie the day before a
meet and enquired what he’d lay about five horses in one
particular race, stipulating that he must have a run for
his cash in each case. Bookie hooked the rare fish at once,
offering Bto 1 all round, starting price. The mug knew
four of the five weren’t going to start, and that the other
was a “ cert.” So it was, and he thus got eights about a
6 to 4 chance.
* * » «
Douglas, whose numerous wins over sticks lately
have shot him into prominence, is a rather ugly rider.
He has no style, and gets in no fancy business, but clanks
round in away peculiarly his own. So long as a jock
scores, however, people don’t care if he rides ’em standing
on his head.
* * * «
The only fear that haunted a lately deceased
opulent bookmaker through life was that he'd die poor.
He died mad.
* * * «
Dear Bulletin, —Why republish “Delaware’s ”
petulant virulence against Musket and Musket blood?
When howling against Musket and asking what of his get,
bar Carbine, ever raced after it was 3yrs., “Delaware”
simply displays gross ignorance of his subject, and forgets
(if he ever knew) such marvellous mares as Necklace
(Musket —Locket), Mitrailleuse (Musket —Erycina). The
first-named ran Nelson the mighty to a head for the N.Z.
Cup at syrs., and Mitrailleuse was carrying such “ anvils”
as from 9st. to lOst. 31b. and winning in N.S.W. when she
was 5 and 6yrs. old. Moreover, Lochiel’s Newmarket was
looked upon all round as hers till the two owners agreed
not to cut one another’s throats. These are two of many
celebrities, but Muskets ran in Maoriland up to quite
recently successfully, and we have only to look to
Musket’s success with Locket, Sylvia, Onyx, and Erycina
and a host of other mares which failed to throw winners
to other perfectly-bred sires to be seized of his extraordi-
nary powers. What more do we need than the fact that
Anteros, a far more fashionably-bred horse than Musket,
had chances with some of the cream of Musket’s loves,
after Toxophilite’s son, and got Silver Prince and Niagara
—the only two worth a curse the Sylvia Park Stud Co.
ever bred from that incomparably-pedigreed sire. Martini-
Henry, Nordenfeldt, Trenton, Thunderbolt, Necklace,
Maxim, Matchlock, Krupp, Artillery, Mitrailleuse, Waitiri,
Foul-Shot, Derringer, Bangle, Tirailleur, Cuirassier, Belle
(who raced and won a steeple in her seventh year), Pearl,
Revolver, Cannon (not Hordern’s, but the M.L. grey),
Mary, Brigadier, and the great Carbine, are a few only of
Musket’s successes brought to mind at the moment. And
other imported sires, notably Anteros, Apreinont, Cado-
gan and Leolinus, had chances with the same dams, and
produced next to nothing. “ Delaware,” when writing
on general horse matters, is readable ; when he gets on
to his bete twir (Carbine beat him, when at syrs. he won
Melb. Cup under lOst. 51b. in record time), the Musket-
cim-Carbine strain, he’s simply a crank.—Yours, Tangata-
Maori.
* * * *
A certain Southern bookie was recently induced
to attend the sports of a Sunday-school at which the bookie’s
youngster was a pupil. In one race the bookie, in a
friendly way, laid his friend odds on his (the bookie’s) son,
who was promptly donkey-licked by a Baptist parson’s
brat. “ Bli’ me I” exclaimed the disgusted penciller,” if
that there sky-pilot ain’t gone and stiffened that kid o’
mine 1”
* » * *
The “Tasmanian fortnight” just over was an
unfortunate one for Victorian entries. Mischief, though
dished up warm, came nowhere in Launceston Cup ; the
Wolf got disabled before the start; and the crack fencer
Flat Iron had to succumb to local superiors.
* * • * *
J. J. Miller is publishing the first six issues of
his Australian Racing Chronicle under one cover. The
book which, although plump, can be easily pocketed,
contains 446 pages of horse-performances, and 84 pages of
index, and is far and away the most complete record ever
published in Australia. It incl udes lots of meetings which
have hitherto been haughtily ignored by slow-going com-
pilers of this style of literature.
» * ♦ *
The promoters of the Australian Eleven are con-
fident that their men will lick the strongest team of
“ throw-outs ” it is possible to pit against them. Other-
wise, they wouldn’t be arranging a farewell test-match,
which is expected to draw a big gate, and serve as a good
advance ad. for the Eleven’s tour in England.
* » * *
The Vic. eleven, by far the better team, was
beaten by the weather (and S. Aus.) by 10 wickets.
* * * *
“Jonah’s” performance, six wickets for 15 runs
against Vic. in the second innings, augurs a good time for
hum on fiery English wickets. But English batsmen will
go out and force the game, not stand (as the Vic’s did) like
sheep to be slaughtered. By the way, there isn’t one
forcer in the present Aus. Eleven’s file of batsmen.
♦ » * *
Lyons made 195 out of 242 in a sharebrokers’
cricket-match in Adelaide on Feb. 14, but he failed in a
club-match next day. He cannot hit good bowling as of
yore.
* * * •
A leading cricketer was much in evidence re-
cently. His hat was bashed over his eves, his legs were
very much mixed up, and he would address the crowd :
“ I’m the boss ! They come to see me in thousands !
When I go out and begin to lift ’em” “What,
pints?” said a juvenile, and the great man subsided.
• * « *
There was niuch discontent in Adelaide over
Ferris’s inclusion in the S.A. eleven. He had only been
in Adelaide a few weeks, and, although the other pro-
vinces gave Way, it is certainly straining a point to play
him as a South Australian this season. Peg-leg, by the
way, took 2 wickets for 120 runs for Norwood against
Adelaide—a miserable performance.
* * # *
Lord Hawke’s English cricket team, now visit-
ing South Africa, went to visit the field of battle at
Krugeisdorp, and were all arrested by the Boers for some
reason unexplained. After a little while they were let go
—also for a reason unexplained.
A strict regulation is that none of the Aus.
Eleven shall contribute to the press. I’resuiued, though,
that long letters to “ friends” will appear, as usual.
• * • *
S. A. cricketer Darling is an enthusiastic lacrosse
player, and while in Melbourne for the recent match
made arrangements for a term of Victorian lacrosseurs to
visit Adelaide during the coming winter.
*°• * *
Fitzsimmons’s victory over Maher should cause
him to name the Irish heavy-weight ‘‘the welcome
nugget.” A purse of 10,000 dollars, the Police Gazette
championship belt, and a side wager of 5000dols. is a
splendid scoop for one minute’s work. Now Slavin wants
to fight Fitzsimmons for ±lOOO aside ; Fitz. should win.
He has never yet had a real whipping. The red-headed
Timaruttian blacksmith is a marvel. Physically, a gaunt
skeleton covered with parchment; all his tissue sheer
sinew. A magnificent boxer and a steady liver, with a
lightning peiception and an ice-cold temper. Fitzsimmons
looks to be champion of the world.
» * * »
Maher’s rocketty downfall is a nasty knock for
the blustering- Corbett, who handed the Irishman a title
which wasn't his to hand.
r r * *
The “ noble art ”is dead in Sydney. Four men
paid a shilling each to see the M‘Grath-Hope contest, and
had their money returned. Also, the Foster-Simpson go,
which was to have come off last Monday, fell through
because of empty chairs and benches. Barker, when he
laid Placke out, killed the laying- goose outright. Sydney
pugs can now command anything sooner than the public
and its money. Athletes can no longer be cajoled into
.fighting before a £lOO house for “ toffy.” And the public
seem dead sick of a sport with the most blackguardly sur-
roundings which ever disgraced a civilised community.
* * * *
Tut Ryan, the heavy-weight pugilist, is just
back in Meib. from Johannesburg, and gives a graphic
account of the Krugersdogp fight, in which he officiated
as timekeeper, or something, from the security of an
overlooking hill. Tut took no part in the fight, being con-
tent, like the war-horse of Scripture, to smell the battle
from afar. Bruiser Goddard, it seems, held the position
of a staff-captain in the Uitlander army which never did
anything-, and had command of 500 men. The moment
the news that Jameson was approaching- reached
Johannesburg, says Ryan, the police were withdrawn for
fighting purposes, and the whole place was given up to
the gangs of spielers who infest it. Traffic was stopped,
and all along the main streets the three card man, the
monkey-trick and all the rest of the innocent accessories
of the racecourse reigned supreme.
♦ * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The scribe who refers to Par-
sons as “the much-over-rated Parsons” is a trifle out.
“ The much-gone-off Parsons ” would possibly have been
true, but a man who has knocked off a mile in 2min. 2sec.
on a poor track like the dull Exhibition Oval, who has put
up a world’s grass record of 5 miles, and who has defeated
Zimmerman, is certainly not over-rated when he is ex-
pected to defeat anything we have in Australia now.—M‘G.
+ * * *
Parsons, riding well last Saturday at Adelaide,
won the first-class mile. “ Jack ”is a brilliant rider, but
many of his best friends think he has a little bundle. It’s
a certainty that the heavy-jawed Yankee Martin hasn’t
even the shadow of a bundle, and therefore the match on
March 28 between the two is odds on Martin.
• » ♦ *
Parsons’s new Australian mile record—2min.
3sec. —will probably be broken on the Sydney Agricul-
tural Society’s track before very long. By the way,
Megson, it is said, is coming on again—the old Megson,
who claimed to do a mile in 2min. on the Adelaide track.
* * * *
“Jack” Fitzgerald, the Vic. ex-champion
amateur sculler, who scales only a little over lost., rides
the highest-geared cycle in Australia, his racing jigger,
locally-made, being fitted with the enormous gear of 102 in.
Zim raced with only a 68-gear, and Pither, who rode with
a 92-gear when he beat Zimmerman, held the gear record
up to Fitz’s time.
# * * •
Mrs. H. P. Nicholls, a Melb. long-distance
cyclist, lately turned off 176 miles in a day, doing the
AVarrnambool-Melb. journey between 1.30 a.m. and 9 p.m.
During a nine days’ trip the same lady pedalled over 689
miles.
* * * *
A Melb. cycling paper publishes a pageful of
weird photos of knobby knees and bulgy calves, alleged
to belong to prominent local wheelmen, and offers a
guinea to the enthusiast who first guesses the owners.
Any odds that the prize will go to one of the impetuous
damsels who throng the reserve on the days of cycle-
meets, and heave bouquets at their heroes. Apropos,
how about a competition the other way round ?
* * ♦ *
Bicyclists in long-distance races, especially paced
races on cold days, often find that toward the close their
vision becomes so much impaired as to spoil their steering,
even to making them run into other machines. The cause
is the friction of the air on the sensitive eye-surfaces,
which brings on numerous bleedings from the small
vessels.
* * * *
M‘Lean, who won the Vic. 10-mile champion-
ship, is not in the trade ! Possibly this fact will not hold
long, as every second Melb. shop' just now is a cycling
establishment, and every proprietor and agent is on the
look-out for good advertising talent. M'Lean is still an
infant, and his pa is Mayor of Northcote.
* * * *
The League of American Wheelmen proposes to
abolish the “ amateur ” and let every racing man run for
cash or trophy as he pleases, thus placing all men on one
satisfactory level.
* * * *
Stanbury, who goes ’Ome to row Harding for
£5OO and the world’s championship, if he has any venera-
tion for “ dear old England,” will allow Harding to take
the belt and the cake. And he will do this also if he has
any love of sculling for its own sake. Sculling, as far as
the British people are concerned, has been damned with
cold neglect ever since the British champions were per-
sistently “taken down” by Americans and “colonials.”
Before that it was a noble British sport, and heaps of
money were spent upon it, and the expert sculler was
abundantly glorified. Now, the cold, proud British
person scarcely recognises it. Pugilism, too. upon which
the Britisher so prided himself when British pugs could
thump creation, is no longer vaunted with patriotic
enthusiasm in English papers and English conversation as
“the manly art” and “the noble British sport,” since
British champions have been systematically wiped out by
Yanks and Australians. If- Stanbury allows the cham-
pionship to go back ’Ome, sculling will boom again in
England, and will rise again to the dignity of being re-
garded by Britishers as a peculiarly British institution
by Jove. And then Australians will be able to make a
little money out of it.
* * * »
Hanlan and Searle met once only. The dav
before the Grafton Regatta of ’BB, the Canadian came
across a novice in practice, and, getting into conversation
learned that the country-boy was to row against him in
next day’s race. Partly through curiosity, partly in fun
he asked for a spin. He got it, and a beating. To the’
disappointment of the public, Hanlan did not start next
day. He contented himself with backing the novice.
* * * *
A very well-known Melb. man, who would
gamble his soul, lately had the bailiffs in. He paid the
debt and got his receipt after the bailiff had been “there”
three days, but the latter would not shift unless he got
his fees besides. The debtor sized the bailiff up aim
offered to play him euchre for double his fees. Now that
bailiff fancied he knew more about euchre than any living
man indeed, he was an ex-euchre spieler. So, after satis°-
fymg himself that the debtor had the double fees about
him, they started it, and within 25min. the debtor had not
only won back the debt and fees in cash but the broker
had to leave an IO U for £5 odd. How the bailiff settled
with the other crowd doesn’t worry the debtor, as he got
full receipts for everything. •' °
* * * •
Syd. Gregory was married last Monday.


Ex-detective Eason died last week in Mel-
bourne.' He was about the last of the old school,
the first Melb. “D’s,”and indeed even the iden-
tities thought him long dead. Eason, Black, and
Williams were the best-known men, but Eason
retired from the metropolis long before the other
two, about 30 years ago. These three were
Aspinall’s chief butts in cross-examination, and
they all entertained a very low opinion of his wit.

cairn.

ret irn by the ordinary trains (Melbourne Express

March ; Albury at 2.45 p.m., Junee, 7.20 p.m.,

March.

mile first-class (minimum 10s.) and ONE PENNY

inclusive, by the above trains.

ward journey by the special trains named, but

can return from Sydney by the 5.15 p.m. Express

Various Verses.
MY ROSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Oft in a garden I have found
A rose that nestled to the sound
Of waterfalls from shadowy hills,
Bourne across the hidden rills ;
Music that has sweeter been
That its cradle is unseen.
She upon her slender perch
Wavers to bird-bills that search
In her coronet for beads
Showered from Eve’s dusky bredes ;
While some coppice-hidden bird’ll
Scatter round her for a girdle
Tangles of his throbbing soul
Por some poet to unroll.
From tier petals I have drawn
Incense waiting for the dawn,
Or to float upon the rain
If the South wind comes again.
To ifiy lips each petal lies
Limpid as my dearest’s eyes—
Eyes more beautiful by far
Than the glow of evening’s star
When her aureole is strewn
Underneath the sickle moon.
Then I leave her in the gloom,
Swooning to her own perfume.
Ah ! my spirit, when I come,
As the next day neareth home,
And my rose of all the brood
Has been plucked by fingers rude !
Thou, that art an opening bud,
By each spirit to be wooed
That cherisheth the ancient lore
To love, and to love evermore,
The beautiful: Oh ! that I could
With thee inherit solitude !
But afar my steps must go,
Thou, perhaps, wilt never know
The fulness of my quiet pain,
Aching that when I come again,
Thou, .of all rosebuds’ diadem,
Mayst have been taken from thy stem !
HUBERT CHURCH.
THE PEASANT’S PEDIGREE.
Sneers my lord with smile superior as he boasts
his “ noble ” line ;
Shows the pictures of his fathers—silken robe
and ruffle fine,
Points to miles of glades and forests green beneath
the ambient air,
Which the sovereign gave the subject when he
found his wife was fair.
Can we claim a higher lineage ? In the marshes
and the brake
Thence to vex the human l-obber lurked bold
Here ward, called the Wake.
Prom the South with hundreds following to
avenge the peasant’s shame,
Dark despair and deep dishonor, martyred Wat
the blacksmith came.
Brave Jack Cade, who led the masses in a fierce
and hungry tide,
Honest Wilkes, the king-denouncer, and a thou-
sand more beside ;
Toil-worn hands and heavy faces, but they lived
to set us free :
Lo, the ancestors of Demos—this the Peasant’s
Pedigree !
Maoriland.
C. CLYDE.
A SOUTHERLY IN SYDNEY.
The morning broke fair,
Not a move in the air,
And the citizens’wives heard the citizens swear.
The astronomers, they
Heard the mercury say
“ I must make up my mind for some climbing to-
day :
Yes, this is a proper thermometer day.”
It’s oh for a Southerly rushing and free,
It’s oh for a Southerly fresh from the sea,
It’s oh for a Southerly out on a spree :
A Southerly Buster, aho !
The heat settled down
On the sweltering town,
And the citizens liquored, their troubles to drown.
Their foreheads they fanned,
Cursing climate and land,
While the mercury hurried up, hand over hand.
It’s oh for a Southerly rolling along,
It’s oh for a Southerly merry and strong,
It’s oh for its cheery and bellowed-out song,
A Southerly Buster, aho !
At last, when the thirst
Of the city was worst,
With a rush the uproarious Southerly burst.
He had cooled every gust
On the Pole’s icy crust,
And for that they all blessed him in spite of
the dust.
It’s oh for a Southerly, though he may thresh
The dust into storm, he is sturdy and fresh,
He is soothing of spirit and cooling of flesh,
The Southerly Buster, aho !
Sydney.

A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later: —“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks Volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”
A Lady’s Wish Fulfilled.
It gives me great pleasure indeed to inform
you that your celebrated Alfaline Universal Pills
cured ine, when all other remedies and doctors
failed, of a stubborn case of Debility of 18 years’
standing- My- wish is-fulfilled at last, and I don’t
know how to thank you enough. Dear Sir, I
shall recommend yourVAlfaline Remedies to all
sufferers I come across. You are quite at liberty
to publish or make what use you like of this testi-
monial, and anyone writing to me will have full
particulars. I am, yours respectfully, Mrs. E.
SMITH, Port Hastings River, N.S.
Wales.

Ithaca-st., Normanby Hill, Brisbane,

Dear Sir, —I am jubilant to inform you that
your wonderful Alfaline Pills completely cured
me of a very obstinate internal complaint; and
that, although I am now over 70 years of age,

always be grateful to you.
Yours very truly, N. LARSEN.

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir,—ln the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness iu the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with L' v er
and Kidney Pills, and" free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

Sir, —I hereby beg to certify that having been
a sufferer from General Debility of the Nervous
System for over seven years, and having tried

happy to recommend your remedies to anyone
suffering as I was. Thanking you for your
courtesy and the uniform patience with which
you have treated me, allow me to say that you
can make what use you may think proper of this.
Wishing you every success, allow me to remain,
yours,
CATHERINE HAGERMAN.
As witness: John Hagerman (my husband),
caretaker Tivoli Theatre, Castlereagh-street,
Sydney.

FOR SALE.
A Railway Romance .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
’Twas but a little trembling maid,
A Vandemonian lass,
Who trained it all alone, afraid
(As Yandemonians are, ’tis said,
In spite of all their gas),
From Hobart unto Launceston—
What was her little heart set on ?
A trembling maid of nineteen years,
A poutress shy and soft;
A seltzogene of ready tears,
Of nineteen harvest-tides of fears !
We do not meet such oft !
Into her train, as it began,
There stepped a horrid, hairy man !
The night was cold ; the train was slow,
As only trains can be ;
The hairy man was kind, for, oh !
He lent his trav’ling-rug, and so
He fairly frightened She—
This baby of the nineteen years,
Who’d read of railway buccaneers.
The hairy man (put this in print)
For Coughing Mixture reached ;
’Twas aniseed and peppermint—
He took a swig ; she took a hint,
And up she got and screeched !
Jumped off the flying train ! By Jo '
It’s lucky Tassy’s trains are slow.
Yes, Vandemonian trains are slow,
And Yandy girls are quick !
She wasn’t hurt a bit, on, no !
She overhauled the big loco.
In quite a fashion slick ;
And gave the hairy man in charge
For drinking Cough Drops thus at large.
Perchance Hysteria had a dream,
For frozen print too warm ;
Leastways, Nineteen explained her scream,
Narrating a neurotic scheme
Including chloroform !
The brightest fancies, one may see,
Of shy Nineteen “gang oft agley.”
The Balkan war-scribes tell a tale
About a market-place
A-crowd with virgins, huddled, pale ;
With troops in charge. A waiting wail—
Then Spokesmaid puts the case :
“ Oh ! sir, when does the violence
That we are waiting for commence ? ”
Dear Maidens of the Heart-shaped Isle,
That men are men is true ;
But let Hysteria not beguile
Your fancies into horror’s wile,
And here’s a truth for you :
Men seldom lurk to take a chance
Unless you make the sign, “ Advance ! ”
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Some idea of the force of the recent
cyclone in Townsville may be gathered from the
circumstance that black seagulls were blown
inland for some miles and perished. The nearest
islands inhabited by these gulls are 1000 miles
away.
Petition from an Indian native to an Eng-
lish official: —
O’nerd Master,—Having heard of you Almighty mercy
and loving kindness to us worms, I tell you my circum-
stances. By the Grace of God and your Lordship, I have
seven children, all babes and sucklings, besides this
abominable litter I have many male and female relations.
What have I done to be blessed with such cursed trials.
As your Lordship is my father and my mother, I write to
request that you will take this worm and wife and suckles
and relations, both male and female, and provide for us
from your bounty at a remuneration of rupees 20 a
month. I can’t read or write, and have only the suckle
qualifications and male relations and feminine, but by the
grace of your Almighty Lordship I look forward to years
of prosperity and happiness. All the “ chaoni ” of
sing of your praises, your justice and mercy. Therefore
call us all that we may fatten on your gentleness. Call
quickly.—Your faithful worm and beast,
Munni Lall,
Despicable brute and unwilling father of babies.
Another Indian servant, a major domo, writes in
the tradesmen’s account-book kept by him for his
master “behind leg of ship” (meaning leg of
mutton), “bullock milk” (meaning cow’s milk,
not from buffalo), and “foodings for little dog’s
child ” (meat for puppies).


i- C. J. Martin, of Sydney University,
lias published a 130 pp. paper on snake-venom
and its action. Snake-poison taken into the
stomach has, lie says, no effect whatever unless
some damage to the walls lets it into the circula-
tion. Venom injected acts more quickly when
the subject has a full stomach than when fasting.
It a fatal dose be injected directly into a vein, all
blood in that vein will in Umin. clot into a solid
P j A. emulation is stopped by the clotting,
and the animal dies of thrombosis. If the venom
be slowly injected, as through a prick from a pin
in the skm, the blood tends to coagulate, but
never becomes fatally clotted ; in fact, in a
couple of hours a reaction sets in and the blood loses
all coagulativepower. In the case of rapid injection
directly into a vein, Calmette’s new chloride of
lime treatment, I)r. Martin says, is valueless;
likewise, if the blood has only slightly lost its
coagulability, a few drops of chloride of lime will
cause it immediately to solidify, and the animal
dies in a few minutes of excessive thrombosis.
Venom attacks the red corpuscles of the blood
and utterly dissolves them, probably thus pro-
ducing a new and very powerful poison ; likewise,
it causes the white corpuscles to burst. Also,
when the corpuscles are thus rendered hors de
combat, the poison attacks the walls of the vessels
and capillaries, and causes much hemorrhage into
the lungs and internal organs. Also, the heart
becomes pai’alysed. All these experiments show,
contends Dr. Martin, that strychnine is valueless
absolutely, chloride of lime practically worthless,
and that the only likely remedy lies in immunisa-
tion by serum-therapy.
