HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
Girder's Little Joke .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Girder was our fellow-passenger’s name,” said
Harrison as he filled his pipe. “We christened
him hestive Girder—partly on account of his
dismal appearance, but more because of a curious
(lamt he had of making miniature coffins. It
appears he had a mania for seeing corpses, and he
told us gruesome yarns about his visits to public
morgues, hospitals, and, in fact, any pl ace where
he could view a body.’ He had a collection of
photographs showing corpses of both sexes and all
ages in every stage of decomposition. He would
narrate with evident gusto how he had managed
to get a sight of the deceased in every house
within a radius of some miles from his lodgings
He was like a crow—could smell death at anv dis-
taker’ turned U P as punctually as the under-
“Wf wer ® b 7 no means squeamish, but some of
Girders realistic descriptions were too much for
us, and an hour of corpse conversation was gener-
ally sufficient for the day. As there were no
opportunities for directly indulging his passion on
board, he sought an outlet in the manufacture of
small coffins, begging chips of wood from the car-
penter and working them up into perfect little
gems of caskets. He had a stock of pins, small
entomologists specimen-pins, from which he made
very good imitation coffin-nails, and each article
he turned out was perfect in detail. He had them
m cedar mahogany, pine, and oak. He covered
them with black or blue stuff, painted, varnished,
or I rench-polished them. He cut out bright little
tm plates and engraved particulars of the name
and age of an imaginary deceased on them. Not
a single detail escaped his careful fingers, and
when the coffin was finished he constructed a
corpse to fill it.
He got thick slices of bread from the steward
toasted them slightly, and, picking out the dough,
worked it up into an extraordinarily-realistic
corpse. After placing it in the coffin he pinked
out white paper, pasted it round the upper edges,
and laid the lid beside it. He had names for all
his defuncts, and would place them in a row in
order of date of construction, and observe how the
process of decomposition was getting on. And
that dough lent itself to the deception in a most
remarkable way. Owing to the wet state of the
ship it rapidly became mouldy, and developed a
green or black fungoid growth that gave the
little figures a horrible appearance. The mouth
would crack sometimes, and the nose fall flat-
pah ! It was a beastly fad.
“ He seemed to be shy of showing his talent to
the skipper or the officers, and they never got an
inkling of his mania, until one unlucky day some
devil of mischief put it into his head to'play' a joke
on the second mate —Coullet by name—a swarthy-
looking pirate who might have been a Creole or a
feouth Sea Island half-caste. There was just
enough tarbrush in him to show in his eyes, his
fingernails, and his brown skin, but not enough to
‘ place ’ him definitely.
“ Well, Girder might have had more sense than
to try his hand on Coullet, but he had just com-
pleted a very beautiful coffin, and had beaten out
a threepenny-piece to make a real silver name-
plate, and some infernal prompting led him to
engrave the second mate’s name on the plate. As
an additional touch of the realistic he blackleaded
the dough corpse. He was so tickled with the
whole conceit that he actually laughed—the first
time since he came aboard.
He took the miniature dead mate awav after
showing it to us. Jack howled after him : ‘ Don’t
let him see that or there will be trouble.’ Girder
°, n y ginned and all through tea that evening he
chuckled softly, especially when his eye fell on
Oouilet who was plying a good knife and fork on
e cold mutton—especially knife, for he had his
own sheath-knife at work. He always used it,
although the steward placed an ordinary dinner-
knife for his use, and to see him conveying
a cargo of turned peas down his throat on that
curved blade was apt to startle you.
“ Coullet, as usual, had finished first, and went
straight from the saloon to his cabin. We were
rounding off with the cheese, when a hoarse scream
rang through the ship. We sat still for quite a
minute, listening, and then the skipper said:
Someone s hurt,’ and half rose from his seat, but,
at that moment, a man burst into the saloon,
staggered to the table, and, grasping the cloth
with both hands, stood swaying backwards and
orwards, trying to articulate in a sputtering way
through the froth on his lips, while great beads of
perspiration poured from his forehead on to the
cloth.
r 'J’ as Coullet, but Coullet white, even to the
lips. Ihe skipper was at his side in a moment,
and soon we had him up on deck, and freshened
up a little with a stiff glass of brandy.
‘‘ He recovered sufficiently to point towards his
cabin and groan out, ‘ My God, look there ! ’ '
Ihe skipper went first, and we followed,
leaving Coullet in the care of the steward. On
reaching the second mate’s berth, we found the
door open, the lamp turned rather low, and, Ivin"-
m the bunk, with the bedclothes neatly folded
under it, was Girder’s latest coffin, with the black
corpse placidly grinning behind the pinked-out
white paper.
The skipper looked very serious. He took up
the ugly toy, looked it carefully over, opened the
port and threw it overboard. Then he turned to
us and said quietly : ‘ This may perhaps strike one
of you gentlemen as very funny, but I dan assure
you it is no laughing matter to play a practical
joke of this kind on a man like my second officer.
1 will not ask which of you did it, but will warn
him, whoever he is, to keep it to himself.’
Girder looked rather sick, but ‘ kept on saying
nothing, and it seemed as if the matter was going
to blow over. More, apparently, to save himself
from the results of a disturbance than to protect
Girder, the skipper had told “Coullet that there
was nothing to be seen in the bunk, and had
almost persuaded him that he was suffering from
a niline.nation on that particular evening, when
Girder s unlucky star came into the ascendant
once more. \
‘‘lt. was a fine day—an unusual occurrence—
and Girder wiis perched up forward whistling the
Dead M irch in ‘ Saul ’ —about the only tune he
could whistle intelligibly-when Coullet passed by
m a had temper, and roughly asked him to ‘ tie a
string to that rotten tune and hang it.’ Girder
felt huffed at the mate’s manner, and was getting
up in a dignified manner to go aft, when a tin
coffin-plate which he had been engraving fell
rattling from between his knees to the deck and
right to Coullet’s feet.
“The second mate stooped to pick it up, but
directly he saw what it was he whipped out his
sheath-knife and sprang on Girder with a howl of
ra ?< e v
“ Girder was taken by surprise, and the knife
went in between his ribs with a curious little
bubbling sound. He uttered a kind of sob and
fell where he stood, and Coullet pulled the knife
out and flung it overboard.
“ The mate was grabbed and put in irons, and
we did what we could for Girder, but it was no
use. He died in an hour from hemorrhage, and if
he could have watched them he would have been
grieved to see how quickly his obsequies were got
over. When we got to port, Coullet changed liis
irons for a cell, but these warm-blooded men fret
among cold stones, and he didn’t last out his sen-
tence.
. “And see this ? ” said Harrison. “That’s one
of Girder’s coffins I saved when the rest were
thrown overboard, and kept as a memento.”
“Ugh!” said Moxley, who had a tender
stomach, “pass the whisky.”
G. F. BASSET HULL.
A ball and supper have been given out at
Hillston, N.S.W., to aid the fund for presentation
to a retiring clergyman.
An important appeal case, Piper v.
RN.S.W., was argued at Sydney before Judge
Windeyer last law term. His Honor reserved
judgment, and sailed for England without deliver-
mg it. The consequence is that the work has all
to be done over again ; the suitors’ time and the
time of the court is wasted, and the lawyers’ fees
are doubled to no purpose. Could not Judge
Windeyer have cleared up his arrears before he
took his holiday ? There is a growing feeling that
the long vacations, the frequent holidays, and the
more frequent whims of N.S.W\ Judges might be
advantageously restricted. Some of them have
proved very unprofitable public servants. Cer-
tainly, to a Judge, every consideration should be
shown ; but, in comparison with their colleagues
in other provinces, do not N.S.W. Judges demand
a little too much ?
Dear Bulletin, —Have just, been a-strol-
ling through the Victorian country—a “before-
breakfast ” walk, so to speak —spying out the
nakedness of the land. At Sunbury the grazing
paddocks looked from a little distance like ploughed
land, so bare are they. Here I first observed
the cattle eating horse-dung, both fresh and stale.
The bunburyites are (have to be) economical—
they subsequently use the dried cow-dung as fuel,
wood being practically non-existent. One sees
the children out of an evening gathering fuel for
the morning’s fire. A trifle further out bunny
puts himself in evidence; and the abundance of
rabbits this year is due less to the retrenchment of
inspectors than to the dry winter. Witness also
the exceeding plentifulness of ants. Between
Lancefield and Kilmore the “cookies” have
been using poison regularly, and still the rabbits
are thick. One man poisoned a furrow 200
yards long outside a patch of amber-cane, just
above the Deep Creek : he got a crop of 240
bunnies next morning. Trappers are doing fairly
well. Sixpence a pair for export, if prime, less
freight to Melb. and charges. Please take a long
breath. A farmer (Jas. Cottam) living on the
aforesaid Deep Creek lost a heifer through her
eating poisoned rabbit —such is the scarcity of
grass ; and other cows have also been seen nibbling
at them. At Whittlesea a man bought three
cows—not old, but poor—for 6s. a-piece. He
intends to sell the hides and feed his pigs on the
boiled-down carcases. Verily, it has been a dry
season ; another such, and Victoria would have to
drop dairying and breed rabbits for export (which
last may come some day).—F. C. Feb. 6.
A Tight Place .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Twenty years ago, in N.Q. The Boomerang,
then one of the A.S.N. Co’s crack steamers run-
ning to Cook town, had gone ashore on the South
Barnards, 40 miles north of Rockingham Bay.
Up came the order, “Lighter her,” and we
gathered half-a-dozen ketches and lightered her,
finding the cargo to consist of blankets, bottled
beer, and some fearful champagne manufactured
m the regions of the “ Suez Canal,” Lower George-
street.
Up came the further order, “ Sell salvaged
cargo by auction.” And we sold it; you bet we
sold it—without reserve. Cases of beer and porter
averaged Is. 6d., and champagne 2s. The labels
were off the bottles ; their outsides were foul and
muddy, but their insides ! It was summer time,
and we were thirsty. There was no competition,
for there were 600 cases; and the population
numbered 180.
I hen for a fortnight ensued pandemonium.
Every man reverted to the customs of his simian
ancestors, and went about on all fours. In the
middle Gov. Normanby called in on his farewell
round of visits, and a long string of quadrupeds
crawled painfully up the half-mile jetty to meet
him, headed by the pilot, for the P.M. was in bed
suffering from fever and ague. Normanby gazed
m wonderment. “ Good heavens, men !” he cried,
“ don’t kneel to me. I’m not the QUEEN —only
her viceroy ; get up, get up.” It took half an hour
and a bushel of hiccoughs to explain that we
couldn’t.
. % heaven ! it was the tightest place ever I was
m—and oh ! that I were 20 years younger and had
to go through it again !
Lithgow.
YARRAN.
“ The complaints of the people are always
just.”—Mirabeau.
Jingo—-but true. At the time of the
Sedan capitulation in 70 a crowd of excited
foreigners congregated outside Melb. Age office,
and when the fateful wire was posted a scene
followed incomprehensible to English-speaking
persons. Eat Germans fell on the bosoms of lean
Germans and wept; young Germans grasped both
the hands of old Germans while they sang snatches
of the Wacht am Rhine.” But when one stout
Bavarian in spectacles and lager beer at the rear
of the crowd started to dance and yelled in Eng-
lish, “Ve vill be in Lonn-donn next ! Ve vill be
in Lonn-donn next ! ” a nuggety Lancashire black-
smith, with a neck of mighty diameter, caught the
gist of the German’s threat and asked the “ blanky
Dutchman ”to say that again. The Bavarian did
say it again, and then he got “one on the jaw.”
That started the trouble, and in three minutes a
general German-English row was afoot. A few
police came along but were powerless to cope with
the angry crowd, and the Governor, who was pass-
ing at the time, being a sportsman, had his car-
riage drawn up to the footpath while he watched
things to a conclusion. England, in strong force,
ultimately triumphed, and the hospital report the
following day was filled with items like “ Carl
bchinidt, torn ear ; August Himmelstrahlende,
contused eye,” and other words to that effect. In
view of the recent disagreement between Hing-
land and Germany, the recounting of a little inci-
dent like this may do much to pour oil on the
troubled flames.

The Queen of Clubs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
I The fortune-teller noticed in the interim of card-
shuffling that her client was very young and dark ;
also, that the white, clenched hand on the grimy
table-cloth had a wedding-ring on it. Madame
contemplated her own tawny fist and sighed.
Then, in the jargon of the seer
“ Blue eyes he has, and very fair. The king ol
diamonds must be your husband ? ”
The girl shut her lips and nodded. Again the
cards were shuffled and cut in three places.
“You are queen of clubs, because your eyes are
brown. See where the knave of spades crosses
the king of diamonds ? ” (
“ Knave of spades,” muttered the queen, why
is he a spade ?”
“ He is dark.”
“How dark?” ,
“ Spade-dark—dark as the pit. Dear me, and
the knave loves the queen of clubs ; but his heart
is bad : small hearts —bad hearts ! ”
“ He loves me ! ” whispered the queen, her eyes
aglow in the dusky room.
“ Beware of him! ” droned Madame; he hates
the king of diamonds ! ’ T
‘ ‘ But he loves me ! ” gasped the queen. lsn t
that enough ? ”
“ You are wet! ” said the queen at the ricketty
gate. Her hand sought the man’s dripping coat.
Very dark he was and very young. He glanced
round the bare room and shivered.
“No fire to dry me. Good God ! ” The queen
closed the door softly.
“The wood hasn’t come yet, dear. She was
very close to him ; her lips touched his cheek.
Outside, the loud, ceaseless calls of the city and
the eternal grind of traffic. A handful of dead
marguerites under the battered casement lay half-
hid under a cairn of road-metal the urchins had
flung in at the cats.
He limped to the tawdry book-case, as it to
select something, then stopped, his back to her,
and his hand uplifted. , . ~
“ I met —I met him to-day. He turned at the
sharp breath-expulsion behind, but the queen was
only dusting chairs.
“You met him?” . ,
“ Yes, him ; he followed me with his buggy
until we reached a back street. I heard the
damned sovereigns chink in his pocket when he
got down. Wish to God ! ” ,
“ Sovereigns ! ” interrupted the queen. He s
a diamond-man, and wears them anywhere in
the back of his shirt, in his bangle ; I’m not sure
that he hasn’t them set in his teeth—and his
boots. I don’t think he’s civilised, and I coiddn t
help running away from a husband like that
now’, could I, Jack?”
The knave rubbed at some greasy spots on his
vest.
“ Of course you couldn’t. ”
“ What did he say, Jack ? ” The knave dropped
into a chair, mopping his white, nervous face.
“ He’s going to divorce you.”
“Is that all ? ” The queen laughed—an agree-
able, well-pitched woman’s laugh. He watched
her in his dull-eyed way, without passion or
interest. Her laugh conjured up a curious inner
vision of the cheated husband, and then of him-
t,elf —dragged on to a dead lee-shore by the strange
force of this woman ! .
The queen took up a candle, setting it near the
window, but the wind came in the rattling case-
ment and guttered it immediately. They were
in the dark again.
“What are you doing?” He sobbed out a
fretful oath.
The flare of a pawnbroker’s lamp opposite caught
her in its angle of light, and he saw that the queen
was smiling munificently—offering him something :
he stretched out his hand and felt her wedding-
ring drop into his palm. “ Get yourself some
chops, Jack.”
And the knave went out.

ALBA DORION.
This from the window of a Russian Jew
bootmaker, recently arrived in Melbourne : “ Boots
and Shoes Repaired. Notice. 2d. paid to every
boy or girl who brings a pair to be soled and
heeled. For patches, Id.”
At Unley (S.A.), a parcel, containing a
bridle, pair of socks, towel, hairbrush, and a key,
was thrown into the police-station. A letter was
enclosed to this effect: —
On December 5, 1895, the enclosed were taken by a
young wild-oat-sower, in devilment, from Wolseley. Now
converted, he restores them with apologies : —Bridle, from
an open shed alongside a store, near Dunn’s ; socks, from
house nearly opposite Dunn’s ; towel, from next to the
sock place ; brush and key, from railway. All were taken
by a Melbourne profligate, for a lark, between 5 and 6
a.m. Under Salvation influences he is now converted,
and, having collected the articles, restores them through
the police.
There are in the world about 7,000,000
Jews. Jews contract fewer marriages, and have
a smaller birth-rate and mortality than other
races. Early marriages are more frequent, and
three times more marriages between relations are
contracted. More boys are born, and the number
of illegitimates and stillborn is smaller. Maxi-
mum death-rate is at over 60. Suicides are less
frequent. Are not predisposed to consumption
and cholera, but there is a decided tendency to
diabetes, hemorrhoids, and insanity. Deaf and
dumb, blind and daltonians are more frequent.
Majority inhabit towns, and the proportion of
poor is larger. Twins and triplets are less
common. Inter-marriages are sterile —cases of
illness are more frequent. Jews are of less phy-
sical development and lower stature.
The disputed territory on the Venezuela-,
Guiana border contains sundry curiosities such as
no other part of the known world can boast of.
These are a number of isolated mountains which
are wholly inaccessible unless perhaps by a
balloon. The best-known of them, Roraima, ha,s
absolutely perpendicular sides, and looks as if
even a fly could not get up without the aid of an
alpenstock. No man ever got up, so far as is
known, or, if he did, he never got down again.
The summit appears to be a table-land of about
130 square miles. A waterfall drops 2000 feet
sheer into the country below, so evidently there
are either lakes or rivers or both in this isolated
patch of country. Apparently the earth fell
away somehow from Roraima and the other moun-
tains of its kind long, long ago—millions of years
ago it may be, and whether there was any race of
human beings on top at the time, and if so,
whether their remote descendants are there now,
and what they may be like, and what kind of
language, religion and civilisation they have
developed on their lone mountain top, and how
they will receive the future explorer who gets
there per balloon, are all interesting problems.
Mate in Two Moves.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“ Are you married, may I ask ? ”
A florid-faced, middle-aged, brusque-mannered
man fired this question in a loud, bullock-
driving voice, while crossing a main street in a
Sydney suburb, one afternoon lately.
The object aimed at was a fairly-made, but con-
siderably worn, young woman, of the domestic-
servant class. She had just emerged from the
gateway of a house in which she was employed,
and the catapulted question naturally startled her.
“ Excuse me,” proceeded the swain, advancing.
“ I know I’m a bit wrong, but I mean no
offence. If you’re not married an’ O, listen a
minute, I’ll show you it’s all right. But are you
married ? ”
“ Well, I’m not,” answered the girl.
“Then,” he said, “you’re just the figure of
woman I’m after.”
He had now come so close that shouting was no
longer necessary. . . ..
“Of course,” he continued, in a voice really
mellow and well-cadenced, “I don t ask you to
b’lieve what I say merely on my own word. D you
know the Rev. Thomas Stubbs ? He ’ficiates in
the brick place on the top o’ the hill. He lias
known me off an’ on for years. I was m the choir
when he first come out. He’ll tell you all you
want to know about me. D’you know Brown the
grocer at the Point ? He’s my cousin. Ive a
couple of hundred in cash, an’ can look the police
in the face. Pa’son an’ Brown ’ll certify that.
Think I’ll do ? ”
“ Eh ? ” he asked, after a pause.
“ But it’s so sudden ! ” the girl answered.
“ ’Tis.”
“ You are in a hurry ! ”
“ Say no, and I’m off again in the mormn .
“ But you don’t know even my name.”
“Tell ms that after. Take your ’davy,to the
Pa’son that you’re free in law, an’ that’s all I
want.”
The girl was silent. Long years of grimy slaving
lay away behind ; long, dim years full of terrors
stretched out before. Two dozen mistresses had
already passed over her head. A week or fort-
night with some, a' month with others, six months
or a year with more, and she was now on the
verge of twenty-eight. It was nearly all darkness;
darkness with splutterings of evil tempers and
suspicious natures, tyrannical dispositions and
imperious minds. It was all hard work, small
pay, little thanks and many insults ; fourteen
years of servitude and sorrow. And two dozen
mistresses and fourteen years more! Baker and
butcher are barely civil to her now ; in fourteen
years more the eyes of even these will be averted
from her!
Raising her head she said, “If what ye say s
true I’ll take ye.”
“ Good iron !” exclaimed the man, seizing her
hand. “ Come ’long up to Pa’son. I’ll stay out-
side while you go in an’ ask all ’bout me.”
On the way up hill they passed two old women,
one of whom, blinking at the other, said :
“See ’im ! ’im with Sarah Dooley! ’E was
courtin’ Minnie Jones for four year back, an’
wrote reg’lar when ’e went away. This last time
he made a heap o’ money, an’ came down to tie
the knot, but lo an’ behold, he found Minnie
married to a pleeceman. It was this momin’ ’e
found that out.”
KAPPA.
The Story of a Crime.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
They had been mates for two years—Tom and
Jack And now, down by the edge of the billa-
bong, they faced each other as enemies and
murder was in the heart of bom.
“So!” he growled. “You mean cur. lhis is
the reward of all I have done for you ? TJ , ,
Jack moved uneasily, but said nothing. Had he
spoken then-if it had been only one word-how
different the issue might have been! But he
maintained a sullen silence, a premonition of a
We’ve been mates together,” Tom continued,
speaking deliberately, as if he feared to let his
furious anger hurry his speech ; we ve been
mates together ever since we met in Queensland.
But never again, Jack, never again. I heres
them that excuse a thief, but I ain’t that sort of
man.” . ~ ,
He paused as if expecting some excuse. Perhaps
it was deadly fear that kept the others mouth
closed for he made no reply. He knew how
violent Tom could be when fairly roused, and he
had never seen him in such a furious temper before.
“ I’ve done more for you than I’d have done for
my own brother,” Tom went on. When we first
met you were down on your
stricken and parish-damned. I took you up, and
yet you steal off me. Why, I’d have given you
what you stole if you’d only asked for it. ”
He picked up a large stone. Jack remained
passive. He knew that Tom was so much the
stronger that resistance would be futile, and per-
haps the fear that was upon him rendered him
powerless. There was no hurry about the mur-
derer’s movements. He poised the stone for some
seconds, and eyed his victim.
Then he fastened it to the dog’s neck and threw
him into the water.
“ He won’t steal my liver-and-bacon again, I’ll
swear,” said Tom, as he watched, breaking on the
surface, the last bubbles from Jack’s lungs.
Z.E.F.
There is to be an election for Mayor at a
W.A. township shortly. There are two candi-
dates—Adams, the local milk-purveyor, and
Jones, the meat-merchant. Matters were quiet
until Adams said that Jones’s meat was frequently
alive after it was dead. Jones denied the resur-
rection, and said his meat did not spread typhoid,
anyhow, thus delicately suggesting that Adams’s
milk was not violets, anyway. The result was a
double challenge, and an expert was requisitioned.
He found that Adams’s milk, although particu-
larly powerful and deadly, was nothing as com-
pared with Jones’s meat ; and when this decision
was made public and the Jones and Brown factions
had successfully slaughtered each other, it was
decided to form an independent party and run
MTavish, the meek little storekeeper who
never, never sands the sugar, or sells athletic
butter, and is humble and pious. So M'Tavish
will probably head the poll.
